The RED PAGE
THE RUBAIYAT OF AUSTRALIA
There are, it appears, thirty-nine Omars in
Australia, most of them in Victoria; and every
single one of them has written at least one good
quatrain. One of the Omars was a Bush Dead-
beat, another a Shearer, another a Swagman,
another a Fruitgrower, and three of them were
Omar Cowmen. There was an Old Man Kyabram,
who did some cheerful “ ruminyatting”; an Omar
Cassandra, and a Fitzgerald who produced a
Rubaiyat of Jud.
In selecting the winner it was necessary to keep
sternly in view the published conditions of the
competition. The sort of parody asked.for by
the “Red Page” was a “ smse-rendering,” which
was expressly defined as a parody utilising not
only the original writer’s diction and style, but
following “a train of thought precisely along the
lines that he would have pursued from the given
premises.” And, in addition, the parody had to
be Australian in sentiment. The Rubaiyat that
most nearly fulfilled the conditions laid down was
written by Fred Davison (Melb.); but its defec-
tive rhyming absolutely excluded it as a Rubai-
yat. Had Davison conformed strictly to the
model of the Fitzgerald quatrain he might have
been the Australian Omar. He passes out,
though here is his effort: —
The same slackness of rhyme throws out on the
heap John Sirlow’s Rubaiyat (Melb.)i which told
us this
The Persian muses in his garden, while
“E.W.D.” (Melb.) tells a dramatic story. His
dying sundowner is, however, excellently pictured.
And that up-turned Tin Dish we Heavens call
Seems to shriek out “You’re Euchred ” till one’s
gall
Gets raised—you’d like to Bash its Brazen Face :
Its Blanky Troubles if one lives at all !
The Grow no question makes of Eyes and Nose,
But here or there as strikes its Fancy goes,
And though a Toss-up which ’twill peck at first
He Knows about it all, He Knows, HE KNOWS.
The Moving Figure writhes and then lies still,
Moves nevermore, nor all thy Power or Skill
Can lure it back again to toe the Line
Of Life—once Killed there’s nothing left to Kill.
Thomas Porter (M.L.) gets nearer the real
philosophy; but his Rubaiyat is spoiled by weak
quatrains. Here are his best: —
O, Thou who didst with Centipede and Snake
And Bull-ant stock the bush and therewith make
Weak Mortal Man to Swear, whereby his Soul
Is blackened, Man’s forgiveness give and take.
The pulling Bullocks pull, and having pulled,
Pull on; not all thy Epigrams well-culled
Can lure them into walking a Straight Line,
Nor into decent Speed will they be gulled.
We, too, are Errant Bullocks, and would keep
To no hard Roadway, but would always reap
The Summer Herbage in some Gully cool—
But that the Whiplash from some Hand might
leap.
The most humorous of the batch is The Rubaiyat
of Jud, by “Dico” (Vic.). Unfortunately, it is
the Rubaiyat of Melbourne, not of Australia ; and
Jud is too insignificant a thing to hang a Rubaiyat
upon. He must manage with a ti-tree halo.
“ Dico’s” humor justifies quotation in full.
“•T.S.” begins magnificently with a brilliant
simile ; but he staggers away and dies miserably
in the bush.
Three Sins, all other Moral Codes are lies :
Don’t Scab, Turn down Your Mate, or Drink
with Flies.
Inconspicuously excellent are Mick O’Maher s
and “ Blighter’s ” efforts. “Ande” gives us the
Rubaiyat of the Bush Pub.
It might have been predicted that the Austra-
Han Tentmaker would turn out to be a swagman ;
but Leontine Scott (Vic.) is the only competitor
who recognises the possibilities Of the subject.
Out of his twelve quatrains these are worth re-
printing : —
“ coming from Tweed River, is
naturally more technical.
The poet prophesied by Furnley Maurice
(R.P. 7/2/’07), who will publicly return thanks
for a nice warm cup of tea or a load of fire-
wood, was anticipated (writes “N.L.”) by one
who used to carry his verses from door to door,
like Homer, in Melbourne suburbs up to a few
years ago. As a rule, the “ poetry ” was in
manuscript, but occasionally profits justified his
getting it printed in sheet form, and adding a list
of his benefactors—Mrs. Smith, who presented a
hat and a pair of boots ; Mrs. Jones, a dozen eggs
and some kind advice; Mr. Wilkinson, much
sympathy and a leg of mutton, and so on. These
presents were made in addition to payment for the
verses on a sliding scale. Thanks sometimes took
the form of a poem to “ Mr. Robinson, who helped
me when I was in distress.” In another MS. copy,
of course, Brown’s name could be substituted for
Robinson’s, and each dedication was good enough
for a little more financial help from those worthies.
Sometimes the poet had a piece of verse in the
“ Missing Friends” column of the dailies, inform-
ing patrons that he was ill, and that Providence
would surely reward anyone who came and helped
him at this juncture. This sort of thing must
have paid, as the advertisements appeared quite
frequently. The poet is dead, but his mantle
does not appear to have fallen on anyone. His
career seems to indicate that if picturesque young
poets travelled on similar lines poetry could be
made to pay nearly as well as dairy-farming. A;.
According to The Athenceum (Bond.), “ Austra-
lia has as yet not done justice to herself in
poetry.” In its review of the recent Australian
Anthology, the kindly old weekly finds that our
feeling for wild life and wild nature comes, ‘‘not
with an inspiration of its own, but second hand-
through Thomas Moore and Mrs. Hemans in the
earlier pages, through Mr. Swinburne in many of
the best of the later pages.” . . . ‘‘How is it that
these open-air people have no muscles in their
verse ? They are constantly reminding us of the
more feminine side of Tennyson, and, if they echo
Browning, turn most naturally to ‘Evelyn Hope
for a model.” The feminine side of Tennyson,
and Mrs. Hemans 1
“Flagwagger” insists on writing an Australian
national song; and taking the advice humbly
tendered him L (R.P., 15/11/’0C) desperately tries
again.
“This anthem,” he observes, “ought to meet
the case. It doesn’t advance, or awake, or arise,
but attends strictly to business right through. It
only needs to be set to music—none of your
imported music, with trills and frills and classical
absurdities, but just a plain brown tune.”
“Agnostic” also impeni ten tly forwards a national
song, chiefly Hurrah! and Advance 1 Some day,
when we n re not feeling so tired, we might
inaugurate a competition for a national song;
but, in the meantime, patriotic poets might be
thinking up some good rhymes for Australia.
There is no hurry. It will probably be a pretty
long meantime.
THE RUBAIYAT OF AUSTRALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wake, for the Austral Land is in its Prime,
Its Harvests ripen in the glad Sprint}-Time,
The red Blood courses swiftly through the Veins,
And every Poet turns his Thoughts to Rhyme.
Dreaming beneath the radiant Summer Sun,
I heard a Voice that cried — “ Ho, everyone.
Come, sound your Country's Praise on every Hand,
Before the short-lived Span of Life be done! ”
Last Year, indeed, is gone with all its Foes,
And all the Years before , where No-one knows,
But still the Mother Earth her Treasure yields,
And still the Balance Sheet an Increase shows.
Come, sow your Seed and reap the golden Wheat,
The rich Response of Last Year now repeat;
The Drought is past, and past the One-Time Gloom,
The Present and the Future both seem sweet.
And look —a thousand Ounces of pure Gold,
From out Earth's Bosom plucked by Miners bold:
What need to roam afar to seek for Wealth
When here one can such Richness find and hold f
How Sweet to Travel far Abroad, think Some;
Others think ever of the Time to come
When then shall save enough to leave our Shores *
But ask Why They Return—and They are Dumb.
Lo, some we loved, the Greatest and the Best,
Have swelled Ihe “ Stinking Fish ” Cry with much
Zest,
But since Prosperity has smiled Once More,
They come back to Australia with the Rest.
Myself, ichen young, did eagerly frequent
Parliament House and heard great Argument
On Income Tax and Land Tax and what not—
Yet never came out wiser than I went.
But leave them all to wrangle and with me
Behold the good Works of the Deity,
The fair green Fields and swelling Hills around,
The Golden Fragrance of the Wattle Tree.
Alas, that Spring should ever leave our Land,
That Men so soon grow old —a Hoary Band —
But still the Land remains to cheer anew
The Hearts of all the Young, who Understand.
Melb.
REMINOTON.
Write ! for the Land its Future does not Know—
The Whitening Path doth Neighb’ring Blackness
show
(Perhaps on Either Side a Pit is hid)
But to its Destined End it Needs Must Go.
The Winds from out The Vastness howl its Fate
In Language never learned until Too Late ;
But if The Message was revealed, The Land
Gould not a Finger of its Doom escape.
The Stars swing on, nor Turn Aside nor Stay,
Though Fowl-like on the earth Man Struts his
Day
And Crows his Wishes to the Cause of Things—
And as The Great so is The Small alway :
For as the Stars Change Not their Course nor
Pause, •
True to Unending Unbeginning Laws,
So just as Surely do the Lesser Things
Unheeding pass to Last Effect from Cause.
For Land and Path and Pit and End wbre born
In Linked fate at far Creation’s Dawn,
When Yesterday, To-Morrow and To-Day
Were Planned and Known from Even until Morn.
And yet the Crowing doubtless helps the Bird ;
The Noise he makes —it by Himself is Heard ;
And, being heard, works out its Certain End—
Although The Universe Hears Not a word.
This Morning’s Orb is but Last Evening’s Sun ;
’Tis Nothing New —can Nothing Do not Done :
So is Australia in the Plan of Things—
The Race she Runs has been Already Run.
The Drover drives In Vain the dusty Sheep,
Tire Miner strives for Naught his Gold to keep—
And all the laird, from Rouseabout to Boss,
The Weary Circle treads with Wand’ring Feet.
’Tis only the Eternal Fool who Tries —
The Waves of Time bring all things to the Wise
Who take their Ease along the shores of Life :
So to the Loafer often falls the Prize.
This Southern Land whose Day has .Tust Begun
Will Work and Sweat till sets its little sun,
Then Pass Away, a Used-up Link between
The Sleeping Peoples and the Time to Come.
They say the Mongol and the Monkey keep
Grim watch upon our land with intent deep,
While Cole, the Book Arcadian —an old ass —
Rubs trait’rous hands—Jmt we are fast asleep !
The world no feature makes of our Yes-No;
Our worst foes are ourselves—the Mikado
’Neath Fuji San —lie watches all our ways.
He knows about it all—he knows —HE K.NOWS !
“White Fang’’ and “W.II.D.” both fail by
penning exhortation, not philosophy. Their
warnings are needed, but not in a Rubaiyat.
“ White Fang ” (Melb.), instead of sitting by ’the
Ah, train the Men —what boots it to repeat
How Time is slipping underneath our Feet,
Anglo-Alliance and the Powerful,
What use are they against a Yellow Fleet?'
How long, how long in definite Pursuit
Of Cricket, Football, Anti-Sosh Dispute?
Better to purchase Guns and Rifle-Balls,
And teach our young Australians how to shoot.
The Grape-shot can with Logic absolute
The wily Yellow Japanese confute —
The subtle Alchemist that in a Trice
Can make an end and settle the dispute.
And that old Mother-Land to which we pray,
To which we our Allegiance owe—and pay,
Lift not thy Hands to It for help, for It
Is impotent thousands of Miles away.
“ W.H.D.” (Perth) disguises his exhortation in
irony, and Omar was too philosophical to be
always ironical.
Wake ! for the Morning Paper let us Call,
And on the Sporting Column let us Fall —
It matters not what Rumors shake the World
If we have drawn a Prize from Tattersall.
Come, leave the Gate, nor list to Them who Say
The Monkey may upon our Garden Prey :
The Lion’s Skin will surely him Affright,
E’en though the Lion’s Voice be but a Bray.
What though we Reap the Harvest that we Sow,
And cannot point a Gun against the Foe :
Yet ask us Records, Pedigrees, and Times,
We Know about Them all—WE Know—WE
KNOW!
O Thou, who dost with bold and bristling Chin,
Call every blessed Thing we do a Sin,
Thou wilt not with vague Allegations round
Enmesh me, and impute my Nose to Gin.
Why, all the Saints and Sages, who discuss’d
The Seven Sins so whirlingly, are thrust
Like dead-beat Boozers forth; they may be
crowned
With Ti-tree, but their Ears are in the Dust.
Think, in this gloomy Caravanserai,
Whose Doorways are Port Melbourne and Foots-
cray,
How Torrey, Booth, and Dowie for a while
Filled gaping Purses, and then went away.
Old Booth, indeed, is gone with all his Nose,
And sank his hard-cadged Wealth where no one
knows,
But still the Gull sings Praises to the Wren,
And still the Liquor from the Beer-pump flows.
So leave the Juds to bellow drearily,
And take Old Ladies home to Sunday Tea,
With some bright Tart on breezy Mount or Sea
Make Sport of those who promised H—l to Thee.
While the Girls bring us rosy Hours of Bliss,
Don’t waste the Time (it may be Sin to kiss);
But when the Judder, with his gloomy Phiz,
Draws up to Thee, —take aim, and do not miss.
Awake ! for on your Gum lree tall and white,
The Kookaburra laughs to scorn the Night:
And Sol, Heav’n’s Swagman, lighting his first
pipe,
With careless Match has set the Bush alight.
Comes the New Year, Old Vices and Desires
Are thrown on Resolution’s new-lit Fires ;
But sweetest Grass on Burnt Earth grows. And
Lo !
Our Sudden Virtue ere ’tis Easter tires.
Oh, Hark to me, nor heed the foolish Wise-
Three things there are which all White Men de-
spise—
I sauntered to a Shanty not too gay
Where Drinks are always served both Night and
Day ; '
Here Trav’ler after Trav’ler, with his Drurn,_
Stopped for his destined Drunk and went his Way.
Old Bung no notice takes of These or Those,
But here and there as sign the Drinkers goes,
And anything that’s mentioned in the Bar
He knows about it all —He knows —Hi? knows.
The Winter far has gone w.tli all its Snows.
And all the Creeks and Danis where no one knows ;
But still the Cocky feeds his little Flock
And still about his Fortune bravely blows.
Some for the Profits of the Tote, and some
Sigh for the Prize in TattersalPs to come.
Ah, spend the Cash and let the Gee Gee go ;
You get good measure for a modest Thrum !
The Mining Float's men set their Hearts upon
Make brave beginnings ever ; and anon
The Reef they hunted underneath Earth’s Breast
For half-a-mile is Barren or is Gone.
Ah, lately by the road to Yargoneer,
While trudging thro’ the Dust, a tramp drew
near ;
He dipt a Pannikin within a Can
And hade me taste of it; and It was Beer.
I sometimes think that never flows so red
The Nose as where Beer-Arguments are sped ;
That oft each Tavern sees some village Squires
Upon his Foeman put a Lovely Head.
Yes, follow my advice, and leave the Lot
•Of Band of Hope or Temperance folk forgot:
The Reverend Henry Worrall at the Bar,
Or Judkins crowned with Tea leaves—heed them
not.
And when Myself with halting foot shall pass,
Bearing my Swag along the Track, alas !
Should I on thirsty Errand reach the spot
Where you make one—fill up a Long Beer glass !
The Shearer’s Rubaiyat is supplied by
“M.E.F.”; while three competitors have shown
us Omar, the Cow-Man, “ Yttik ” (Meib.), who
is doubtless Kitty seen from the udder end of the
cow, produces a clever piece of work, from which
we omit the weaker quatrains :
Wake and prepare to meet the Brindled Cow ;
’Tis time to bring Her to the Milk Bails now,
And though your heavy Eyes are dull with Sleep,
You’ll simply have to open Them somehow.
How Sweet to drink is fresh warm Milk, think
some ;
Others think fondly of the Cheese to come.
Ah ! if they had to struggle with the Brutes,
They’d leave off wanting Milk and take to Rum.
The Cow no Question makes of Ayes or Noes,
But near or far as strikes her Fancy goes,
But He who has to seek the Beggar out . .
He knows about it all—He knows —HE KIN OY S
The moving Fingers squeeze, and having squeezed
Full every Drop that can be outward teazed,
The wily Cow then plants her Foot well in 1
And if She overturns the Lot seems Pleased.
Oh, Thou who didst with Dairy and with Cow
Surround me till I hate the name, I vow,
Thou, wilt not with Misfortune hedge me in
Nor e’en a mod’rate share of Cash allow.
And when Thyself with fjhiningFoot shall stray
Among the Souls of Men who’ye passed away,
And if Thy joyous Search should be for me
Thou’lt find me surely in the Milky Way !
The moving Fingers deftly seize the Teat
And charm the Lovely Fluid out of it.
Ah ! some will Tug and some will Swear, but all
Their Blasphemy won’t win a Little Bit!
0 ! could we dairymen with Fate conspire
To change the sorry Scheme of Things entire,
We would breed only Heifers from the Cow,
And grow Paspalum on the (you know) Mire.
The worldly Hope I’ve set my heart upon
Is Grass Enough to feed the cattle on,
And Fame I dream of, if I dream at all,
Is lo ! a Cow with good Escutcheon !
Bail up ! my Beauty, Fill the Pail that clears
Us all of Poverty in future years.
To-morrow ! why to-morrow in my herd
Perhaps there’ll be but Redwater and Steers !
What matters, here, a Watson or a Reid ?
And Johnson’s win o’er Squires can’t hurt my
breed ;
Full-bellied with Corn-beef I fall asleep,
My trust in God and Good Paspalum Seed.
The Fruit-Grower’s Rubaiyat is supplied by
“N.L.” (Vic.). Unfortunately, one weak qua-
train, omitted here, spoils its excellent chances.
Its originality gives it high place, and the philo-
sophy of Omar of Rodney is delightfully Fitz-
geraldian.
Wake ! for the Sun’s bright Chisel has releas d
The Planks with which Night hid the tinted
Feast,
And in fair rows of Sold entinged withKed
Dawn’s ripened Apricots enrich the East.
Before the glitt’ring Blade had broken through
A Voice beseeehed —“Come, rouse out and turn to!
We’ve many Cases to send cityward,
And Time and Trains wait not for me or you.”
I[ sometimes wonder if we all and each
rlave better Destiny than any Peach ; . %
backed to be jostled in this worldly Case :
L’he hardest triumph—yet Destruction reach!
With me adown some long Tomato row, 3
Where wilted Green ones with the Ripen’d grow ;
for some, like Souls, are blocked from Fortune’s
warmth, * . ‘
Mid ne’er, or but in part, with Scarlet glow.
)n great J.P.-ship some Men stake their Tact;
Jthors for Heav’nly Wings themselves have
backed ;
)h, Gamblers all, why make this very Globe
Place within the Meaning of the Act?
Flicy say the Rabbit scuttles o’er the Loam
Where Cherryman once reared a Lovely Home;
Mul Dairyfat, who milked a Record Herd—
L’he Kine above his lowly Roof now roam.
welcome Weekly down beside the Crick
If so it be that Snakes are not too thick),
W trusty Pipe, and Kitty’s ready Smile—
Mi, Joy don’t all consist in Getting Shick !
Ln Shick’s Detraction, though, too much is said
By froward Souls, and others by them led ;
[ often think Beer’s Enemies are nark’d
Because one Shandy dooms them to a Head.
Oh, Gloomy Ones, can you postpone the Day
When human Aphides must meet Death’s Spray ?
What matter, then, what matter is’t we seek
Fermented Juice to solace our short Stay ?
And so, by this process of elimination, we are
left at last with the winning Rubaiyat. It is not
such a good Rubaiyat as Fred Davison’s, nor is it so
humorous as the Rubaiyat of Jud, nor so original
as “ N.L.’s ”; but it is sincerely Australian in
tone, offends not the conditions of the competition,
and by its rather precise merit slips past more
brilliant but more errant competitors. It is an
excellent sense-rendering of Omar’s philosophy
planted in Australian soil. But it is not a work
of genius. The winner is Miss Brook, 438 Chancery
Lane, Melb. Other competitors of whose verses
more than five quatrains are here printed will be
paid for them at the usual Bulletin rates. |||
Burst up the big estates, my boys,
Build up the tariff wall,
And make the birth rate boom along,
And borrow not at all.
Lay down the mighty battle-ships
As fast as you are able,
And let them bear, from stem to stern,
A truthful union label.
Bay off the debts and forge the guns
To guard our country sunny,
And see that all these things are done
With wdiite Australian money.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Parable.
The artist threw on his canvas
The thought of his inmost soul,
The tints of the higher vision.
The glow of the altar coal;
The light of the star-shot ether,
Beyond the sun’s far sight,
And the flash of angel robes that trailed
In the blue of Bethlehem’s night.
They came with their ready praises,
His name in their books they wrote,
They told the cut of his trousers,
They told the hue of his coat;
They promised him gold and silver,
They poured him their costly wine,
They chattered, and chattered, and gabbled,
And bore him away to dine.
And the picture was never finished,
For his soul grew coarse and old ;
The lines on the palette faded,
The glory grew dull and cold ;
The ether grew thick and sombre,
And he blotted his angels out—
For the soul of the artist perished,
And the man grew rich and stout.
MAORI LAND. DAVID McKeE WRIGHT.
Lost Waters.
Painted and garnished, shining in the daylight,
All along the high road the homesteads lie.
Young maids, white maids, find the pleasant way
light;
Heavy is the going to such a one as I.
Not for me the waters singing down the valley,
Glinting in the sunshine, dreaming in the shade !
There, where the footfalls of happy lovers dally.
My feet pass onward and will not be delayed.
On past the ploughshare, tilting in the furrow,
Past the fringing green crops along the woodland’s
edge,
Up by the treed, silent slopes, where to-morrow
Will ring out the music of axe and of wedge
I know a track ; I’ve trod it late and early
Looking for the cows there, and never come to
harm.
Oh me, the little head, golden-haired and curly,
Lying close, close in the hollow of my arm !
How could I leave him, my baby, to another
To cry in the night time and ne’er a one to hear,
To break his little heart there, a-sobbing for his
mother,
The only one that wants him, my sweet and my
dear ?
I know a hid creek, whose waters hardly flowing,
Brim to a still pool grey as stormy skies;
Knee-deep about it the twisted trees are growing,
Stark out across it a dead gum lies.
Up to the fork I must clamber, single-handed,
Creep along the limb with my baby at my breast;
Me and my baby, spat upon and branded,
WeTl shut our eyes—and fall—and be at rest.
Soon ’twill be Over, we’ll not be long a-sinking,
My baby, my sweetheart —but, great God above !
Keep me from thinking. Dear Christ! keep me
from thinking
Of him—the man that shamed me, the man that
I love !
QUEENSLAND.
A.W.
A Bush Girl’s Song.
VIC.
My love is late a-coming
If there’s a love for me,
Old Time the years is summing,
My ’teen will soon be ’ty.
The golden hours are humming,
There’s honey in the hive ;
But love is late a-coming,
The luscious comb to rive !
The apricot and apple
Have doffed their bridal blooms ;
The sun their fruits will dapple,
And mellow their perfumes.
A warm wind is a-humming,
The summer soon will be ;
But love is late a-coming
If there’s a love for me !
A.J.B.
Prudence.
Prudence, upon the margin see
Her gentle name is writ,
In faded letters, shakily,
Prudence. John Jones fecit.
Draw near and study the sweet face
Of Prudence in her youth,
Her slender form instinct with grace,
Her eyes alive with truth.
Her parted curls of chestnut hair,
Her rosy gown cut low
To show her arms and bosom bare.
As white as drifted snow.
Prudence ! The clay clods cover thee
Long dead. But what of it ?
Your portrait lives eternally.
Prudence (John Jones fecit!)
VIC.
DOROTHY FRANCES McCRAE.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1907.
Price 6d.
THE GATHERING OF THE CRIMINALS.
A deputation of fruitgrowers recently waited on Customs Minister Lyne to complain that, under his Commerce Act, restrictions are placed on
the trade in diseased fruit. The Bulletin hears on bad authority that next time the fruitgrowers call to complain because they are not allowed
to poison the community they will be supported by other influential offenders—especially representatives of the Burglars’ Federation, the United
Embezzlers, and the Baby-Farmers’ Union. William is again expected to be unsympathetic.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

By sending direct to Tins Bulletin O/lice Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling. you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week, for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director,
ltow to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editbr,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
oflicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special ’Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be, refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other A rticles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
hvo columns in length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address As specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is fonvarded ivithout stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further., those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in lllach and White.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 21, 1907.
THE SALVATION OF THE LOST
PROVINCE.
It was announced last week that Prime Minister
Deakin had shed some of his needless and dread-
ful affability and Done Things. A year or two
of correspondence between him and the State
Government of S.A. brought the cession of
the Northern Terrritory to the Commonwealth
no nearer, and when The Bulletin went to press
last week the position was exactly what it had
been any time since 1901. The Chow was still
swinging on Australia’s back door. The greedy
eye of militant Asia was still looking through
Australia’s back keyhole. The Territory was just
as hopeless as it had been for 120 years—killed
dead by want of communication, with no prospect
of a railway because S.A. can’t put up the money,
with no prospect of settlement for want of
access to markets,' with the Chow pecking
feebly and miserably at its tin mines, the
British gold mining companies giving things up
as a bad job by reason of the incapacity of the
British Johnny management, and the foreign
land syndicate monopolising huge areas on which
to run a few wild and staring cattle. Now, it is
announced that by personal conference, Alfred
Deakin and Thomas Price have, in two days,
practically fixed up all arrangements for the
transfer of the Territory to the Commonwealth,
and if the two Parliaments agree, and the Terri-
tory receives even a fair semblance of good
management afterwards, the day of resurrection
is fairly in sight. As regards the first “if,” it is
impossible to imagine that the Parliaments will
not agree. It may be taken for granted that little
Afternoon-tea Kelly - of the Reps, will cackle
and obstruct; Kelly always obstructs, and he
would pour forth his irritating cackle if an
enemy were already at the gate—unless the enemy
had set fire- to his motor-car. It may be fairly
assumed, too, that Bruce Smith will ask for an
assurance that the Territory will be filled with
niggers and Chinamen. Then there will be a
complaint from the new Queensland senators,
Sayers and Chat away ,and Sledger ; they gained
their seats partly by tickling Queensland’s vanity
with a wild suggestion that the transcontinental
railway should be abandoned, and Port Darwin
be connected with Brisbane by a huge coastal
line, and Sayers and Chataway and Sledger
must needs make some show of protest when their
scheme falls through. The Tasmanian contingent
will be indifferent, and the Westralian push will
want some definite promise that its own pet line
from Port Augusta to Kalgoorlie will not be
overlooked —an assurance which it should cer-
tainly get, even to the extent of-the passing
of an Act pledging the Commonwealth to
build the line within a specified period. But,
with all drawbacks, it is hard to imagine that the
Australian Parliament will hesitate to take over
the Territory on almost any conditions.
* * * *
The terms on which South Australia surrenders
its proud white elephant will, presumably, include
the taking over of the Territory’s debt and the
immediate construction of the transcontinental
line. The line must be built in any case, other-
wise the Territory will be the same old, empty,
useless.deficit that it has been so long. As for
the debt, it is time the Commonwealth made a
beginning, somehow and somewhere, at the busi-
ness of taking over the State liabilities, and this
is as good a way to begin as any other. Moreover,
it would be grossly unfair if the debt were not
taken over, and the Territory is cheap at the
price. It would be cheap at an immensely greater
price.
* * * *
If the long-postponed “deal” comes off this
time it should open a new era in Australian
politics. The Commonwealth will have a chance
to demonstrate, in a huge arena, what it can do
in the way of administration—such a chance
as a great statesman and organiser yearns
after, yet very seldom finds. The Commonwealth
will have an almost virgin field, in which it will
be supreme. There will be no Carruthers in
the Territory weeping over the imaginary wrongs
of Ma State; no Newton Moore braying that
his State is robbed and “ruined”; no hostile
press —for a while at least; and very, little
Stinking Fish, barring Chinese Fish, and that
hardly counts. With anything like good manage-
ment, the only serious obstruction to be looked
for between latitude 26 and the Arafura Sea
should be an anxious and disturbed foreign race
saying “Whaffor?” But it is essential that
the problem should be attacked with courage
and enthusiasm, with a resolve to find joy
and pleasure in facing huge difficulties, with a
determination to Do Things and to delight
in doing them. It will be necessary to pour
money into the Territory in order to make it a
profitable asset that will repay the money many
times over. And in the process a deaf ear will
often have to be turned to the member for
Smith’s Curse, and his demand to know wfiat is
the use of saving one-sixth of a continent from
uselessness and possible Asiatic annexation when
the Government, in its haste to build the first
railway across Australia, has forgotten to put a
fresh coat of paint on the letter box just outside
Smith’s Curse where the road branches off to
Jones’s Ruins.
* * » #
A good resolve to begin with is that there
shall be no administration of the Territory on im-
ported Johnny principles. The Territory requires
no British “capting” at the head of its local
affairs; this country has had all the British
“capting” it can stand in Papua. Next there
will be the debt problem to consider. So far the
Commonwealth’s I O U has never been seen in
the world’s money market, and in order that
the Federation may live up to its principles it is
most desirable that the Territory debt shall be
converted, whenever the chance is available, into
uniform Federal Consols, with a sinking fund
attached. After being, for six years, the only
institution in Australia that is out of pawn, it
would be a lamentable and disgraceful thing if
the Commonwealth were content to regard its
new debt as a permanent affair, to be carefully
stacked up in a corner as if it were a treasure and
handed down to posterity as if it were an heir-
loom. And, as a still larger financial problem,
there is the building of 1100 miles of railway at a
cost of perhaps £6,000,000 or more, for it is essen-
tial that it should not be a meregimcrack line. It
will be, for a time at least, the only road On which
Australia can rely if it has to send the means of
defence to the Territory in a day of invasion,
and a railway on which a country’s very
existence may depend requires to be something
better than a narrow-gauge concern of cheap
light rails lying almost unballasted on the floor of
the wilderness. It should be a broad gauge affair,
suited for swift travelling, and that would involve
some future arrangement for the Federal pur-
chase of the part of the transcontinental which
lies within S.A. territory, and its conversion from
the present rattletrap to a railway suited for a fast
express service. It will certainly be difficult to
build a work of this size out of revenue and yet do
it with the speed that the case requires, yet it is
by no means impossible. At the very least the
Commonwealth should find half the cost of the
work out of Federal direct taxation, and make
sure that the debt which represents the other half
is speedily wiped off again by the operation of a
sinking fund. Either way, the finding of the
money will necessarily arouse that quaint circus,
the State Frights person, with his impossible tale
about some unfindable agreement, said to have
been made by parties who are still at large,
whereby “ the power of direct taxation is reserved
to the States.” The pleasure of arousing that
State Frights person to helpless fury will be
almost as good as an asset in itself.
* * * *
Then, in addition to all other responsibilities,
the Commonwealth, when it takes over the Terri-
tory, will require to find some way of discouraging
the great and objectionable Northern Chow. It
will require to actively police the coast to prevent
the surreptitious introduction of more Chow, as
the country becomes more attractive to that
evasive yellow personage. It must needs take a
more active interest in defence, for the effective
occupation of the Territory will be a shock to
many Asiatic hopes and aspirations, and of all
Australian ports, Darwin, as the nearest to the
scene of danger, will be the most liable to attack.
Above all, the Commonwealth will have to pro-
vide itself with a land settlement policy and an
immigration policy—two matters which it has
hitherto got along without. To make the Terri-
tory pay, to make the great, expensive railway
(and the future branch railways connecting it
with Queensland, N.S. Wales and Westralia)
pay, and to garrison the new possession,
the Commonwealth will want some hun-
dreds of thousands of strong white settlers,
and to attract these it will probably be necessary
to give away small farms gratis to pioneers who
are willing and qualified to go out and argue the
point with the wilderness. It will certainly be
necessary to make rigid laws to prevent the
aggregation of great estates, and there will be work
to do at gently elbowing out or buying out the
great estate-holders who are already in possession.
There will be need for a medical school for the
study of tropical diseases ; the Territory is prob-
ably quite as healthy as Victoria or Tasmania,
but if its inhabitants have to depend on medicos
who know nothing of the ways of the local germ, it
may easily get a bad reputation. There will be an
education problem and a local government problem
—in fact, there will be so much to do when the
523,000 square miles of the Lost Province become
Federal property that for a year or two there
should hardly be time to listen to Carruthers’
plaint that the Federal cow is eating his imaginary
State grass.
* -* * *
The Bulletin enumerates these many and
weighty responsibilities solely by way of en-
couragement. If the Australian Parliament is
worth its salt it will be glad right down to its
feet that, there being so much to be done for
Australia, the chance of doing it should have •
come in the way of this generation. If there are
men in it to whom the doing of great things and
the solving of great problems is a joy and a satis-
faction, then the Territory problem will be a
fascination—one that they wouldn’t miss for an
Agent-Generalship. It should especially irppeal
to those who are not wholly parochial—who
have some glimpse of the Australian spirit,
and nope tor something better in the
way of Federation than a discordant central
Parliament eaten up by State jealousies and
surrounded by a catfight of grudging and peevish
local Premiers. The Federal authority can only
become the influence it should be in Australia
by being a strong authority, and it can only prove
its strength by Doing Things. The Territory is'
an arena in which it can Do Things. It will have
room there to put many democratic theories into
practice, and to refute a horde of carping Tory
critics by accomplishing what they say is impos-
sible, and by bringing prosperity out of devices
which they allege to be crude and ruinous. The
annexation of the Territory, in short, will be the
greatest event that has happened to Australia
since the establishment of the Commonwealth.
Arid Prime Minister Deakin, as the man who is
largely responsible for both events, has vindicated
his right to be regarded as an Australian states-
man—despite the superfluous affability which so
often retards his movements, and makes him waste
time bowing and scraping to a political enemy
when he should be taking the enemy’s scalp.
PREMIER KIDSTON’S POLICY.
Queensland holds its general election this year,
and apparently four parties will go to the poll.
This is rather a variation from the usual course of
State politics, which have, since the fiscal issue
ceased from troubling, been drifting back towards
the two-party order of things. In Victoria,
Bent has practically swallowed Mackinnon and
Peacock ; and in N.S. W. Carruthers is greasing
his lower jaw to see how it will work in the pro-
cess of swallowing Twaddell and Owe’Sullivan.
In Queensland, however, factions have multiplied
rather than diminished, arid chaos tends to be
more chaotic. The old Philp crowd is there, of
course, with its unspeakable record of blundering,
jobbery, financial incapacity, huge loans and huge
deficits ; and behind it is the Tory and land-
jobbing press, the boodlers and the wild-cat
men, and the big landlords, and everything that
Queensland could best dispense with if an in-
scrutable Providence saw fit to take it from this
earthly scene. Attached to this gang is Dig by
Denham, butter-man and recent renegade, and it
will be accompanied to the poll by any section of
the old Morgan or Progressive party which he
may be able to draw with him, though so far he
has drawn nothing but himself. Next there is
the large and intelligent section of the Labor
party which follows Premier KiDSTON, and allied
with it the old Morgan or progressive party,
barring Denham, butter-man and renegade,
already mentioned. Finally, there is a very
small but incredibly bitter and impracticable sec-
tion of the Labor party, which demands all or
nothing, and wants it instantly; and which, in
its fury because Kidston doesn’t go the imme-
diate hog, the whole instantaneous hog and nothing
but the hog, seems half-inclined to lean towards
Philp the Tory, and make alliance with him.
This section is represented by the Worker,
which professes to be intensely democra-
tic, and also professes to see no reason
why Australia should not throw open its
ports to the goods of the low-wage Asiatic
non-unionist. AH these odds and ends make a
curious political medley. The election may sort
things out to some extent, but the probabilities
are that it won’t make any very great, change.
In such a position there are two courses that a
Premier may take. He may announce his policy,
and stand or fall by it, and if it is a good policy
and the Premier is a strong Premier he generally
comes out on top. Even if he fails he at least
fails with honor and dignity, and, as a rule, he
only retires to come back to office at a later date
and on a stronger footing. The other course is to
strengthen himself with alliances, to make com-
promises, to trim his sails to imaginary or antici-
pated breezes, to swallow part of his principles in
order to save the rest, to hold conference with
Philp and Cribb and Leahy, to butter Denham,
to find out the price of the Courier’s support,
to swarm secretly up the backstairs* of the
Daily Mail to ascertain its figure in concessions,
and to negotiate for the support of howling sects
and secret societies of the Angora Goat. That is
the way of the Old Parliamentary Hand. It is
the way of Drake who is politically dead, and
McCay who is politically defunct, and
Minister McLean who is gone, and Anderson
Dawson whose light has blown out, and George
Reid whose light is burning very low ; but it is to
be hoped it will not be the way of Kidston. It
often leads to office in the immediate present,
and it always leads to failure and contempt in the
future. In the end it generally pays very poor
dividends compared with the policy of the man
who goes straight ahead and takes results as they
come.
• * « *
Premier Kidston’s programme and his record
both justify him in refusing the greasy hand
of friendship held out by his late colleague
Denham, and the oily overtures of Philp, and in
ploughing his own furrow. The Governments of
which he lias been either the leader or a promi-
nent member have established the principle of
one adult one vote; they have passed the Workers’
Compensation Act, and the Shearers’ and Sugar
Workers’ Accommodation Act; they have ex-
panded the Agricultural Bank which supplies
cheap money to settlers, restored the deductions
which Philp made in the salaries of public ser-
vants, abolished Philp’s infamous poll-tax, and
exempted people with small incomes from the
operation of the income-tax. Also, they have
enacted a law providing for the compulsory re-
sumption of great estates, created a Labor Bureau,
and built a good many miles of new railway. And
where the unspeakably incapable Philp heaped
up £1,150,000 of deficits in three years and added
enormous amounts to the public debt, the Minis-
tries with which Kidston was connected have pro-
duced, outof asmallerrevenue, anaggregatesurplus
of £129,000 and haveslightly reduced theawful heap
of Queensland’s public liabilities. That is the past
record on which Premier Kidston asks for a.
renewal of confidence. For the future his pro-
gramme includes Old Age Pensions, a system of
Wages Boards on the Victorian model, a Public
Service Superannuation Fund, and a scheme to
settle disputes between the two Houses by re-
ferendum ; he has an immigration policy and an
irrigation policy ; a proposal for State accident
insurance; and a number of v other vigorous
democratic ideas. There are spots pn Kidston,
no doubt. His unaccountable refusal to com-
mit himself to a land-tax is, one \spot, and
his folly in letting the central sugar-mills, which
State management had dragged out of bank-
ruptcy, go back to the hands o.f the old imbecile
management, is a whole blot. But a great deal
may be forgiven to the leader of a party which has
given Queensland three years of solvency and
honest finance. To anyone who comprehends the
vastness of the borrowing curse and the horrors of
the loan slavery these three years of honesty
would cover a whole stack of sins—far more than
there are to cover in Kidston’s case.
* • * *
The Government party is the middle party in
Queensland politics, just as T Waddell’s is in
N.S.W. and Mackinnon’s in Victoria, but it
doesn’t necessarily follow that its fate will be
theirs. There is a difference in middle parties.
When Moltke pushed his wedge into the Aus-
trian line at Sadowa he won through being the
person in the middle. When he closed in on two
sides of the French at Sedan they lost through
being in the middle. It is all a question of the
kind of person who is in the middle and the sort
of individuals who are on the two sides. One
thing is certain —when all the possible leaders
now in sight are considered, by far the best hope
for Queensland lies in Kidston’s success. On
one hand is Philp, who went down in
1903 because many of his best friends left
him in terror, regarding him as a crazed
person whose mad extravagance and childish
incapacity must end in bringing Queensland
to public insolvency. Philp was “ the limit ” for
incapacity ; his whole life was a muddle; his
knowledge of public affairs was that of the lower
animals, and his public finance was the finance of
the beasts that perish. His only claim for
restoration to power is the effluxion of time ; he
is four years older than when he muddled himself
out of office, and presumably four years more
incapable; also, the public has had four years
in which to forget his ineptitude. The
other alternative to Kidston seems to be Joe
Lesina and the two or three wild-eyed incom-
prehensibles who circle round the Worker, and
have the immediate and wholesale reconstruction
of society for their object. About the goodness
of their intentions there seems little doubt, hut
their programme is a shriek and a cloud of vapor.
In the circumstances, Kidston’s policy is plainly
to plough his own furrow ; to cultivate a vast
contempt for Philp ; to disregard the treacherous
paw of amity held out by Denham ; and, if his
allies of the old Morgan party should desert him,
to make no attempt to retain them by compro-
mise, but just to let them go. Kidston’s policy,
despite some omissions, represents quite as much
progress as any Ministry is likely to accomplish
in the life of one Parliament. If Queensland
doesn’t recognise that now, a brief trial of Philp
or Lesina will probably convince it that the per-
fect leader is rare, and that it is easy to travel
further than Kidston and fare a great deal worse.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Unsatisfactory Immigrant.
New South Wales has only made a beginning—
and a small baby-size in beginnings at that—with
its new assisted immigration policy, and already
there are unpleasant rumors. The most unplea-
sant is that many of the immigrants are not
agriculturists at all, but townsmen; that they
refuse the agricultural work offered them on the
ground that they wouldn’t know a plough if they
saw one; that even if they didn’t refuse it the
farmer would assuredly refuse them, having no
use for people who fancy that a cow should be
turned upside down and milked with a shoe-
horn; and that some of them are already drifting
towards the dosser’s refuge in Sydney Domain.
This trouble is quite apart from, and in addition
to, the other trouble about disgusted English
farmers, who have come to N.S.W. and found
nothing offering except a chance to go farming on
shares for the benefit of about the most exorbi-
tant landlord on earth. In selecting his assisted
immigrants Agent-General Coghlan doubtless does
his best to find the genuine British agricultural
person, the real Hodge or Giles Chawbacon. Of
course, he can’t personally inspect all of them;
he can’t wander over the country to look at Giles
and Sabah Chawbacon, and it would be too
costly to bring them all to London for his direct
inspection. But as far as he and his trusted
agents can manage it, the article sent out is the
genuine Chawbacon, warranted to rise early, be
respectful to the squire, and take an interest in
“turmuts.” And the reason of the partial failure,
as one of the authorities at this end disgustedly
puts it, is that there ds no real Hodge left in
England—or none woitth speaking of. The im-
ported men are brought from the backblocks of
Britain, and that looks like a guarantee of their
bucolic character, but it isn't. They are towns-
men all the same—the only distinction is that
they are dwellers in the small towns instead
of the large cities. In England and Wales almost
all the distinctively agricultural counties, such as
Hereford, Huntingdon, Cambridge, Norfolk, Ox-
ford, Rutland, Westmoreland, Cardigan, Pem-
broke, Merioneth, and Montgomery, showed a
loss of population between the census of 1891 and
that of 1901 through the continuous vanishment
of Hodge and Giles Chawbacon, and even
in these counties the . population consists
mostly of the inhabitants of desolate little
towns. Rutland county, for instance, has only
152 square miles, and 19,709 population, yet
there are 14 towns on the map pf Rutland.
When their inhabitants are allowed for, it doesn’t
leave much real Hodge or Chawbacon —much of
the genuineold-fashioned villager—in the allegedly
agricultural county of Rutland. Yet it doesn’t
necessarily follow that immigration from the
British Isles should be a complete failure. A large
proportion of the population of the small English
towns has only lately been driven off the
land, and should be capable of being driven
on to it again. And though the chrome
townsman —the “ stunted factory operative,
of whom so much is heard —is poor material
out of which to make a farmer, yet fn a
new atmosphere he is quite capable of having
a tall brawny son in whose person the lost
agricultural instinct might revive. At all events
Australia should do a lengthy think, and give the
British immigrant a very long and patient trial,
before it rushes to fill up its vacant spaces with
bucolic Italians and Hebrew agriculturists from
Russia. There are agricultural races which are
such and have been such for ages by compulsion
only. :,iul


surroundings, promptly throw off all disguise and
open a squash shop, or a depot for the sale of
speckled fruit, or an ice-cream barrow. And there
■m people like those of the British Isles who have
been driven off the land by villainous legislation,
but who may yet be capable of going back to
it and making a better show than the Roman
or the agricultural Hebrew from Russia. These
last have one serious drawback. Their
agriculture is of the primitive, impoverished
variety that ploughs with a forked stick and tears
off the crops by hand. In these days of intricate
agricultural machinery many an engineer from a
Clyde-side foundry would, with a year’s training,
make a far better farmer than the Neapolitan,
whose ancestors have been dragging things out
of the ground since the days of Scrpio. The keen-
witted engineer would have to learn cows and
grass, and the dull-witted Italian Hodge would
have to learn machinery. It is hard to say which
of them would have more to learn, but it is pretty
safe to say which would be the better pupil.
4
Burnt Grass.
“ Tarboy ” writes from Toowoomba (Q.): —
Have just been reading (B. 7/12/’O7) “J.P.’s” account
of the state of the country west of Charleville (Queens-
land). He is right about the first part of it, for it is like
a wheatfield. It would also “ give a Hunter River pad-
dock a start.” The country is also part of a State which,
if not quite decimated by the Great Drought, as he says,
has suffered very much. So far—good ; but I want to say
something about his conclusions, and I do so because of
his “Stinking Pish” description of the men who came
through that drought with credit to themselves and sub-
stantial profit to their country. By implication, he says
that they are indifferent to their own interests, abso-
lutelyimprovident, lazy, and continually drunk, now they
are prospering again after seven years of hell on earth.
All this because there is much grass in the country, and
it is not being cut for hay. “ J.P.” is a typical tourist—
here to-day and gone to-morrow, and settling problems
which people on the spot have studied for years, without
being able to solve them. Like other tourists, he is fond
of the word “.obvious,” and only a tourist would gibe at,
and offer valueless advice to, the men who worksd hard
and became old before their time in a drought, as to what
they ought to do in the next one.
The lesson “J.P.’’learned is, that it will pay to cut,
make, and stack, natural grasses and hold the hay against
the next drought. He does not state what the “mini-
mum of cost ” is to cut, make and stack one ton of hay,
nor does he state how much one sheep would require as
a ration for one day. He says “obviously,” and there
you are I If he had given figures, then the ignorant, im-
provident, drunken persons who are working the country
might learn how little they know of their business.
Here are a few particulars. It is the result of an ex-
periment carried out on a big station in the West. It
was conducted under ideal conditions, on an open plain
free from obstructions, and only a mile from water. The
cost of cutting, making, stacking, and distributing
worked out a trifle over 10s. per ton of 22401 b. The
ordinary ration for one sheep for one day came out at
.ibout sib. The sum then to be worked on most stations
is, supposing “J.P.’s” advice be taken: If it cost 10s.
per ton to feed bush hay to sheep on the basis of a ration
of 51b. per head per day, how much will it cost to feed,
say, 20,000 breeding ewes in a drought lasting 12
months? Eliminate interest charge on capital sunk and
lying idle until the drought comes along, insurance
against fire, and cost of fencing the numerous stacks.
Answer: 45 tons per day, or 16,425 tons for the year,
which, at the rate of 10s. per ton, works out at £8212
10s., equal to Bs. 2d. per head. If the drought lasted
three years, the cost would be £1 4s. 6d. On dry
feed there would be few or no lambs, and if every
ignorant, &c., manager in the country took “J.P.’s”
advice, and made hay against a drought for his sheep, it
would require a large army of field laborers. Labor for
bush work has not been too plentiful lately, so where is
it to come from for that work? Then there is “J.P.'s"
gibe at the men who asked for cheap rates for carriage of
starving stock. He should ask out on the Barcoo about
those unfortunate men who sent their sheep to the coast,
and how few of the many thousands sent away returned.
Grass seed and fluke killed them off like flies. Mostly
small men, they were ruined by the assistance given. In
the part of the country “ J. P.” writes of, water generally
fails long before feed. Hence, even if the feed had
been cut in times of plenty, it would have to be carted
long distances at great expense.
Obviously “ J.P.’s” politics differ from those of the men
he slanders. My politics differ from theirs too, but that
does not prevent me from trying to be at least fair in
judging those brave and, on the whole, clever men who
work the western runs.
And this is “R.H.P.,” from Longreach, on the
same subject:—
Does “ J.P.” think that men who spend their lives in
the back blocks putting down artesian bores, fencing,
tank-sinking, purchasing high-priced rams, ploughing
firebreaks, and battling, year in year out, are going to
let the drought smother them because they won’t trouble
to avail themselves of the abundance of grass? If “ J.P,”
tried to feed 4000 sheep on bush hay for 12 months (rain
might come in that time, and it might not),
the cost would be £2OOO or thereabouts. Bush hay
costs from £1 to £1 ss. per ton, and it would
take 1400 to 1500 tons to feed 4000 sheep for
12 months, and, in addition to initial cost of hay, there
is thatching, fencing of stacks, draught horses, and labor
to cart and feed to sheep, deaths (for when did anyone
feed sheep on bush hay and not lose a big percentage ?).
Also his water supply must be adequate. After feeding
for perhaps 12 months, “ J.P.” might wake up to find his
immense stacks vanished, and the stock routes closed,
and, after spending the worth of the sheep, lose the
greater number of them. And how many are solvent
enough to keep thousands of pounds stacked for perhaps
five or six years on the ground ? “J.P.” will need to ride
a bit further on coaches, and converse more with the
drivers thereof, before he can teach his pastoral grand-
mothers how to suck eggs.

Irvine, the Little Tutor.
The idea of defending ourselves by any, however well-
trained, military forces we may possess, is, to all
who have studied the question, an impossibility . . . Mot
only our first line of defence, but practically our only
national bulwark at all, lies in the continued and com-
plete supremacy of the British navy. (Loud applause.)—
"• H. Irvine at Knox’s smoke social.
■This W. H. Irvine, the hysterical bounder who
repealed the British Constitution and proposed to
license the push to break into and sack any house
•t suspected of containing strike literature, and
who finally scampered out of Victorian politics
when the bits of newspaper which kept his back-
bone rigid began to peel off, started life in Aus-
tralia as a tutor. And he has the tutor soul still.
Luring the weary years in which he hammered
arithmetic and abbreviated English history into
the skullq of the young Tories whose parents
employed him, the little-souled tutor developed a
fearful upd wonderful reverence for the British
navy—as it was written up by Collier. This
sort of history pictures the terrible British fleets
sweeping the*seven oceans and filling the world
with awe. W. H. Irvine sucked it in
ike a spapgaipiakb gradually the British navy
became in Mof|p||p a sort of fetich, a shield
under which scattered Empire might
sleep, undistqiVp'A. by haunting suspicions that
somebody to break in. But his remarks
we mere wofe. If they mean anything, the
Substance of them amounts to a statement that if
ever W. H. Irvine becomes Premier of Australia
he will give up all attempt to create an Australian
army or navy, because the safety of Australia
depends upon the British fleet. At the census of
1901 there were in the Commonwealth 644,000
men of military age, that is between 20 and 40
years of age Under a proper defence scheme
almost all those men could be available for light-
■ng purposes—a very respectable army, from
which a mobile force of at least 150,000
tould be drawn to carry on an aggressive cam-
paign against an invader, while the less efficient
balance could occupy fortified positions. Irvine’s
theory is that a country with resources like these
should just lie down and be a weeping sneak, de-
pending wholly on mother for defence. He de-
liberately advocates cowardice and lack of self-
reliance. And his remarks about the “continued
and complete supremacy ”of the British navy
clearly indicate that he had either never learned
British naval history, or had forgotten it in the
enthusiasm which Knox’s smoke social roused in
him. A smoke social is the sort of place in which
a man’s fetich is always liable to spring up and
get him down, and when Irvine arose, last Thurs-
day, on the two legs which helped him to retreat
out of Victorian politics, his particular fetich, the
creation of his old tutor days, got hold of him,
and caused him to open his head and fall
in. The “ loud applause ” indicates that
the bulk of the company fell in also. When the
British navy was at the height of its supremacy,
during the Napoleonic wars, it destroyed, accord-
ing to Haydn’s “ Dictionary of Dates,” 1110
foreign warships. Unfortunately, while it was
doing this, and asserting its “complete and con-
tinued supremacy,” no less than 10,000 British
mercantile ships were captured or sunk. The
significance of this lies in the fact that in those
days Britain grew its own food, and had every
British ship gone down it would not have brought
Britain to a state of starvation. To-day, the
position is very different. Britain draws the
great bulk of its food supplies from overseas,
and, in order to keep the food routes from Canada
and the U.S. A. open, every available British war-
ship would be wanted in the Atlantic and around
the British coasts. That the British Government
thinks so is indicated by the manner in
which, during the last two years, the battleships
have been gathered around the heart of the empire.
We Australians do not make the British navy.
We have no voice in its increase or decrease, and
although under Irvine’s scheme the supremacy of
that navy is necessary to us, we have no power to
make the British taxpayer build. Irvine’s idea
is that the safety of Australia should depend
upon the whim of the British elector. If he
decides to economise, then Australia must
perish, because W. H. Irvine, the man
who proposed to abolish Magna Charta,
has decided in his little tutor mind that
the military defence of Australia is impossible.
Yet, consider some of the things which, in their
day, were considered impossible. The Irvine
who lived in Rome about the year 219 8.C., and
made speeches at smoke socials, no doubt declared
that the invasion of Italy by a Punic force march-
ing overland from Spain was impossible. The
other Irvine who supped in the Carthaginian
camp the night before Cannae no doubt declared
that for Hannibal to surround 120,000 Romans
with 30,000 Punic troops was impossible. The
same type of croaker doubtless declared Marathon
and Bannockburn quite impossible. Nobody can
say what courage and good management may not
do, and the man who advises Australia, with a fight-
ing population of 644,000, to sit down idly and
trust its existence to the whim of an overtaxed
people 12,000 miles aw.ay is a sort of lunatic who
is a danger in Parliament, and a coward who
ought to have bricks thrown at him every time he
opens his mouth to advise the country to commit
national suicide.
Britain’s Inability to Hold the Sea.
British Admiral Harding Close, concerning
an important subject:—
We spend 31 millions a year on the navy. We might
as well chuck that money into the sea for all the good it
will do, for what is the use of our going to sea and
winning battles of Trafalgar if we leave a starving popu-
lation behind ?... It is no use our boasting that we have
a powerful navy, and that, therefore, having command of
the sea, our food supply is safe. You cannot get a naval
officer to say so. We never had command of the sea, so
far as the protection of our merchant ships is concerned.
If there was a period in the history of this country when
W'e might say we had command of the sea, surely it was
after the battle of Trafalgar, when there was not an
enemy left on the water. Yet after that battle hundreds
of our merchant ships were captured, and it will be so
again. We cannot protect our merchant ships ; the thing
is impossible. The true blockade will be the impossi-
bility of our ten thousand slow merchant vessels
obtaining any insurance, and being laid up as useless,
as the United States merchant ships were laid up when
the Alabama was about. This will prevent the weekly
arrival of the 400 merchant craft which bring our food,
and cause panic on the corn market, the enemy having
made food contraband of war.
These remarks furnish something for the Aus-
tralian Foreign-trader to chew on. If Britain
can’t protect its own food ships in time of war,
what chance has it of protecting ships carrying
the necessaries of existence to Australia—neces-
saries that the Foreign-trader refuses to allow
Australia to make for itself ? On a rough esti-
mate, Britain lost 10,000 merchant vessels during
the Napoleonic wars, despite its invariable suc-
cess on the sea, and despite the fact that it
devoted itself mainly to keeping a narrow
stretch of water clear of the enemy. The idea of
expecting it, even in the most favorable circum-
stances, to police the many thousand miles
between Europe and Australia, so that the im-
ported guns and cartridges, and machinery and
clothing, and whisky and cigars may arrive safely,
is the hope of a madman.

The Degeneration of Parliament.
The Owk’Sullivan— suggesting the man who,
except for the fact that he had galloping con-
sumption, was quite healthy—is, outside of his
borrowing mania, a sensible and capable politi-
cian much above the average. He made a wise
remark the other day to call attention to the
steady degeneration in grade of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment. This Parliament, he contended, touched
the lowest point yet reached. The frank state-
ment has excited the indignation of the “Re-
form ” newspapers, which are largely responsible
for the present Cornstalk Assembly, but that
makes it none the less solemnly true. Nor is
there much hope that the next Parliament will
show any improvement. It is, indeed,
already announced that some of the leading
men of this Assembly, who, poor though
they be, distinctly raise its average, will not
again be candidates, and there are possibilities
therefore of a still weaker Legislature to come.
There is a remedy, of course—the accession of
fresh strength from outside ; but there is no sign
of that accession, and very little hope of it. The
fact is that Australia must face the problem of its
politician and his pay. At present the politician
is,a sadly sweated person. He gets paid mostly
in honor and glory. His money reward is only
sufficient to keep him in bread and cheese after
necessary expenses are deducted. Naturally that
debars many desirable men from thinking of a
political career ; also, just as naturally, it attracts
a certain number of vainglorious men who are
ready to live from hand to mouth and condemn
their wives and children to eternal poverty for the
sake of the reclame that comes to the man in
politics. Neither result is desirable. Australia
is a country which asks of its nublic men
not only statesmanlike gifts in regard to
purely political matters, but also administrative
and business ability for the general control of a
number of huge State enterprises. The ideal
Australian politician would be not only a wise
diplomat and a courageous leader of public
opinion, but also a cautious banker, a firm
administrator, and a man of affairs generally.
And Australia pursues that ideal with £4 a week
or less in its hand (taking a rough average, allow-
ing for additions by Ministerial salary, and
making deductions for election and other ex-
penses, the politician’s reward in Australia is
probably below £4 a week). The pursuit is rarely
successful. An occasional freak is secured—an
able man who has money enough to stand
the racket, or an able man who is so little
regardful of financial considerations that he is
prepared to be impecunious all his life in his
country’s cause. But Parliament, under the
sweating system, does not nearly represent the
young, vigorous, keen intellect of the country.
That is true of the Federal Parliament as well as
of all the State Legislatures. The people have
to put up with, as a rule, second, or third, or
tenth class abilities. To ensure a remedy, the
terms of political employment must be made
vastly better. Fewer members may be employed,
in the State Parliaments at any rate, but those
engaged must be paid very much higher wages.
It is an axiom that you can’t get first-
class abilities at tenth-class prices. A few
politicians contradict that axiom by giving £2500
a year services at an average reward of £2OO a
year. They only serve to prove the rule. The
average member of Parliament is getting to be
worth just the £2OO a year that he will earn (with
fair luck in getting a share of office) during his
political career ; or is getting into the habit of
adding to that £2OO, by dubious or actually
dishonest means, what heconsidersa “fair thing.”

Training a Child—in Melbourne.
It is unfortunate that, just as that Victorian
rescuer of distressed damsels, the Rev. Geo.
Tkegeaß, is getting his wind for another cam-
paign against the Abbotsford Convent, a bad case
should have occurred in Melbourne, and that the
person implicated should be a parson and a
brother Methodist. This brother Methodist is
connected with an orchard and market garden at
Burwood (Vic.), which were donated to the
“church” by jam-maker Hoadley, another pro-
minent Methodist. There the Rev. G. H. Cole
trains “wayward” youths, some committed by
the Courts, some handed over by parents too
careless or incompetent to control them. On
Feb. 12th the mother of a 12-year-old boy named
Martin desired Tregear’s brother Methodist to
take the boy out to Burwood and “ care for ” him
for a while, he being “wayward” and “irregular.”
So Tregear’s brother Methodist took him—
in a way that focuses public attention on
his methods. He first marched the 12-year-
old child up to the Detective. Office and
left him in charge there while he got his buggy.
Returning, he stood the child on the footpath,
while he snapped a pair of handcuffs on his cap-
tive’s wrists. Next, he removed the child’s braces,
so that, having to hold up his pants, he could not
run away; after that he put the manacled
prisoner in the buggy, and started on the 12-mile
drive to Burwood. It didn’t transpire whether the
rev. brother thought it necessary to put leg-irons
on the kid, or whether he carried a rifle ; nor has
it transpired whether the child (who had never
been before a court in his life, and probably didn’t
know what handcuffs were) was put in irons in a
dark cell on his arrival at this “ home farm but
from what happened while people could see, it
seems a fair thing to be told what happens when
the public can’t see. If a grown man, judged
fit to help in the training of 30 or 40 lads,
cannot control an ordinary boy of 12 without
manacling him in the public street, what sort of
methods does he use to control those two
score youths, some of them aged 18 and 19, out
on the farm ? In truth, the whole business of
training and “caring for” neglected children
wants a good deal more State supervision than it
gets. According to the Vic. Inspector of
Charities’ report, the return of inmates’ labor
above the cost of maintenance was 16s. fid. per
head at Abbotsford, while the Salvarmyßefuge,
which seems to work its victims much harder,
made £7 17s. sfd. per head. What the Rev.
Cole’s charming farm makes goodness knows.
There is another training home at Burwood, run
by a private person who, presumably, cultivates a
large block of land and grows fine crops of vege-
tables just for the pure love of teaching boys how
to cultivate. Also, there is a Girls’ Home at
Mintaro, privately carried on, from which girls
have been in the habit of escaping when they
got the chance. The Church of England and the
Methodists also have “homes” at Cheltenham.
After all, there seems, then, to be money in the
business, and there are certainly large advertise-
ments and comfortable billets ; and that fact alone
ought to make the State suspicious. If the work
were unprofitable, and provided nobody with a com-
fortable job, it might be accepted as a fact that
philanthropy was at the bottom of all the efforts ;
yet even then, as philanthropy can be misguided,
the State wouldn’t be justified in letting things
slide. When philanthropy takes the shape of a
grim-looking preacher making a public street
exhibition of a weeping child in handcuffs —a child
that can’t even wipe away his tears because he
requires both hands to keep his trousers from
falling off—there is room for a lot of State
inquiry. Some people may imagine that the
arrest of the rev. gentleman for assault on a small
boy might be a fair beginning of the investigation.
“ Brother Charles.”
About a cleric whom we’re sorry to lose :
The Rev. C. H. S. Matthews (better known as “ Brother
Charles,” of the Brotherhood of the Good Shepherd)
leaves Australia. Certain utterances of his during- the
election of last December got him into trouble with the
fashionables. Mr. Reid was reported to have said that
Socialists could not be Christians. Brother, Charles
took the ground that Christians coujd be Socialists, and
offered himself as a specimen of whgit'Mr. Reid appeared
to consider a contradiction in terms. Mr. Matthews
called himself a Socialist. He did not call himself a
Christian. He left other people to do that. And other
people did it. Brother Charles was not one of those who
suffer from being- found out. No one within reach of
his Brotherhood had any more need -to be told that its
Vice-Principal was a Christian than they had, need to be
informed that Mr. Reid was not particularly thin.
One supposes that Mr. Matthews’ secret, was his
sympathy. The old Terentian tag might,have been
(and, indeed, was) his motto from beginning to end, and
it begins “lam a man.” Inside the clothes of a clergy-
man there is sometimes not so much manhood as clergy-
manhood. Under Brother Charles’s black coat there
was a white man. He was endowed with the glorious
gift of enthusiasm. * No doubt he had his windmills
(enthusiasm must always have its windmills, especially
when the enthusiast is young ; and Mr. Matthews is
not Mr. Methusaleh). Anyhow, windmills are better
than windbags. Whenever and wherever Brother
Charles saw, or thought he saw, anything wrong—any-
thing mean, or greedy, or canting, or cruel, or dishonest
—whenever he perceived any ugliness of any sort or
description, he charged at it with fury. There was never
any need to s’ool him on, and it was never of any use to
call him off.
Of course, Brother Charles gave offence. That was rather
tragic. But it was still more tragic that he gave offence
to people to whom his sermons had given no offence at
all. In a letter to the writer he said: “I have consist-
ently preached from the pulpit for five years the identi-
cal doctrines I have now proclaimed from the house-tops
—or as near to it as I could get—and no one
took offence, because the pulpit has ceased to influ-
ence conduct in Australia.” It astonished Brother
Charles to discover that people had been glad to hear
him for five years from a pulpit because they thought
he didn’t mean what he said, and that these same
people were horribly angry when he spoke for five minutes
from a platform because they thought that he did. He
was not the “ extremist ” that Dubbo dubbed him—or
some of Dubbo. In the before-mentioned letter he
writes :—“ The defeat of the extremists in Queensland,
where the extremists are in force, was as grateful to me
as the victory of the N.S.W. brand of Socialism in its
own State.”
Of course Matthews had a send-off, where things were
said —the sort of things he hated to have said in his hear-
ing. But he is out of hearing now, and I have said some-
thing more. And I have said it to The Bulletin because
its brilliant band of artists and authors seem somewhat in
want of a parson who can be truthfully expressed
in terms other than those which are required for such
subjects for pen and pencil as Messrs. Stiggins and
Chadband.
Brother Charles was not a scarecrow, and his “ re-
ligion ” was not a scarecrow either. The Brotherhood to
which he belonged was really a Brotherhood —there was
nothing unbrotherly about it at all. And his creed led
to his politics. He called them Socialistic. The main
aspiration of the Socialist—according to certain definers
—is the one that aspires after other people’s money. The
dreams Of the Socialist (say such critics) are of such stuff
as fortunes are made of. Brother Charles didn’t mean
that (he was as poor as St. Francis), and he didn’t want
anyone else to mean it. He himself thought as little of
money as of the ways of some of those who have much of
it. The last time present scribe saw him he was waiting
for a train, and carrying a rug made (as he said)
in Auscralia out of Australian wool by Australian
workers. The straps bore a legend stating that it was
the gift of a few Socialist friends. The whole thing dis-
closed the rug-owner’s assimilative and sympathetic gift.
He had appreciated, and he had been appreciated. And
this because he looked on humanity as real members of a
real family, whereof he knew himself to be one, and not
by any means the only one.
