About 24 Dirty Councils.
A Sydney Health Board return furnishes more
evidence that a Greater Sydney can’t come along
a moment too soon. Under the Health Act
Sydney and suburbs are parcelled out into 49
districts for convenience in making examination
of milk and other foods. These 49 districts are
administered by the City Council and the 40
doyley municipalities, and the return shows the
manner in which the doyley councilshave exercised
the power given them by the Act to protect the
public against adulterated milk and other impuie
food. During 1906 the city itself, and some of the
suburbs, as, for instance, Paddington, Woollahra,
Newtown, Balmain, Ashfield, Leichhardt, Mar-
rickville and Randwick, made some pretence of
doing their duty. Others made either a very
infirm pretence, or no pretence at all. In the
whole of the northern suburban area, containing
something like 55,000 inhabitants, only 15 milk
samples were taken during the year, and Wil-
loughby supplied the whole lot during one month
(November) ; North Sydney (population about
29,000), Mosman and Neutral Bay (11,000) and
Ryde (4000) supplied no milk samples; and Redfern
(24,000), Darlington(4ooo), Botany and Erskineville
(16,000) and Camperdown (of course) supplied
neither milk nor food samples. In fact, of the
49 districts, only 25 supplied any sort of samples ;
that is to say, the dirty, slothful councils respon-
sible for the other 24 districts were too busy con-
templating their little bit of tinsel dignity to care
how many infants were slaughtered by adult-
erated milk, or how many people became diseased,
and consequently incompetent citizens or prema-
ture corpses by consuming impure foods. Wheie
the local authority allows the law to become a
dead letter, the Health Board may procure
samples and prosecute, but the Board is ham-
pered by Supreme Court decision which makes
it difficult to recover incidental expenses; so, by
way of coaxing the supine and dirty councils to
do their work, the Board offers to pay half their
sanitary inspectors’ salaries. The bribe ha 3 been
ineffectual in all save a very few instances. Of
course, the horrible situation has brought out the
usual crop of suggestions for dealing with it, ope
suggestion being to call upon the people to “rise”
The Young Man With an Old Man’s Mind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Least hopeful object of his kind
That in this happy land I find—
The young man wdth an old man’s mind.
He holds the old men’s moral briefs,
He cherishes their stale beliefs,
Has faith in horrors that ensue
From things original and new,
And knows this modern Socialism
To be a frightful hell-born schism !
The pride and lasting joy is he
Of staid Respectability.
Apostle of the Used-To-Be,
He fears the “ Democratic trend, ”
And prates’about its certain end
In anarchy arid blood and fire,
And devastation deep and dire.
He does not hesitate to say
’Twill frighten Capital away !
The veteran with quaking limb,
Whose intellect is growing dim,
Is worth a regiment of him,
If in his youth he helped the storm
For that mad, Radical reform,
The right of man above the beast
To have one little vote at least.
To ancient wrongs he’s quite resigned,
The young man wdth an old man’s mind.
He thinks the race of man is run,
That all worth winning has been w'on,
And all worth doing has been done.
To no authority he’ll trust
But that he delves from out the dust.
The wisdom that he prates about
Is deaf, and blind, and has the gout.
He tells us man by God was made
To honor Fat and vote Freetrade.
The young must daring be and strong.
To drag the timid age along
They need the courage to be wrong.
Since youth the way has still to find,
He serves who fails. Who hangs behind,
And sits among his father’s bones,
And helps the chorus of old crones,
Is guilty of a bitter crime—
A worthless renegade of Time.
VIC.
EDWARD DYSON.


It usually takes years to get a new invention on
the market, but the Tyree Spray has proved an exception
to the rule. Although this machine has only been three
months before the public, already over fourteen hundred
have been sold, and they are still selling rapidly. All
the Government Departments in Sydney, and the prin-
cipal business houses are now using them.
The Tyree Spray is pronounced by all expert-* to be one-
of the most important inventions ever introduced to-
public notice. It is being sold largely for, —
Spraying dairy cattle for tick.
Spraying sheep for fly-blow or maggot.
Spraying fruit trees, vines, potatoes and other vegeta-
tion with insecticides.
Spraying hospitals, gaols, asylums, schools and other-
buildings with disinfectants.
Painting and white-washing dairies, butter factories,
creameries, piggeries, abattoirs, slaughter houses, poultry
yards, schools, factories, wool sheds, stock yards,
barracks, etc.
Killing prickly pear, gorse, lantana, briars, black-
berries, with arsenic.
Painting green skinsfor export with arsenJte of soda.
Fire extinguishing and cutting out fire breaks as a
prevention of bush fires.
It works without labor, and does the work well. Send
for printed matter to W. TYREE, 314 George-street,
Sydney.*

Concerning a Federal Graduated Land-Tax
and an Offer of £l5O.
Some time ago, when the calamitous Senator
Gould made a loud statement to the effect that
a graduated land-tax was forbidden by the
Federal Constitution, The Bulletin was moved
to offer a reward to Mr, Gould, or any-
body else of his way of thinking, who would
produce the clause or clauses in the Constitution
which forbade such a tax. The reward rose
gradually to £l5O, but weeks of eloquence failed
to bring Mr. Gould up to the scratch. He
plainly hadn’t the courage of his alleged opinions,
and he lay low. The money, however, was
claimed by Mr. J. Foster Rogers, of Melbourne,
who based his theory that a graduated land-tax
was illegal on this clause of the Federal bond :
(51) The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution,
have power to make laws for the peace, order, and good
government of the Commonwealth with respect to —
(I)
(II) Taxation, but so as not to discriminate between
States or parts of States.
This is the only clause in the Constitution on
which it is possible to base any semblance
of a theory that a graduated land-tax is uncon-
stitutional ; and, as The Bulletin wholly denied
the applicability of the clause, the matter was
referred to two barristers for arbitration
as provided in this paper’s challenge. The
Bulletin nominated Mr. T. R. Bavin, of Sydney.
Mr. Foster Rogers nominated Mr. Walter
Edmunds, also of Sydney. In the case of a dis-
agreement, they were to appoint a third barrister
as referee, but as both gentlemen decided
that there was nothing whatever in the clause
which made a graduated land-tax in the least
degree unconstitutional, and thus knocked out
Mr. Foster Rogers in one round, a third
man wasn’t required. Senator Gould and the
party that denounces a Federal graduated land-
tax as “ unconstitutional ” will now please look
round for a fresh argument. And next time Mr.
Gould makes a loud and violent assertion,
it is to be hoped that he will show more
grit in backing up his opinion. In this case Mr.
Foster Rogers has lost, but he at least had the
courage of his convictions. Mr. Gould lacked
that courage so badly that even an offer of £l5O to
nothing didn’t fetch him.

A Party Disappears.
In Victoria the swallowing of the Mackinnon
party by the Bent Government is almost com-
plete; and the swallowed party—as is usual in
such cases—comes out of the operation very
badly. Six weeks were spent in discussing “diffi-
culties in the land policy ” of the two parties;
and this, according to the Argus, is how the diffi-
culties have been settled
The arrangement will provide that, if any land taxation
proposals are brought forward, they shall he merely in
the direction of removing anomalies of the present system.
Also
The Government policy will not include any proposal
for the compulsory purchase of land, on this point the
feeling of the parties being that the present law should
be tested first; and the most that will be said will be that
the question of adopting compulsory purchase will be
considered if it is found that the existing law is in-
sufficient.
This is the sort of “compromise” that the man
with the biggest axe usually makes with the other
man. It will be a surprise, however, if the whole
of his 11 followers pursue Mackinnon down
the Government throat. If they do, the need for
a real, live Labor par ty that has no connection with
the dreadful Jack Wren becomes more and more
urgent. Also, the fatuous folly of “ leaving land-
taxation to the States,” which was one of Reid’s
war-cries, is made strikingly apparent. With
curious irony, the Argus says that the most diffi-
cult question to settle was that concerning Scrip-
ture-teaching in State schools. But the same
issue of the Argus reports, in connection with an
Immigration League meeting, that “ population
is declining in the counties of Dalhousie, Talbot,
Follett, Wimmera, Lowan, Millewa, Gladstone,
Gunbower, Rodney, and Wonnangatta.” And,
while population is declining in 10 counties, in-
cluding among them the most fertile part of Vic-
toria, the whole worry of the Argus is about
whether Balaam and Jonah will be taught in
State schools.- By the way, apropos of a current
snake controversy, did Bent slime Mackinnon
before he swallowed him ?
♦
A Bob and a Cot.
A thousand people, at least, were expected to
attend the first “ big” meeting held by the Vic-
torian League of alleged Sportsmen, which took
place in the Athenaeum Hall, Melbourne, on the
12th inst. Although, according to the secretary,
50,000 certificates of membership had been issued,
and although there were some 10,000 members
already enrolled, only 300 citizens arrived. The
Sportsmen have made a bad beginning by offering
a sop to Cerberus. They propose to endow a
“ Sportsmen’s Cot” at the Melbourne Children’s
Hospital, out of cash received from the sale of
certificates, which are being issued at a shilling
each. This good work is not to be done right
away, but after liquidating “ the cost [not stated
or estimated] of organising and completing the
objects of the League.” One of the . objects to
be completed before the cot is endowed is the
“ repeal or entire amendment of the unfair and
inequitable clauses [not stated] of the Licensing
Act and Gaming Act.” This is sufficiently in-
definite to make the promise of a cot seem a very
unimportant matter when one is thinking about
sending the Sportsmen a shilling. Brother
Newby, the Sportsmen’s organising secretary,
who wrote the circular, is nervous about the
damage done by the Acts to “the most cherished
principles of Ber-ritish liberty (hie) and free-
dom ” ; also to “ our rights, freedom and liberties,
as men and citizens,” and to the “spirit of fair
dealing.” There is also an inclination on his part to
drag in Anti-Sosh, by mentioning the well and
favorably-known “ sanctity of the home ” —not to
speak of the sacredness of Sunday beer and the
holiness of after-hour drinks at the Club before
one dashes for the last tram. And yet, in spite
of all these promptings and appeals, the misguided
public only turned out 300 of its representatives
to remove the yoke placed upon its neck by (see
circular) “the tyranny of a few noisy extremists.”
To make out, as the Sports want to do, that
Judfull and Co. were responsible for the passing
of the Licensing and Gaming Acts, on their own,
is a lamentably stupid start for a new movement,
if there is a movement.
♦
Some weeks ago a North Queensland parson—
Lev. James Gillespie, of Walkerston, near
Mackay—wrote a blazing letter to the Brisbane
Courier, about some real or imaginary Kanaka
children, born apparently in Queensland, whose
mother was compelled by the brutal authorities
to take them away to savagedom in the. Islands.
This was so absolutely contrary to the intentions
of the Act that the boss of the department wrote
in haste to Preacher James for the name of the
woman and any further particulars. And, like so
many other preachers, the Rev. James backed
out in this manner :
Mr. Douglas Rannie, —Dear sir,—Your letter came duly
to hand last Friday, but owinjr to my going away I had
not time to attend to it. I desire, however, to say that
as I do not suppose it is the intention of the Government
to do anything- in the way of making amends, I cannot
see my way to help them in trying to obscure the facts I
adduced. I, therefore, decline to forward any informa-
tion on the matter.—l am, sir, &c., James Gillespie.
Not once in a hundred times is it possible to get
the sensational and excited preacher to back
up his amazing narrative with details. In almost
every case, when he is asked for facts, he gathers
up his hymn-book and his hat, and says he
declines to hold discourse with the ungodly, or
something like that, and departs with a long
stride. Any decent layman, when he publishes
a gross charge against his fellow-man, feels bound
in honor to produce his evidence when called
upon, but the sensational parson seldom does.
He seems to be born with “ something lacking,”
the thing that is lacking being certain of the
instincts of a man and a citizen. In this
respect, the Rev. James is something beyond the
average, his avowed reason for not producing his
evidence being that, if he did so, the parties lie
had accused might then be able to clear their
characters—or, as he puts it, they might “obscure
the facts.” That is to say, they might obscure
them by producing some totally different facts.

Premier Carruthers, of N.S.W., after playing
with the machinery requisite to the levying
of shires’ rates, and other matters pertaining
to the system of Local Government till
the eleventh hour, got scared, and engaged
an army of inexperienced clerks. Also,
he put the whole lot on overtime till 9.30
p.m. This army is officered by clerks taken
from other departments, and, in the hope
of getting a move upon his forces, Joseph
ordered the suspension of the regulation which
says that every clerk who works overtime shall
give two hours for nothing each day and only
be paid for the balance. He ukased that men
should be paid in full for each hour worked. This
generosity was hailed with delight by those con-
cerned. But they knew not Joseph. He has his
surplus to consider, so the regulation is only
partially suspended. The new temporary clerk
gets full overtime pay. The permanent officer,
who is putting in the same long hours
to keep things going in the departments which
Joe has depleted to get his shire work done,
plods wearily along every night, in the hopeless
endeavor to keep his work up to date with half
the usual staff, and earns exactly three-fifths of
the overtime paid to the new temporary clerk.
Pick and shovel men would not stand it. Wharf
laborers would apply to their union. But as a
civil servant wailed to The Bulletin : “ We have
no union, and James M‘Gowen is not interested
in our welfare.” And James M'Gowf.n might
fairly reply that the Civil Service has shown
mighty little interest in his or his party’s welfare,
and that the C.S. person’s aloofness has been so
vast that his grievances, like those of the hermit
crab, often escape the notice of a Laborite who
would gladly take a hand at redressing them.

“0.K.”: Re the allegation that no N.S.W.
tweeds are to be seen at the Australian Manu-
facturers’ Exhibition in Melbourne. There is, as
a matter of fact, a “stand” of Marrickville
tweeds at the eastern end of the Exhibition
building, a little to the right of the centre avenue
of entrance. Also, there is a considerable display
of Albion Mills (Vic.) goods, and a glass case full
of Castlemaine woollens at other places in the
crowded show. But the intended effect of these
good Australian products is spoilt, in a great
measure, by their surroundings. The Marrick-
ville goods are environed by a brigade of tem-
perance-drink bottles,'and a shining display of
pumps and other metalmongery. The Albion
Mills “ stand ” is bounded by bold arrays
of pots and pans, pianos and tomato sauce,
whilst the case of Castlemaine woollens is put into
the shade by a brilliant revelation of starch. If
the three kindred exhibits had been allotted
spaces in a reserve for textile fabrics they 1 would
have attracted ten times as much notice as they
get under present conditions. The Exhibition is
a jumble of heterogeneous manufactures, less
strikingly instructive than it might have been,
but a great success, nevertheless. People who
have lately visited the Christchurch “Inter-
national” Exhibition are unanimous in saying
that the Melbourne show of things Australian is
the better show, apart from its sentimental
attractions.

“Leah Kleschna,” which does a brief sprint
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), will be remembered as
the rather remarkable stage-yarn of a young
French Deputy who doesn’t have to wait more
than 24 hours before he bumps hard against the
necessity for putting his own fancy theories into
practice". His idea is that, if you dig deep enough,
and put out drives and crosscuts at the right
levels, you must strike a vein of good in every
man and woman; and that it’s the business
of the State to develop the good, and not
spend so much money on gaols trying to root
out the evil. So when he finds Leah Kleschna,
daughter and pupil of old Kleschna, thief, of
Vienna, taking the family jewels, he doesn’t call
for the police—he talks to her. Sylvaine, of the
Deputies, has a lot of luck when he strikes Leah.
First of all, she had a good mother, and remem-
bers the fact; then she is sentimental, and has
ideas beyond a big fiat with four feathers in it.
But Sylvaine doesn’t know his luck ; so when
Leah weeps and says she’s really sorry, and comes
back next day in the appropriate black frock of
penitence, Sylvaine is so proud of his triumph
that he puts Leah’s name in his pocket-book,
and draws a red mark round it. It looks
as though she were the only “convert” Syl-
vaine ever made; any way, though there is
nothing whatever to suggest matrimonial in-
tentions up to the end of Act ,IV., he pursues
her to a Neustadt vegetable garden in Act V.,
and the wedding is taking place what time the
viscount at the foot of the d.c. stairs is yelling
down Pitt-street for “ Mrs. Foozlem’s carriage.”
Mrs. Foozlem, while she waits on the mat, no
doubt says it’s delightful; but if Miss Muggins,
daughter of old Muggins, the burglar, and herself
a distinguished purse-snatcher and pickpocket
for 10 years, proposed to marry young Foozlem
on the ground that she’d given up picking pockets
and turned scullerymaid, Mrs. Foozlem would
certainly say she was a brazen hussy, and call for
her salts and a policeman as well as her carriage.
Like the Chinaman and the Jap, these brands
plucked from the burning by the hero in Act IV.
are all very well until you are asked to accept
them for a son-in-law or a daughter-by-marriage.
* * * *
There is really no need, though, for
Sylvaine to personally pursue the matter to a
wedding. Obviously he can’t, without run-
ning some risks, repeat the deed if he continues
in the reform business. The most that is called
for from him is an occasional picture post-card.
Still, especially with Miss Brune’s following (all
the nice, good, soulful little girls in the com-
munity swear by her), there ought to be
wedding-bells in the last chapter; also, if
possible, an ivy-covered porch, and an old
vicar, but a wedding certainly. So there you
are. Yet, leaving out this fifth act, it’s
a good, serviceable story, with at least
four fine characters in it—Sylvaine, old Kleschna,
Leah, and Schram. Miss Brune shows more im-
provement as Leah than she has shown in any-
thing else. She used to be too explosive—remem-
ber how she banged the mirror and generally
ramped round in “ L’Aiglon ” ? She has dropped
that, and is acting with more brains. There are
great possibilities about picturesque old Kleschna,
hut the character doesn’t hang together too well
at present. He is supposed to be the great and
successful criminal, but he doesn’t show it during
the play. Thomas Kingston has Roy Redgrave’s
old part, and both of them leave one unsatisfied,
and with no very clear conception of what
the old chap really was. Gaston Mervale,
as the Deputy, breaks right away from Douglas
Gerrard’s reading, and puts some earnest reality
into the character ; Athol wood repeats hjs success
as Schram; and, of the minors, Miss Irby
Marshall, in the only gay moments of the
piece, lifts Sophie Chaponniere into the right
atmosphere, and Maurice Dudley aids and abets
her.
* * *
‘‘ I was educated at Eton and Oxford,
and” — “And then you joined the Service, and
were soon told to send in your papers ’cos you
was too smart at cards to suit your brother
officers,” replies the butler-burglar, who was
seven years in his last place (seven years’ hard).
The expensively schooled card-sharper, who pro-
vokes the reply, laughs “Ha-ha.” He always
laughs at serious things, and the more serious the
thing—such as gagging his wife and throwing her
down a well—the longer is the ha-ha. But the
villain’s clothes fit him beautifully, his hair
is charmingly brushed and brilliantined, and the
audience can hear all his villainous soliloquies
without effort. The good, persecuted young man
of the melodrama suffers from wool in his elocution.
All this has to do with the new melodrama, “A
Modern Judas,” staged at Sydney Criterion last
Saturday, by the Howard Dramatic Co. It
makes heavy demands on the audience’s credulity.
The villain goes a-robbing and a-murdering with
a greater lack of precaution and more open-air
candor than any other criminal this writer has
watched. A small Sydney newsboy would be
more secretive over the purloining of a penny
than is Captain Melville over the diamond rob-
bery and the attempted destruction of his wife.
• • * *
This is the story in a pill-box, A
diamond merchant named Rivers a most
gloomy person, well played by Harry Diver
—is robbed of £BO,OOO worth of gems by
Captain Melville and the Rivers’s butler. At the
moment of the' robbery the merchant dashes
through a secret panel of the room, and is
stabbed to death. The good young man, who has
just become engaged to Miss Rivers, dashes in by
the conventional doorway and is accused of the
robbery and the murder. The police are called
and the handcuffs put on. While the police are
seeking a clue and formulating a theory, a
comic groom unlocks the darbies, and the innocent
man dashes for freedom (this is the third dash).
He becomes a hunted outcast; and his betrothed,
who is certain of his innocence, sits on rocks, by
the loud resounding sea and asks in piercing
tones if there is no justice in Heaven ? Then she
puts white flowers on her murdered parent’s tomb,
and scornfully repulses Capt. Melville’s attentions.
The Captain has a wife already, and she is j D
rags and en route to commit suicide. Miss Rivers
is chloroformed by the villains and dragged off to
a hut; her hunted lover runs to her rescue • the
police dash in (this is the fourth dash), and so’does
the injured wife, who has been an eye-witness to
the murder and diamond robbery. After that all
is happiness and applause for the good persons.
The bad ones fall into the hot soup, and the vil-
lain’s bell-topper can just be seen boiling faintly
in the middle of the bowl, and the audience knows
that several wedding cakes will be ordered next
day. For it’s up to all the comically virtuous and
virtuously comic persons to get married. Jackson
Wilcox plays the r6le of John Vavasour, the per-
secuted hero. George Mountford is Captain
Melville. He, the capable Diver, and Sydney
Howard, as The Moochcr, are the only profes-
sional-like actors in the piece. Lilian Hallows,
as Claire Rivers, looks pretty in her mourning’
Miss Meade is a likeable old maid, and that about
ends the catalogue. “A Modern Judas” will
pursue his nefarious way until fate, in the shape
of a change of bill, overtakes him.
** * *
“ The Kelly Gang ” still rides, and shoots,
and dies triumphantly at Sydney Palace, and the
generation that has arisen since that thirty-years-
ago war peruses with delight the historical page
of blood, and bathos, and slain bobby, and stolen
beef. The show isn’t quite all Kelly Gang, but
that nefarious tale of gore fills so much of the
space that the rest hardly signifies.

There is one joyful circumstance about
Bland Holt’s revival of “The Breaking of the
Drought,” at Sydney Royal: Walter Baker is
cast in a part in which he has absolutely no
chance to whine. But the best part of the drama
—better even than the absence of Mr. Baker’s
note of injured melancholy—is the scenery. The
tableau of the bush fire, and the unfortunate and
mistaken rescue of Gilbert Galloway, are great
painted events; Writer has seen two Aus-
tralian bush fires, and they were both
exactly like that which rages at the Royal.
Another streak of accuracy is the Hurricane
Club, and Yales, the colored comedian; they
bear a startling, although glorified, resemblance
to the famous, or infamous, Wind Club, which
was started in Sydney some years ago by a colored
comedian whose name wasn’t Yales, but some-
thing resembling it very closely. That very
unveiled allusion to the Wind Club makes for
popularity. But, if report speaketh truly, there
is a mighty difference between the Hurricane
Club at the Royal and the Wind Club that used
to rampage at the back of the Central Police
Station in Sydney.
».*■»*
Like many other melodramas, ‘ ‘ Breaking
of the Drought ” tries to compress a three-volume
novel into three hours’ acting, and the result is a
sense of fulness. The beginning of the story
is forgotten long before the end is reached.
And, sad to say, the teaching of the play is
strictly immoral. Gilbert Galloway, having
forged his father’s name when the old man had
been burnt out, and thus added insult to
injury, reduces the whole family to desti-
tution and Woolloomooloo lodgings. He goes on
his gay career, walking round Paddy’s Market
in evening dress with the lydies of the Hurricane
Club, and finds his ma running a coffee stall. The
old man has saved G. Galloway by acknow-
ledging the forged signatures as his own.
Gilbert apparently never stops drinking cham-
pagne for one moment, and he only resolves to
change his mind and mode of life when all his
money has run out, and his father has got back
to comparative affluence through being appointed
manager of his old station by the bank, then
Gilbert comes along, is forgiven for mother’s
sake, and taken to the bosom of the
family. If this isn’t immoral, what is?
The drama should teach emphatically that
the wages of sin are death and cheap burial.
The acting in the revival is as good as the quality
of the play will allow. The star part of old Mrs.
Galloway is cleverly done by Miss Maggie
Knight, who rises far above the merits of the
piece. Miss Harrie Ireland, of the beautiful hair,
has been the lovely adventuress so often now
that the character comes natural to her, and
although she has little opportunity she makes
the very best of what she has. Miss
Frances Ross is certainly good in the first
part, but where she gets into the Hurricane Club
her acting is severely overdone, and, as Mr.
Yales says, “If this is your unsophisticated
mountain daisy, then the bush for me. ’ That
same part of Freddy Yales, by Charles Brown, is
one of the bright things of the piece ; and Walter
Baker does better than this writer has seen him
do for the last five years. The Drought will con-
tinue to Break every night till further notice.

If ever there was a man entitled to sym-
pathy it was Mr. Donoghue, who allowed himself
to be inveigled into being married at “Wonder-
land City” (Sydney) last Saturday. On the
evening of the torture the groom and the
lady were corraled at Tattersall’s Hotel, in
Pitt-street, where the unfortunate man was
deprived of every chance of escape. A
swift motor whirled them away to Wonderland
City, where their faces were stained with walnut
juice, and their figures draped in some weird
Oriental garments, and they were loaded down
with jewellery that was bogus and gems
that were brum. The actual marriage took
place in the King’s Theatre, for the clergy-
man objected to perform the ceremony m
a howdah on Alice’s unsteady back. Imme-
diately the knot was tied the two were
placed in the howdah, and thence led out into the
glare of 30,000 pairs of eyes. Music rolled and
crashed, the confetti fell m showers, and sham
Hindoo girls danced and whirled their draperies.
The confetti got into Alice’s ears, and the old
elephant blinked and nodded as she wandered
round the paths and by-ways of the gardens,
while a vast mass of inquisitive and (much ot
it) envious female audience laughed and cheered in

A Queensland dame has suggested to the
Melbourne Argus that the black gin may prove
an ideal solution of the servant gal trouble. Her
strongest argument is, “Wages vary; never ex-
ceeding ss. per week. Sometimes this is paid
partly, or wholly, in kind.” A section of Toorak
thinks seriously of cajoling Bent into the importa-
tion of a few shiploads of black Mary, the de-
lightful creature who runs wild in the Northern
Territory and who can be paid with a pair of old
boots and a pumpkin from the kitchen garden.
But Mary, in her native garb, boiling the ’possum
on an elaborate kitchen range at St. Kilda, seems'
only a dream.

Next Saturday at Wonderland City will be
a night of nights. The management reckons that
the thirst of gold will bring to Wonderland the
entire population of Sydney and suburbs. The
Poseidon Rush will be re-enacted. A nugget will
be buried on the beach, and everyone who
cares to buy a sixpenny spade at the gate may
prospect for the prize which the proprietor wiil
afterwards buy for £2O. Many bogus nuggets
will also be buried, but these will betray themselves
by the inscription on the box—“had again,” or
something like that. Still for a blissful second
the digger will think he has the Poseidon prize.
What will happen if the tide comes in whilst the
search is in full blast is not specified. Nor do
the nugget sponsors say
anything about what will
be done if, with the cussed-
ness of inanimate objects,
the gold is found almost im-
mediately by the very first
shoveller. Apparently the
only thing then will be to
hang out a notice “Nugget
found, Poseidon Rush burst
up for the night.” And
how much will it cost W.
Anderson to make the beach
presentable again after the
mob has dug it full of
craters ?

These are days when
the largest stars at the Tivoli
are preparing to pack the
things they glitter with into
iron-bound boxes marked
“Not wanted on the voy-
age.” For, when you come
to think them over, numer-
ous recent stars have mainly
twinkled through their
properties—Chanti, for in-
stance, the man who is
many things in a little
while. But the long run
of juggler Selbo is still unbroken, and Gracie
Graham continues to sparkle daintily, and their
travelling trunks aren’t visible yet.

In “ The New Clown ” there is a new idea.
At a careless glance, the ludicrous situations have
an appearance of being filled with farcical charac-
ters, but when the matter is closely considered it
is found that only one character—the central one
—is of a farcical nature. The others do nothing
that is positively absurd, although in two or
three instances they seem stone blind to a
palpable fraud. Last year the Willoughby-
Ward Co. was successful enough in the attempt
to tickle Melbourne’s fancy with “ The Man from
Mexico,” an altered American comedy which gave
the audience little or no relief from its strain of
impossible circumstances. This time the same
co., making its reappearance at the Princess’s
without any danger of disappointing a too
extravagant expectation, is not unlikely to be
nioie successful than before. A little piece of
originality will sometimes go a long way in public
favor, or another little piece may fall as flat as a
pancake on the threshold of Lent. One never can
tell. “The New Clown,” at any rate, is only a
very little piece.
* * * *
The story differs at the outset from the
ordinary old tale of a fool flying from imaginary
trouble, and taking a new name. As a rule, the
cause of flight originates before the curtain goes
up. Lord Hugh Ward, an effeminate dilettante,
whose principal vice is a cigarette, arrives at his
woe in full view of the audience. He uninten-
tionally pushes an awful military captain off a
window sill into the river, and supposes his friend
has found the watery grave he deserves. Ward
is a grotesque Wilfrid Denver, suffering agonies
of fear on account of a corpse that never occurred.
A drunken circus clown has reasonable cause for
selling his business references and other personal
belongings to Lord Ward. So the seared little aris-
tocrat comes to be engaged to make a professional
A/r George Willoughby’s circus, where
Miss Grace Palotta is the proprietor’s coquettish
niece, and a star equestrienne in the arena. The
new clown surpasses all understanding. He is
too helpless and unmanly for words, but the girl
naturally softens to him owing to his gold-
mounted cigarette-case and his lovely match-box
and because he treats her “like a lady,” whilst
her sworn admirer, the circus Strong Man, is a
jealous dumb-bell shifter, who treats her more
uke his future wife. The clown, therefore, is
cordially smiled upon and encouraged, and
cross-examined by La Belle Palotta what
time his own best girl, who visits the
circus with her friends, is cut off from
inm by his disguise. A flimsy sub-plot is hatched
from a £5O legacy, which comes to the new clown
in his impersonation of the old clown. When
the misunderstandings are at last explained, the
circus staff pays homage to the lord whose great-
ness has been levelled to the sawdust. The most
inconsistent features of the performance are cer-
tain interpolated songs and dances. The dances
are brighter than the songs, but when the clown
shakes his educated legs he shatters his own pre-
fence of lordly incapacity for clowning. Also the
best song, which deals with the Fall of Adam,
must have been written by someone who had
never read his Bible and didn’t understand the full
significance of fig leaves in Eden. Hugh Ward’s
conception of the effeminate lord character is not
at all convincing in the first act. In the broadly
farcical incidents he is Ward at his best-weak of
voice and personality for so exacting a part, but
always a humorous, resourceful comedian, rather
than a mere player of the fool. Miss Palotta and
Willoughby are so well placed in their smaller
and easier parts that they look almost as im-
portant as the man who does most of the work •
Arthur Cornel] gives a rather clever sketch of the
beery old clown, and among the others who play
characters the two ladies are more satis-
factory than five or six men. A silent performer
(Joe Walhalla) snatches a burst of applause for an
eccentric skipping act, which suggests that it
would be advisable to introduce one or two other
such items (indigenous to the show), and cut out
the same number of incongruous ditties for the
principals.

This is the ninth week of the panto, at
Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), and the rumor that
next week will be its last sounds more truthful
than theatrical rumors in general. But the roots
of “Mother Goose’s ” popularity have struck very
deep down, and whilst the tree continues to yield
fresh crops of golden fruit it would seem a pity to
shift it.

“The Squatter’s Daughter” is destined to
fill Melbourne Royal for some weeks. The scenery
and fixings, as previously stated, are the most
admirable Andersonian achievement up to date,
and the labors of the orchestra deserve
special mention. There are few moments of any
particular importance when the band doesn’t
play. Music soft and s'ow, or fast and furious,
accompanies the drama on its wild Australian
way. The wickedness of the chief villain is pro-
claimed by lurid bars of infamy just before he
appears, and the perfume of the wattle is touch-

Mrs. Barbary, at a meeting of mediums in Melbourne,
contended that Australia should be for Australians, and
that no foreign spiritualistic talent should be introduced.
Here we have a Chow spook trying to pass the
education test.
mgly indicated by the first cornets. This scribe
has forgotten whether the drummer bangs
his instrument when the elderly spinster from
England sits upon a bull-ants’ nest, but he thinks
the occasion is marked by a note of exclamation.
No trouble has been spared in the management of
“ The Squatter’s Daughter.” The scenic acces-
sories are so full of Australian color that some of
it has run, and the character that Miss Guildford
plays—an improper white daughter of the soil—
is tinged with native naturalness. All the same,
this country may be earnestly advised to aim at a
higher form of Australian drama than it is
getting just now in “The Kelly Gang” Biograph
and the highly-decorated production at the
Royal. A lot of Australian sentiment might be
stimulated by the scene of Waterfall Gully,
without dropping the bushrangers into the wet.

** * *
Harcourfc Beatty and Miss May Congdon
have had a “Midnight Wedding” at Adelaide
Royal for a whole week. Beatty makes a dash-
ing swordsman, though there are other men, not
up to his standard of acting, who could do the
thing quite as well. The play shows how people
who are shot or run through the body can be
about again, bright and well, within a week ; also
the villain has to ask many foolish questions —
“Who will prevent me?” “Why will you not
fight me ? ” and so on—on purpose to get regular
knockdown answers. But the piece goes with a
swing, and it lingers in the memory by pleasant
contrast with the dreadful thing that has replaced
it. “The Fatal Wedding ”is indeed deadly.
Fred Bluett is back at the Tivoli, and the Hippo-
drome has a Handcuff King.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Melb.”: Mr. William Ryan’s extremely interesting
recollections (B. 14/2/’O7) go backwards and forwards in
point of time in some matters. Allow me to straighten
out dates : The year 1855 was marked by three successive
Melbourne theatrical events : Brooke’s opening at the
Queen’s, the opening of the Theatre Royal, and that of
Ooppin’s Olympic. The year 1856 was signalised by
manager Black’s breakdown at the Royal, whereupon
Coppin and Brooke went there. Then ensued their part-
nership in Theatre Royal and Cremorne. In 1858 Brooke
ventured on the Royal management alone. He left for
England in 1861. Barry Sullivan came in 1862, Lyster in
1861, Mr. and Mrs. O. Kean in 1864, Dillon in 1865, Mont-
gomeri' and Anderson in 1867, Celeste in 1868, Mathews
in 1870, Ristori in 1873, Creswick in 1876. I don’t think
Rogers and Lambert acted together so much as Mr. Ryan
makes out. There was keen jealousy between them. I
have only come across one occasion when they acted
together in Melbourne. Mr. Ryan does not precisely
discriminate between Mr. and Mrs. Charles Young and the
brothers Richard and Frederick Younge—with an “e.”
“ F.” : He “ The Boomtime Stage ” (B. 14/2/’O7). If not
an impertinence, kindly permit me to point out some
errors. In 1862 Mr. Ryan opened in “ The Dream at Sea”
in the York-street (Sydney) theatre under “Paddy ” Bel-
field, and six months later he was engaged to support
G. V. Brooke. This tragedian did not arrive in Australia
until early in ’55, his first appearance at the Victoria
Theatre, Sydney, being May 15th in that year. Harwood
himself said that his first appearance w as made as Flavius
Corunna in “Brutus, or The Fall of Tarquin.” Brooke
did not open the “Iron Pot” (the Olympic). That
theatre was opened by Jacobs the Wizard, Brooke being
engaged elsewhere. Brooke and Lyster were not in
opposition; the former left Australia in 1861, and the
latter arrived in the same year and opened at the Theatre
Royal, Melbourne. Brooke did not marry Avonia Jones
in Australia ; they were married at Liverpool (England)
in 1863. And, by the way, who was “Bob” Young?
There were Richard and Fred Younge, and Charles Young
(without an “ e ”). Can’t locate Bob.
The New York American, just to hand, reports what it
calls a London scandal, and says the same W'as hushed up
and kept out of all the papers, by the influence of a
duchess. This is its yarn : Mrs. Beerbohm Tree had been
dissatisfied with the parts assigned her by her husband.
She was jealous of the Potter, when that beauteous clay
.appeared as Calypso, in “Ulysses.” Mrs. Tree took an
engagement wnh Waller, who is galvanising the Lyceum
as Irving’s successor, and who was formerly in Tree’s co.
Waller boomed her on the strength of her fashionable
reputation. Also, Waller has a motor car. Tree, in
allowing his Mrs. to go to Birmingham W'ith Waller, made
a stipulation that they were not to motor together. Mrs.
Tree broke that. She drove with Waller to the duchess’s,
the potent dame who keeps things out of the paper.
There was a fearful motor accident. Mrs. Tree was
carried to the duchess’s with her face so disfigured that
she can never act again. Then Tree came from London,
and shook his branches indignantly at Waller. That’s
the tale of the New York American.

The £l5OO rhinoceros in the Adelaide Zoo is dead, after
24 years’ residence. It was supposed to be only a year
or two old when it arrived from Singapore, but “ senile
decay ”is officially given as the cause of death. Senile
decay of a rhinoceros sounds like nervous breakdown
of a pyramid.
“Hassan”: Madame Flaro, who, after pouring kero-
sene over her clothes, and applying a match thereto,
slides down a wire into a pond, had a rather narrow
escape of being grilled last week (13/2/’O7). I think that
half the pleasure of the looker-on at these foolish “acts”
consists of the fearful anticipation of an unhappy ending.
One fe Is just the same as toward the hero of a melo-
drama, or novel, but then it is only a diluted pleasure.
The hero runs frightful risks, but we know the author’s
hand is on the helm. In real life there is no one at the
helm. When the accident occurs, the pleasure that we
felt over its possibility becomes merged in sympathetic
horror for the victim. That is why the women who
cheered Madame Flaro as she slid down the wire shrieked
and covered their eyes as she dropped blazing to the
ground, and was rushed into the pond by an attendant.
But why not let our community give up the hypocrisy of
sham horror at Nero, who, being a good soul at bottom,
while enjoying the thrill and artistic effect of incan-
descent Christians, muttered “Poor devils 1” as they
squealed.
Louis Bradfield, the nimble, gentlemanly, little comedian

ideal Captain Ooddington of “In Town is working his

“ Snell ” : The eternal bushranger crops up in Ander-
son’s successful Australian drama, “The Squatter’s
Daughter,” a play made on the premises by a brace of
actors, and one shudders at the idea of the bushranger
becoming a convention of the local drama. Conventions
are stubborn things, particularly in melodrama. Once
they are established, it would appear to be impossible to
break away from them. Nearly all the conventions of
the British melodrama have descended to us from the
days of Richardson, and, if you repudiate them, the
patrons of sensation plays won’t believe your play is a
play at all, and won’t have it at any price. These con-
siderations make the painful recurrence of the bush-
ranger in plays of Australian manufacture a source of
great uneasiness to one who has hopes of living to see
the Australian market for melodrama largely supplied by
the Australian manufacturer. For heaven’s sake, let us
break with the ordinary blood-and-thunder bushranger
before he becomes chronic. Australia is no mean coun-
try, and is not so poverty-stricken in the matter of villains
that we cannot afford to abandon Kelly, Thunderbolt,
Starlight, Hall and co.
A case heard at a Melb. court last week threw a blaze
of light on the suburban theatrical business. Plaintiff,
who lost his case, had been manager of the Royal
Dramatic Co., and he claimed a third share of the profits
of the enterprise, which amounted, he said, to £45 in 22
weeks. The “ star ” artists were engaged at half-a-crown
a night, and, presumably, they drew their salaries,
although the margin of profit was sometimes very small.
Accounts for one night showed takings £1 13s. 4d., and
expenses £1 13s. 2d. Needless to state that the Royal
Dramatic Co.’s repertoire included “The Kelly Gang.”
But its most successful production was “ Poisoned Milk,”
a drama of domestic interest, which always appealed to
the ladies.
“F.”: Re “The Boomtime Stage” (B. 14/2/’O7).
“ Paddy ” Belfield’s proper name was Peter, a tailor by
trade, who was performing in Sydney in the early forties
in the City Theatre, Market-street, under Jce Simtnonds.
When the stage left Bellieid, he returned to his trade,
and in the early seventies was in business in Oxford-
street, near the Old Robin Hood Inn, between Edward
and Brisbane streets. While there he conceived the
notion that Woolloomooloo and Surry Hills should have
a theatre all to themselves. He opened negotiations
with Sydney Burdekin, who granted a lease of certain
vacant land in Liverpool-street, with a right of entrance
from Oxford-street. The site was about midway between
the old-time pub., the Blind Beggar, and the lane lead-
ing down to Yurong-street. A company was formed,
and the walls of the new building were run up some feet.
Then funds gave out, and the scheme collapsed. Sydney
Burdekin seized the bricks, and with them built a row of
cottages on the site. Mr. Belfield subsequently started
business in George-street, Brickfield Hill. “ One morn I
missed him on the. accustomed hill,” and found that he
had become a boniface at the Victoria Hotel, attached
to the Victoria Theatre, in Pitt-street. That was in 1878.
He played the part of host for a few years, and again re-
turned to his trade, this time in Liverpool-street, a few
doors from George-street. In 1889 he was gathered to
his fathers.

“ Hiphen ”: St. Kilda (Melb.) has been transformed
from a quiet, respectable residential suburb into a howl-
ing Coney Island, and the outraged inhabitants, who are
highly “ clawss,” gnash their teeth helplessly and curse
the Council and its doings to ten places of decimal .
There are English Pierrots, a Royal British riding-gallery,
a French cinemetograph, a German band, Italian ice-
cream caterers, and numerous Yankee attractions —
“ straight from Coney' Isle.” The largest of these last
distractions is “ Dreamland,” which, to those residents
who live within range of its brass band, is a nightmare.
When all the shows get going, it is pandemonium, com-
pared with which a Judkins meeting is a rest cure.
“ Dreamland ” has a band of the most virulent type, the
merry-go-round has a steam hurdy-gurdy' with a reper-
toire of four tunes, the German band was made in Ger-
many, and some of the Italian music Was made
in hell. Some evenings these noises are augmented
by the efforts of the tramway employees’ band.
The latest and most thrillful Yankee importation
is a glorified switchback arrangement yclept “ Cutting
the 8.” When I first cut that 8 I thought I was going to
throw a 7. But it’s quite safe, and you obtain all the
sensations of an appalling catastrophe without the un-
pleasant after-effects.
A retiring person is the successful mummer. Edna
May, the original London Belle of hew York, has been
saying to an interviewer : “ Popularity—or, shall I say,
familiarity—is gratifying, but very embarrassing to one
who shuns publicity and desires only' to do one’s work
conscientiously and artistically.” On this Glasgow
News comments: “Miss May* shuns publicity' to the
extent of about forty' new photographs and a score of
interviews a week. Her efforts to remain in the back-
ground are a conspicuous success, of which other per-
formers are keenly’ jealous.”
The story of the escape of Eroni’s lioness at Bateman’s
Bay recalls an old-time incident somewhere in the Vic-
torian bush. between Creswick and Ballarat. A small
circus, coming down from Maryborough, camped for a
performance in the locality mentioned, but the very day
it arrived rumor reported the escape of one of the
animals of the unparalleled menagerie. Panic ensued.
Not a single soul turned up to the performance, but the
adjacent male populace went out with guns to suppress
the ravening animal. They didn’t find it, but in the
morning, shortly after daylight, one of the parties, dead
weary and footsore, and trailing its guns after it, came
to the circus, and found a bill on the tent, offering 2s. 6d. .
for the recovery of a vanished bandicoot.
In a November issue of London Express Archibald
Haddon furnished some figures showing the growth of
the mumming profession in Britain. Thirty-five years
ago there were in all the United Kingdom only 166
theatres, employing less than 8000 people ; now there are
over 1000 theatres, employing 32,000 persons, of whom
12,000 are actors and actresses. London at that time
had 45 theatres and practically no music-halls ; now it
has 800 theatres, music-halls, and concert-halls. Thirty-
five years ago there were only three travelling theatrical
companies; now there are nearly 300. The salaries of
“ theatricals ” in 1872 were less than £1,000,000 a year;
the amount now paid in salaries annually is fully
£4,000,000.
The Musgrove co. is to produce Meyerbeer’s “Le
Prophfete.” It was an eminent Italian tenor who, after

Prophet is."
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. report:—For our
seventeenth wool catalogue of the season we submitted
2413 bales, and disposed of by auction and private treaty
2192 bales. The selection was rather a miscellaneous
one, and included only a few superior clips, notably the
Goldswortb/v/New England, from Uralla, in the greasy
section, which sold up to 13fd. per lb. Scoureds were
represented by wools from various districts of N.S.W.
and from Central and Southern Queensland, the highest
price obtained being 22$d. for a small parcel branded
Caithness, well-prepared by the Alba Wool Scouring
Works, Watten, Queensland. The market as compared
with our last sale showed no quotable change. All care-
fully-prepared wools were keenly competed for, whilst
the irregularity noticeable for some time past on burry
and wasty sorts was still apparent. Our sales to date
total 55,933 bales.*

Matthew Davies (knight), after years of
patient waiting in the shade, shows a disposition
to obtrude himself upon Melbourne’s notice once
again. Matthew is president of the local Law
Institute, in which capacity he helped to welcome
Chief Justice Madden on his return from Europe
the other week. A few days later he presided
over a meeting re the proposed Legal Benevolent
Fund. By a strange coincidence there happened
in the meantime the death of poor old Ducker,
the liquidator of a Daviesian bank and a one-
time sturdy spokesman on the subject of land-
boom financial operations. The revival of Davies
may have hastened aged Dueker’s decease. But
he was 86, anyhow.

SOCIETY
It was the Australian Stab that launched
Carl Zeno on to the unprotected public of Sydney,
and, while he had the opportunity, Zeno made
things hum. Large headlines and a quarter
column of inky amazement at the wonderful
powers of its new pet started a wild rush of
fools, affluent and otherwise, to where Zeno hung
out his shingle in Eorbes-street. He was not an
ordinary fortune-teller, and did not read hands,
or go. into trances, or anything so common. He
started his shekel-raking with an imposing trick.
Anyone who had money to spare for a 10 minutes’
interview with Zeno wrote down his questions on
a small piece of paper, folded it twice, and placed
a forefinger on it. Zeno gat at the other side of
the table, drew a chart of the hand on a
piece of paper, and placed it over the
slip on which the client had his finger.
Having drawn a chart of the hand and
indulged in some occult talk, couched in horribly
bad English, he would beg the client to take his
mind right off the questions. As a rule the client
took his eyes off,them at the same time, and that
was when Zeno managed to extract the paper with
the questions from under the client’s hand if he
had previously failed to get it away while drawing
the chart. Picking up a piece of cardboard he
would lean back and “workout the horoscope.”
As a matter of fact, he was unfolding the paper
with the questions, and these he would proceed to
r ead out in a disjointed manner to the gaping
Jient and answer—also disjointedly. Then came
A DRY SPLASH.
THE HERE ( who has spilt wine on his neighbor): “ A thousand pardons, madam; but
foriunatlee it was zee dry wine."
the ticklish part of the business. The paper had
to be refolded and got back under the client’s-
finger, and the dummy Zeno had placed there had
to be slipped away. . This was the only scientific
part of the swindle, and as, a sleight-of-hand
artist Zeno must have taken a lot of beating.
Zeno was a sad example of a clever man handi-
capped by lack of education. He could nbt speak
cqrrectly, and deciphering the written word was
Ins weakest spot. So it frequently happened
that, after he had gone to all the trouble'of ex-
tracting the blessed paper, he was unable.,te read
the questions on it. As far as he was concerned the
writing was cuneiform. But his genius was equal
to all difficulties! Sometimes he would have the
questions written out afresh, and if the writing
was still unreadable he would explain gently
that his gift was—notr working well to-day.
But rthe. dady -• below’o di'd not " refund the
money. The client paid to see Zeno, and
having seen him and occupied his time for so
long, had got his money’s worth. When Zeno
started, he charged half-a-crown for five minutes.
The price jumped to ss. for three minutes, and
the rush increased. Then carriages and motor-
cars appeared, and prices soared up once more,
till a Week or so before his exposure he was
getting as high as 255. and 30s. for every 10 or 12
minutes of his time. He must have coined money
while he lasted. Some of the allegedly shrewdest
business men in Sydney consulted him regularly
before embarking in anything big, and were said
to have practically handed over the control of
their businesses to him. But ho names are sup-
plied and there have been no big financial smashes
of late, so this last item is somewhat discredited!

Melbourne Continuation School* the
Socialistic institution which was started a year
ago to carry On the education of kiddies after
they have imbibed all that the primary schools
Cart teach them, has just issued its first scholastic
balance-sheet, and the Squeerses, Who raised
loud laments of coming trouble When it Was
projected, are down and out. Though it
has been going only a few months, the
Continuation School has put up a record
never approached by the multitude of private-
enterprise secondary establishments. It has the
Unique distinction of having scored 100 per cent.
Of passes with its half-dozen chemistry and draw-
ing pupils in the junior public examination just
Over. The Continuation School sent altogether
33 candidates for the examination* ahd 24
passed fully, eight Of the remaining nine hat-
ing to pass One subject only to Complete
their examination. The lowest aterage gained
by the Continuation School Was 71 per Cent, of
passes in Latin ; the whole of the private schools
Of the State gained 48'6 per cent, in the Same
subject. In chemistry and drawing, ih Which
every one of the State school candidates passed,
the private school percentages were : chemistry,
76 ; drawing, 29'4. In English the socialistic in-
stitution’s percentage was 97, that of Private
Enterprise 47‘5. Next to chemistry, the subject in
which the outside schools scored best was history,
74’5, while the Continuation School secured 96
per cent, in the same subject. No wonder the
private Squeers is bitter.
m * * *
Evil is a wide-spreading . thing, and the
consequences of it turn up in remote places. The
North Shore (Sydney) people have what they call
a septic tank for sewage at Middle Harbor. A
real septic tank is absolutely covered in, and in
the darkness organisms are developed which
destroy all the foul matter. What goes in is
sewage ; what comes out is clean water. Unfor-
tunately, the Middle Harbor tank wasn’t pro-
perly covered. Therefore, it wasn’t a septic tank
at all; only a bad smell a,nd a huge puddle of
refuse. Now that nefarious-bogus-sham tank has
spread as far as Geelong (Vic.). Geelong has a
clean party which Wants to get septic tanks for
the purification of the town, and a dirty party
which quotes that bad smell in Middle Harbor as
an evidence that septic tanks are of no use, and
as a reason for letting Geelong remain dirty.
That is as far as we knoiv the evil example to have
spread, but the same old beastly tank may be
serving, for all we can show to the contrary, as an
excuse for not cleaning up the alleys of Timbuctoo,
or as a palliation of the garbage in the streets of
Punto Arenas. The Bulletin feels inclined to
preach a sermon on the effects of bad example,
with the tank for a text.
Premier Bent is now preparing to build
four socialistic Queen Anne cottages on the
filleddn Elwood swamp, as an example of the
type of building Private Enterprise ought to put
up, but doesn’t. They are to be built of wood,
as the made ground isn’t deemed capable yet of
standing the weight of brick or stone. Melbourne
has, however, a vast area of land that isn’t liable
to sink and swallow up a building, even if it were
as heavy and substantial as Queen Bess; so the
haste to put up a flimsy Queen Anne affair on
ground that won’t yet bear anything less shoddy
than wood and hessian seems uncalled for.
SOCIETY MANNERS.
HOSTESS: “I suppose you often accompany songs, Professor ?”
PROFESSOR: “ More of den, gonversation, madam.”
“Flaneur”: A Stinking Fish daily set
going an article the other day .on “ Buenos Aires,
the finest city in the Southern Hemisphere,” and
all,the little Stinking Fish papers are giving, the
yarn a wider circulation, on the principle that
pretty well anything is better than Australia.
It started with a Chambers’s writer, who called
Buenos Aires a “ Queen City filled with Parisian
attractions,” The facts : Most of the streets are
narrow, and two tram-tracks occupy
them so fully that vehicular traffic is
possible in only one direction. To
allow vehicles to go both ways in some
main streets only one tram-track is
laid. The fashionable street, Florida,
is but.6oft. wide. Parisian attractions !
,In Music ? The opera is irregular,
poor and dear ; theatres and actors are
fourth-rate ; the music halls are un-
speakable. In Art ? The Buenos Aires
art gallery consists of half-a-dozen
narrow rooms on the first floor of a
business building, and the paintings
and sculpture are only fit for distri-
bution by the Eight-Hours Art Union.
In Literature ? Argentina has no litera-
ture if one excepts the pedigree stock
books of cows, horses, pigs, and sheep.
Its great journal, La Prenza, is about
as well-written and conducted as La
pRESSE, in Paris, and every traveller
knows the worth of that. Most houses
in Buenos Aires have flat roofs, where
the citizen stores his rags and empty
bottles, and brings up his fowls and
dogs. The roads are made of cobble-
stones in the city and mud in the
suburbs. The lighting of the squalid
streets is imperfect. Perhaps the
Parisian attractions are the cafes,
where vile cocktails of bad liquor are
sold at a shilling, and backblocks
“swanky ” at sixpence.
The . faded, crooked tail-end of George -
street, Sydney, down by Circular Quay, is to be
widened to 90ft. It isn’t nearly enough, but even
that ' concession was only gained at the last
moment; the C’ruthers Government was on the
point,of selling its resumed allotments and leav-
ing only 80ft. of a roadway. Some day Sydney
will have to face the problem of making George-
street a straight, imposing avenue at least 100 ft.
wide, instead of a highway of varying width
which still follows the tracks of the old teamsters
as they dodged the stump of century before last.
There was a chance to make a fair beginning of
the work, but the present N.S.W. Government is
a Ministry of lost chances.
SWEET FANTASY.
[Fob The Bulletin.]
As I went up on Selsby Hill,
On Selsby Hill—
As I went up on Selsby Hill
Early in the morning—
The poppies in the fields of corn
Were crimsoning to the crimson morn,
As I went wandering, all forlorn,
On Selsby Hill in the morning !
There was a maiden fair to see,
Fair to see 1
There was a maiden fair to see
On Selsby Hill in the morning.
Her eyes were clear as a summer moon,
Her lips as red as a rose in June,
Ah ! as she sang the wild wind’s tun.q
On Selsby Hill in the morning ! . •
“ What is thy name ? What dost thou here
On Selsby Hill ?
What is thy name ? 'What dost thou here
On Selsby Hill in the morning ? ”
“ I know' not what I do,” said she ;
“ My name it is Sweet Fantasy,
And there are many who seek for me
O'n Selsby Hill in the morning.”
With that she kissed me, free and full,
Free and full;
With that she kissed me, free and full,
On Selsby Hill in the morning ;
And I forgot, forgot again
The moons that wax, the stars that wane,
And I forgot both Joy and Pain
On Selsby Hill in the morning.
And then she laughed, and fled away,
And fled away ;
With that she laughed, and fled away,
On Selsby Hill in the morning.
False is she, and fickle is she,
Sweet Fantasy, Sweet Fantasy !
Ah ! but she kissed me, full and free,
On Selsby Hill in the morning !
England. Dora Wilcox.

About the sadde&t vessel that roams the
sea is the Italian sailing ship. It generally takes
120 days to go a distance that any other vessel
would cover in ,70; it is mostly old ; almost always
slovenly ; and it has a capacity for getting lost
that is incredible. Last week a Roman vessel
that had been out 36 days from Sydnev, and was
fondly supposed to be near South America with
its 2400 tons of coal, came ambling back. It
hadn’t even got as far as Maoriland. While
washing about and getting no forrarder, seven
men—three Englishmen, three Australians and a
Frenchman—went on strike, and the 11 Italians,
not feeling equal to the occasion, turned the craft
round and wallowed back to Sydney to get expert
opinion. It is to be hoped that South American
town isn’t in a hurry for its coal.

Rev. John Dixon, known to his flock as
‘‘plain John,” who has succeeded the now Bishop
of Bendigo as pastor of St. Philip’s, Sydney, re-
cently announced at the conclusion of his sermon
that he had found a Tattersall’s sweep ticket in
the Sunday-schoolroom, and the owner could have
the unclean thing by applying at the vestry.
When the rev. gentleman proceeded thither he
was surprised to find about three-fourths of his
congregation waiting for him. (The last part of
the story is pure invention.)

the respectable-looking shop-lifter struck a snag,
tiimj r W^en an elderly woman recovered
£2OO damages from a Sydney Universal Provider
for alleged wrongful arrest. The shop-owner’s
case wq,s that someone told him the elderly party
was seen taking goods off a counter. She carried
them wrapped in an old bag, and could not pro-
duce her receipts or identify the sales-lady who
served her. tale wa& that all* ladies behind
counters are as like as peas in a pod, and no old
female could be expected to tell t’other from which.
She said she had dropped her receipts somewhere,
and that she wrapped her purchases up like that
because the paper cover came undone and also
dropped somewhere. She said she was a respectable
female, who was suddenly arrested about l p.m.
ur ed in a cell at the police-station until
10.30 p.m., when she was bailed out. She added
that the police collared.even her.tram fare,’and
left her to walk home, and she -asked for. £4oo to
square it. Two juries have believed the widow’s
tale, and the second gave her £2OO.
A person who has been little before the
public of late broke out twice last week in
N.S.W. At Broken Hill, the lodger fired at the
family, and did little damage; at St. Peters
(Sydney) the husband shot at the lodger, and how
there is a lodger less in the world. The Broken
Hill man was really an ex-lodger ; he just 'called
in at his old residence, and fired generally,
without any serious results. The St. Peters
lodger had, a really bad grievance, but, being
dead, he doesn’t know it now. He appears to
have been a very old man, suffering under the
unjust suspicion that he was a gay young dog.
According to the story, a loafing Maori lander,
named Leith, married an Australian girl some
three years ago, but as he never supported her
she went back to her mother. The mother helped
to maintain herself by taking in an ancient
boarder, and last week the Maoriland man came
round with a gun, and extirpated this humble
source of revenue. He afterwards shot himself;
but that is a side issue. The real point involved
is that it seems almost equally dangerous to keep
a lodger and to be a lodger. Between the irritated
young boarder who shoots at his landlord, and
the infuriated husband who shoots the old
boarder, it looks as if it would be a great deal
safer to c' .np out. •

There is a strong movement in Adelaide
to bridge the long gap from Oodnadatta tq Pipe,.,
Creek, on the transcontinental railway—nabout
1100 miles, by a motor-Car service. An offer has
been made to send two cars through for £6OO.
They would survey a track, and then make >-a'n :
offer for regular trips. Albert Macdonald,'
who has cycled the whole wav, tells Ade-
laide Register that, except" for thick,
scrub, the only obstacle should be the sand—
but that is a terror. For 40 miles above Char-
lotte Waters there are sandhills every mile or so,
“ and some of them are as high as the G.P.0.”
By a mysterious but well-known natural law,
they slope off on the south side, but are abrupt on
the north. How a motor-car is to deal with them
is difficult to see. Going north it might climb up
and fall down. Going south it would apparently
have to fly up and roll down.

Sometimes he forgets to inquire, and sometimes

“Bend Or” : The snobbery of the sub-
urban person is a sad and curseful thing to
dwell op. A.humble artisan or civil servant
sticks up a one-eyed structure, jammed’close
against other like architectural ' microbes,
and forthwith he brands it with the sign of
the snob, “Windsor” or “St. Ives,” or
some such banal thing. In my immediate
neighborhood, which is a humble ope in
the bye-ways of Sydney, there are so many
“Cora Lynns” as to lead me to believe
that the original Cora was a lady of parts.
“Hampden,” “Mona,” and “Parsifal” are
a queer mixture. Then a fellow I know stuck
up over his single-fronted four-rooms and
“ ’ceteras ” the name “ Welbeck.” Amongst
the better class of dwellings, villas and
mansions, native names predominate. Touch-
ing the latter, I have a fact to tell.. A
fellow went over West in the nineties; arid
made a big and golden smite. Coming'
back he built a spacious and ornate 'villa.
“ replete,” as the house-agent says, all
modern conveniences, in a street w,here
dwelt the sleek and purse-fat. Bill wanted
a name for it. “Something OsStralian,” he
put it. He sought out a person who had the
reputation of being an aboriginal expert,
and who was also a great sinner. Shortly
there appeared a word over the gaudy en-
trance, the English of which
would have got anyone three months. Bill
got married, and in the fullness'of-time Mrs.
Bill increased the population by.a little Bili
and a Billess. BH triumphantly advertised
the fact in all the leading newspapers of the
continent, and gave the aboriginal,a y ddress,
in full. He got about 78 letters of remon-
strance, and now his house has no” name—
it is simply “No. 18, Fish-street.”

treats herself. The poor kid isPauntle-

young man who sells lace and feminine’

silk petticoats.
The Northern Territory, that land of
weird isolation, has been the refuge of many
Australians who were commonly supposed to
have fled to South America, and whose footmarks
were sought for in vain round Ohili and Bolivia, and
other far-off places. JVfanymust have perished
in the attempt to cross the great waterless plains
while on the trarnp to their intended place of
exile. Many got to their destination and lived
there for years without disclosing their identity,
and in the end died as naturally as one can in the
Territory. Many shot or hanged themselves
under assumed names, when the weary immensity
of South Australia’s white elephant became too
dreadful to be borne any longer. It is calculated
that 300 men have died in the. Territory,- mostly
by suicide, during the last 30 years, without leav-
ing a reliable name, or an address, or a trace of
their identity. They were Bill—just Bill of the
Territory. One suicided near the Victorian
River Depot, the.other day. He was supposed to
be a native of N.S.W., and to have once been a
citizen of some tonnage, till he “ did something.”
He left £l3 and a number of horses to help out
Premier Price’s surplus.
What to do with a.n ex-Governor was, one
time, a British Government puzzle. Mrs. J. R.
Wood, of Newcastle, provides a hint. That
esteemed lady has invented a new dog kennel—a
white-painted cedar thing for travelling dogs—
and has sent 11 of her terriers so housed to
England. Also she has obtained from Earl
Carrington a promise that he will personally look
after the consignment on its arrival.

The N.S.W. Supreme Court has granted a
new trial in the case; of the Sydney Harbor Trust
v. Wilson, in which some £440 of wharfage rates
are involved. Briefly, the Railway Department
imported some locomotives in little bits from
America, but would not take delivery until the
engines were on the railway and under steam. On
their arrival—in bits—in 1905, the Harbor Trust
claimed wharfage dues, not from the Railways,
which were to ultimately own the locos*, but from
a firm of middlemen that did the indenting. For
18 months a legal war has raged re the liability
of the Railway Commissioners as owners of the
engines. If the Railway Department was the
legal owner when the goods arrived, it is exempt
from wharfage dues. The State is in a curious
position.- Its costs already are more'than £440.
Should the Harbor Trust win, the middlemen will
simply tack the wharfage on to the cost of the
engines, and the State will lose. Should the Har-
bor Irust lose, the State loses the case equally,
and pays piles of costs on top of all. And, even
if at great expense a principle is established, the
State still stands to lose, as, in future contracts,
the middlemen will charge up all wharfage before-
hand. To allow; one State Department to waste
huge sums in suing another only means that the
State must pay the costs, and it is a fool of a
game. There should be a Minister with power to
arbitrate between Departments. • And, anyway,
why import the locomotives at all ?

As The Bulletin expected* the “romantic
story ” of the Russian lovers, whom an enraged
general was supposed to be chasing over the
earth, turns out to be mostly fairy tale and
neurotic temperament. It is said now on good
authority that Madame had been under treat-
ment for some time for nervous breakdown when
sbe eloped with her alleged captain, and that her
general held that young invalid person in such
profound contempt that his journey to New
York and Paris was solely to make arrangements
for his wife’s financial comfort and ease. She can
return to-morrow if she likes, he says. Mean-
time, however, though the bottom is out of the
“romance,” the couple are patching up the leaky
vessel and imagining that it’s brand new, and the
anaemic captain delights in seeing spies behind
every lamp-post.. What he really needs is not a
sweetheart so much as a doctor and a month or
t-wo on a- dairy farm.
WHO’D LACK A LASS?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out on the drowsy river
Rose-gold and love-light quiver...
Who’ll loiter on the path to bliss
A-down the river’s brim ?
Gay hills and gullies brighten,
Sweet honeysuckles whiten...
O who would lack a lass to kiss
And wander far with him ?
Dear eyes and wind-blown tresses,
Soft summer idlenesses,
Serene, heart-warm philosophies—
No more I wish, not I,
Ere wintry tear-fall numb me,
And death-chfll overcome me,
But teach a lass the mysteries
Of love and tear and sigh !
Too brief, too blind, our reign, love,
Yet were yye .wondrous vain, love ;
In flowery meads elves laid our couch,
Stars were our tiring-maids...
Now violet-heart is riven,
Brown summer-leafage driven ;
No witnesses remain to vouch
For love, save hapless shades.
When dream-blooms droop and wither,
All wind-blown, hither, thither,
And soughing low the night-wind’s is
A drearsome sound to hear,
And haunted night-birds flutter
Against the secret shutter...
O who would lack a lass to kiss,
A dark-eyed damsel dear ?
N.B.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Chairman Robert Harper went att lilcb or two
deep into the affairs of the Assets Trust for the first
time at the meeting of the Commercial Bank Of Aus-
tralia. Five years ago the then flew general manager re-
valued the assets of the Trust alone at £1,800;000 ; its
liabilities were £3,334,903, ieaving a deficiency of
£1;534,903; Since then “ properties Which Wete valued
at £479;566 have been sold for £503;752; thus realising a
surplus of £24,186 over valuation.” The assets of the
Trust ate now valued at £l;33o,ooo—that is, £470,000
less than irt 1901. So the Trust has sold £479,566 of
assets (at a profit of £24,186 oVer valuation!, ahd has re-
duced the Valuation of assets by only £470,000. It has
apparently written’ up the remaining assets a trifle
(£9566) ; but what has become of the £24,186 of profit ?
No doubt, as the whole business is based on the general
manager’s estimate, the g.m. is entitled to build
dp a reserve fund to protect the balance of
his estimate ; if he has done better than he
expected over some assets, he is entitled to
have something up his sleeve as a set-off to possible
deficits when he comes to sell the unsold assets. From
time to time, however, those interested are entitled to
know how that suspense account is looking. It is satis-
factory that the estimate so far has panned out so well.
Viewing the Trust and the Bank as one combined con-
cern, in five years, apart from this £24,186 in suspense,
and accepting- the general manager’s estimates, the net
deficit has been reduced by £356,065, of which £208,565
represents profit and £147,500 forfeited capital. There
still remains £1,151,219 of a deficit (still, cßmbihing Trust
fthd Bank figures); and if it has taken five years to wipe
mlt £208,565 of deficit out of profits, it would take 27 to
Wipe out £1,151,210 at the same rate. “A” deposits have,
noWeVer, been got rid of, and “B” deposits (quoted at
I7s.)are being bought back ; moreover, the Bank’s profits
from new business are increasing fairly rapidly (though
against that must be put the possibility of an'increased
dividend to preference shareholders) ; so the process of
wiping out the ghastly deficit is likely to be expedited.
The situation at one time looked desperate, but there is a
gleam of light ahead.

1906 .. 205,910 46,757 75,251 849 83,053
This office works on different lines from those
of other Australian offices; and you could argue from
here to Cape York on the question whether it is the
management or the principle that is responsible for the
unquestionably fine results it is beginning to snow. The
management, being modest, asserts that the success lies
with the principle. The co. is a proprietary concern.
But its success is due to two facts, among others.
(1) The proprietors, besides being able men, are still
mostly the managers of the concern, in some capacity or
other. (2) While it thus gets, so far, the full advantages
of the proprietary system, because so far it is prac-
tically a profit sharing system, the evils of the proprietary
system are whittled away; for dividends are limited,
and the co. can’t grab more than than one-fifth of the
profits out of which bonuses are paid. In return for the
concession of a share in the profits, the co. guarantees
that the expenses (in the ordinary branch) shall not
exceed 16 per cent, of the total income. The weak point
in the business is that, while the policy-holders put up
the vast hulk of the money—in this ease, lumping the
two branches together, £1,782,609—and the shareholders
put up only £20,090, the shareholders run the whole
business. But the weak point under the mutual system is
that, though nominally the policy-holders manage the
whoie business, in actual practice “ the office ” (or, as the
Americans call it, “ the administration ”) wins every
time. It should not be so, but it is : even such scandals
as there have been in America were not sufficient to
neutralise the inherent advantages of “ the office ” in any
really serious contest. The success of the Citizens’
certainly doesn’t mean, though, that proprietary offices
have only to put up a brass-plate in order to succeed :
they have only a hope of succeeding, in fact, when the
proprietary element in the undertaking has its claws cut
or hidden. And if they are only hidden it can’t succeed
for long. The Citizens’ founders, wanting their office to
last, showed their wisdom in nothing more than in
deliberately cutting the claws at the very outset: the
pruning was part of the constitution.
The excellent organisation of the Citizens’
office, however caused, shows up in the rapid decrease in
the expense rate :

It is all right to look at it that way from the point of
view of the staff ; but the policy-holder may look at it
differently. The claim is that the policy-holders get a
more efficient service on account of the principles on
which the office is run ; and they have to pay one-fifth of
the profits to get that efficiency. So that one-fifth must
be added to the cost. The actuaries’ report says there is
a surplus of £50,384 available, of which £49,301 is to be
divided “in accordance with the articles of association ”
One-fifth of that, or £9860, must, therefore be
added to the expenses in the ordinary branch. Taking
that in with the other expenses, the rate is
£l6 os. lOd. per cent, of total revenue. That is the
total cost to the policy-holders ; and only one office in
Australia shows up better. Industriarbusiness is not
affected by the proprietors’bonus; it has to carry the
dividend, however, and that amounts to £2OOO a'year
Yet, including that, the total expenses (£36 Is lOd p c )
show a far lower rate than any other Australian office
and; it is claimed; show' the lowest industrial rate in the
World. It is something for an office to be proud of. In
both Branches the dost of hew business is very small.
Take the ordinary. Add to expenses (agaih; by the way;
why are they described as “ net ”?) the amodnt written
off, £396. Charge renewal premiums with 5 p:c. of their
total for expenses, and charge the amount received for
Annuities with 3 p.c. ; then the balance, representing
the price of new business, will show that the latter cost
only 76 - 6 p.c. of the total first-year premiums. In the
previous year the cost on that basis was 79 p.c. No
other Australian office got within 30 p.c. of that.
Yet the bonuses at present are not very large.
There is £49,301 to be divided, of which policy-holders
get £39,441. The profit-sharing policies pay premiums
amounting to £219,223 ; so the available bonuses equal
18 per cent, of the premiums. But, as a partial set-off to
that, it has to be noted that the valuation is conducted
on the assumption that as little as 2J per cent, will be
earhed—the lowest rate assumed by any office in Aus-
tralia; This has the effect of reducing the present
bonuses; bdt it gives ah assurahce of large bonuses in the
future. And, further, the Citizens’goes for safety rather
than high interest, as this distribution of the assets
shows

worth the money ; for, after all, these 25,000 shares, though
they don’t rank for dividend this past half-year, never-

over the even £l. Provision has now been made for all
losses ascertained up to November 30th ; presuming that
