The Silverton Tramway Co., which owns the
30-odd miles of railway between Broken Hill and the
world: —

It is a tribute alike to this company’s management and
the wealth of Broken Hill that there is hardly any kick-
ing, even though 48* p.c. is paid on the capital put into
the concern. Only when the metal markets slump at an
inconvenient time is there talk of a new connecting link,
and then it is generally compromised by a freight reduc-
tion of decimal 17 or so of a penny per ton. So far as the
State of N.S.W. is concerned, there isn’t much hope ; it
wouldn’t build the line in the first place, because it would
rather let Broken Hill starve than help it reach its
natural seaport, seeing that that seaport is in another
State ; so it gave this co. a concession, and the promoters
got a clause put in the Bill that, if the State at any time
took the line over, it should be valued on the basis' of the
co.’s profits; so if the State did take the line over, it
would either have to charge present rates or thereabouts,
or make a heavy loss. It is on the basis of profits that
the market values the concern ; and, instead of valuing it
at the £122,105 originally put into the business
(stock was subsequently watered up to £197,806),
it puts a price of £998,920 upon it (£5 Is. per share)—
a remarkable spring from the £534,076 (£2 14s. per
share) at which it stood at the end of 1904.
Present dividend is Is. 6d. per share per quarter;
that gives an investment return of a shade under 6 per
cent. There is, however, the possibility of a larger divi-
dend ; for both lead mills and zinc works are increasing
their output, and that means more freight. Out of tin-
disclosed profits in good times the co. has spent large sums
in “ maintenance and repairs ” ; in many cases this has
meant renewals and additions. Meantime, large amounts
have been written off ; so that the whole fixed assets,
though they have been latgely added to in recent years,
Plow standi at only £137,759, and rdlling-Stock and plant
Stand at £50,390. Five years ago,- ofie item stood at
£155,795 and the other at £58,650. Ih the fivfe years, in
Spite of the additions that have been made and the heavy
Expenditure on renewals, the surplus of liquid assets over
liabilities has increased from £58,146 to £96,-517. That is
to say, after paying dividend, and spending large sums
dn renewals and additions, including £32,000 for new
tails and sleepers ih 1904 alone, the co. is £38,371 to the
good in five years. Of present assets, £79,144 is repre-
sented by public securities and bank deposits. That is
the brig ht side. On the other are some risks. The mines
must end some day, and the use of the railway will prac-
tically end with them. On the other hand, if the mines
become very large, low-grade propositions, having to look
at every ha’penny, they may build a line of their own.
And in that connection it has to be remembered that
whereas the big Broket) Hill men were at one time prac-
tically the Tramway Co., the ownership of Broken Hill
is now' passing into other hands, and the control will pass
with it. What is happening to the Water Co. may there-
fore happen to the Tramway Co.

This is one of the few Australian trustee cos.- that have
not mended their w-ays when they needed mending, and
which still give the least possible information about
their proceedings. The idea seems to be that they
are really too respectable to need to give any informa-
tion. It was just the same Way with those venerable
firms of family solicitors whom trustee cos. were designed
to supplant. Those venerable firms traded on their
respectability, and the public pretty often fell in. Some
trustee cos. and a good many others seem to have the
idea that all the respectability in the w'orld lives behind
closed doors w'ith the lights turned low; and it doesn’t
by a long chalk. This co. certainly says what its assets
are; though, since it lumps all its receipts under one
bead, it isn’t possible to tell what the assets are earning.
Also it doesn’t tell the gross value of estates in hand.
A shareholder asked about that at the meeting the other
day,- and the chairman said he didn’t see how
the information ] would benefit the shareholders.
Apparently, therefore, in the chairman's opinion,
it pays the shareholders to keep the public
in the dark about certain things; and that’s
a more severe thing than this paper is prepared to say.
The profits of the co. are erratic (how much of the
corpus commission is taken Credit for when an estate
comes in it is impossible to say); for the last half-year
they were at the rate of £lO 12s.- lid. per cent, per annum
on capital and reserves ; for the past year, £lll2s. 7d,
per cent. It is a very fair return ; after showing it, The
Bulletin can quite understand the management making
disrespectful motions towards any criticism of its
methods. Still, with better methods, it might make 15
percent. And, anyhow', the shareholders are not the
only' persons to be considered.

When half the capital was written off, properties were
written dowm to correspond, and the “profits on pro-
perty sold” simply means that the loss then provided for
out of capital has not been quite realised. Since
January, 1901, as a list that follows will show, fore-
closed properties have been reduced by £15,954, and the
profits have been £6306. But some other properties
may have fallen into possession during the past six years,
which would mean that more than £15,954 (book value)
of properties have been sold, and that would reduce the
proportion of profit or recovery per cent. Further, it
isn’t certain that the properties sold are an average
sample. Still, on that point, the figures are highly satis-
factory. In the half-year to Jan,, 1901, net rents of fore-
closed properties show'ed a return equal to £5 4s. per
cent, per anuum of book value. For the past half-year
they show £5 19s. 6d. per cent, per annum. Presuming
that about the same proportion of gross rents has been
spent on repairs, &c., this would hint that the assets that
are left are a more profitable lot even than those that are
gone. As for some other details: —

This co.’s accounts are presented very fully and fairly.
Even full and fair accounts published by a gas co. don’t,
however, let the shareholder and investor know whether
enough provision is being made for depreciation of mains,
services, &c. The plant, mains, and services have
a book value of £23,171. Last year something much
above the average was written off out of profits,
yet it amounts to under 2 per cent, for the year. In
addition, repairs and renewals are paid out of revenue;
hut they are a small matter, amounting to under £3O per
half-year- Still, it is an old-established concern, dating
from 1866, and it is run on economical lines, and the
stitch that saves nine has never been neglected, so far as
can be seen. Before paying the current dividend the
co.’s liquid assets, apart from stock, exceeded liabilities
by £5005,

Rockhampton Gas and Coke Co. (Q.):—
Dividends, Writ-
ten De-
Profits. Pref. Ord. Total. Off. licit.

June,’o4 1501 9,, 116 706 116
Dec., ’O4 1666 9 „ 2* „ 719 831 -
June,’os 1237 9,, 116 1237 116
Dec., ’O5 1337 9 „ 2* „ 717 504
June,’o6 1573 Nil 1573
Dec., ’O6 1514 'lB „ 2* p.c. 833 681
This is one of the most valuable lighting cos. in Aus-
tralia —as the shocking example. For years it paid ex-
cessive dividends that didn’t look excessive on the surface.
The fact was that the plant, into which the capital had been
put, was being used up, and wasn’t being replaced out of
profits. Then, at last, a cold day arrived when the audi-
tors declined to sign the balance-sheet, on theground that
the assets weren’t worth what they purported to be. It
became necessary to either write off a great deal of the
capital as lost (it had actually been paid away to the
shareholders in excessive dividends) and call up more
capital to put the assets in order, or to knock off divi-
dends, and out of the profits either replace worn-out
assets or pay off debentures. As far as the demands of
the preference capital will let them, this.is being done;
the ordinary dividend is cut down till it equals lj per
cent, per annum —that is, a div. at the rate of 2*
per cent, per annum is paid every other half-year.
The 18 per cent, for the past half-year is equal,
of course, to the old 9 per cent, for the full year.
The debenture debt has been reduced to £lB,OOO, and, as
liquid assets exceed current liabilities by £1427 before
paying dividend, another £lOOO will come off this half-
year. How much more the plant needs to be written
down is not ascertainable from the accounts:
but as the electric light department showed a working
profit of only £l5 last half-year, and the plant, mains and
fittings still stand in at £9833, there is evidently a long
way to go yet.

» * * *
Ivanhoe South Extended, Kalgoorlie, after
grafting since 1899 as a Bull-Oohen concern, has struck a
lode in the shaft at 2000 ft., carrying visible gold. This is
supposed to be an entirely new make, and may yet save
the co. A shade over two years ago the co. had £19,0C0
in hand ; it has since then sunk 600 ft., but should still
have a fair amount of money to go on with. Some time
ago pay ore was cut by diamond drilling at 1477 ft. and
again at 1900 ft., while a rich vein was cut in the shaft at
1320 ft. This mine is controlled by the Horseshoe crowd,
and is economically managed by J. W. Sutherland, of that
mine. The adjoining leases on the south were bought in
1903 by the Horseshoe from Hannan’s Golden Treasure for
£14,600. They,consist of 57 acres, and have been used as
a tailings dumping ground, but now have a prospective
mining value. The Extended intends opening out at
2000 ft., and the result will be followed with interest.
*** . *
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie, is now equipped
with a 10-head mill, rock breaker, self feeders, two
Wilfle.v concentrators and cyanide plant. A few details
require completing, but the plant is nearly ready to
verify, or falsify, Manager Hooking’s optimistic predic-
tions. Crushing was expected to start by February 1,
but there have been the usual delays incidental to erect-
ing machinery. According to Manager Hocking, there
are thousands of tons of ore in sight, worth 9dwt.
fine gold bv assay. The same statement was made
by ex-Manager Johnson and Tom Hewitson, but test
crushirigs returned less than 3dwt. The gold is so fine,
and the ore so slimy, that a very large percentage is lost
in the slimes. Filter presses cannot be employed eco-
nomically on small mines, as three have to be installed,
and with two men operating them in turns, they can
work more slimes than a 10-head or 20-head mill cafl pro-
duce. A Ridgeway filter press might overcome the
difficulty.
* * * *
Boulder No. 1 (W.A.), which lias not been
worked by the co. for about eight years, and has been in
the hands of tributors for the past three years, is looking
up. In a shaft on the east boundary, near the Hainault,
a party of five tributors carried uown the hole to 200 ft.,
and started opening up a large sulphide lode. The first
small parcel gave 41dwt., including everything, ana a
second parcel is expected to do better still. This loae
was never worked by the co., whose main workings are
100yds. west. A second party of tributors is working tne
oxidised ore at 60ft. from the same shaft, but it only giv p s
them wages. Something definite should he known pf tne
prospects of the new discovery in the course of a montn,
and may quite alter the co.’s prospects. All the expert
have insisted that gold values to the north were cut ofi
by a crosscourse north of the Ivanhoe ; but this has bee
disproved by the Hamilton shaft on the Great Bouldei,
where a new mine has been opened up below bOTO-.
although nothing could be found at shallower levels.

The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving- by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These tw.» plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth’and ability] Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. and J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert and Co., of London, the largest firm in the
world for the nianufacture of transport appliances.*

* * * *
The Charlotte Plains Consolidated G. M.
Co.’s new Havillah Shaft has just installed a high
lift pump to make all necessary provision against
any increase in the volume of water now that they
are opening up large areas of wash dirt. This instal-
lation is in addition to the large three throw pump
which has given such high efficiency and such constant
service since its inception. The new pump is of the high
lift turbine type, electrically driven, and is capable of
lifting 2,016,000 gallons of water in 24 hours against a
total head of 350 feet. The mechanical efficiency of the
pump is guaranteed at 71'5 per cent, and all repairs and
replacements apart from undue wear are likewise
guaranteed for twelve months. The pump has four
runners and four vanes made from best bronze and the
shaft is attached by means of a flexible coupling to a
Ganz three phase motor 440 volts pressure, 60 cycles
capable of giving 207 h.p. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have executed this con-
tract. This Company has had the manufacture of most
of the big volume deep alluvial lead pumping schemes in
Victoria and has met in each with unqualified success.* [a]

of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for malting-boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,

Bft. The next 10 days were spent sinking a 20ft. sump, or
well, and no news being mostly bad news in mining, bears
took the opportunity to bang the shares. Manager
Henderson is confident that, when he opens out, values
will be found as good as ever. I\ ssimists judge by the
adjoining Norseman mine, which was shut down in 1902
because values at 500 ft. were poor where the reef was
cut, and work was abandoned without any driving being
done. The Govt. Geologist pointed out that the gold
only occurred in shoots; but as the co. was losing
£24 6s. 6d, per month, or 2£d. per ton on the 2300 tons
treated monthly, worth 465. per ton, it threw up the
sponge. As dynamite and cyanide are about half the
price they were in 1902, the mine will probably be re-
opened, especially in view of the projected railway.
* * * *
Greenbushes tin field (W.A.) has enjoyed a
prosperous year. The output of black tin has increased
to 778 tons, valued at £78,737, for 1906, as against 638
tons, valued at £52,400, in 1905. The number of men
employed has increased from 240 to 515. Outlook is
promising for a further increase, as manager Moss, of
the Kalgurli, is erecting a sluicing plant.
An important co., with a capital of £150,000,
and bushels more behind that, has been registered in
N.S.W. to erect electrolytic refinery works and handle
c implex ores, hitherto little sought after by Australian
smelting works. The concern is practically a fusion of
the interests of the Mt. Morgan Co. and Aron Hirsch and
Sohn, whose Australian agent, Francis H. Snow, handles
the Mt. Morgan output at present and ships it to the Dela-
mar works in America. If the Barron Falls could be shifted
from the back of Cairns further south along the coast,
there is no doubt where the works would be ; hut as
there’s some difficulty about shifting the Falls, the loca-
tion of the works is an unsettled point. Captain
Kichard, general manager of Mt. Morgan, has made a
hurried departure from Australia, to find out the things
in America and Europe that the co. needs to know. He
will act as technical adviser to the co.
* * * *
The Broken Hill Prop, made £345,620 in the
co.’s half-year to the end of November, of which it wrote
off £31,336, leaving £314,284 as net profit. The amount
written off was not quite equal to what was spent on new
plant. Unless some secret reserve has been made to pro-
vide for an adverse verdict in the Potter case, which
hadn’t been decided when the accounts were made up,
this includes all profit from zinc as well as from silver and
lead. Formerly £60,000 was provided as a reserve
against a slump in the zinc market before average prices
of shipments are adjusted ; and this reserve has not been
increased during the half-year. The quantity of ore
handled was 298,360 tons ; so that, including the profit
from zinc, the total net profit was £1 Is. Id. per ton.
This was with silver bringing 2s. 9 47-64 d. per oz. net to
the co. (that is, after bearing realisation charges), and lead
£l6 ss. sd. per ton. The ore reserves on November 30
were estimated at 3,000,000 tons, as against 3,200,000 tons
in May, 1905 ; since the latter date, 828,000 tons have
been extracted. At £1 Is. Id. per ton, the 3,000,000 tons
in the mine would be worth £3,162,500, or £3 6s. per
share. Fifty per cent, added to that for developments
would, on the same basis as to metal-values, bring up the
total value of the mine to £4 19s. per share. To that
must be added 12s. 3d. per share to represent liquid
assets in excess of liabilities. But there is a tremendous
risk in accepting any such basis of metal values. •As said '
just now, the net prices for the half-year were 2s. 9 47-64 d. ,
for silver, and £l6 ss. sd. for lead. For the previous 12
half-years the average of theprices realised was 2s. 4 39-64 d.
and £ll 16s. 3d. At those prices the profit would be
£133,250 instead of £314,284.
There is very little doubt that, conditions as to
supply and demand being equal, average prices for silver •
and lead are likely to be higher for some years to cpme
than they have been in the past. The increase in gold-
production (which almost resurrects that ancient and
dreary old nuisance, the Medium of Exchange) will see to
that. But the extra price isn’t going to be all profit. If
everything (including silver and lead) are dearer, labor
will cost more ; ditto machinery, timber, &c. And, any-
how, the increase has been in progress during the past
six years covered by the preceding remarks. This in-
crease in gold production, that has been so often exhibited
lately, didn’t commence yesterday ; it was in full blast
seven years ago, when both the Rand and Westralia were
in full fig, and at neither of those places have :the
promises of seven years ago been sustained. The
average prices of the past six years may not be an in-
fallible guide to the future ; hut they are a mighty lot
better than the whispers of men with stocks to sell.
Of course there’s the zinc. But isn’t “the
zinc” about played out? When the boom was being
whipped up (it is easing off a hit now) “the zinc” was
mentioned in much the same awesome way as the old
lady mentioned Mesopotamia. The dumps of old tail-
ings were supposed to be worth untold millions. At
the best, they found firm buyers at from 6s. down
to 3s. per ton ; and the cos. that sold at 55., so far
as experience has gone, have had all the best
of it. What profit the B.H. Prop, is making out of the
production of zinc concentrates it doesn’t mention ; but
whatever it is, it is included in the calculations above.
More than that, it is extremely doubtful whether the co.
is producing anything like 121,895 tons of tailings (the
quantity put through last half-year), of a quality that
can be handled at present with appreciable profit. What
hasn’t soaked in .yet is that there are tailings and tail-
ings ; and the B.H. Prop, isn’t putting stuff through
haphazard.
* * » <
In 1900 the ore reserves in the Broken Hill
Prop, were estimated at 4,588,000 tons. They have now
got down to 3,000,000 tons. Assuming the estimates to
have been made on the same basis, and to be correct,
about 65 tons of ore have been developed for every 100
tons extracted. The outlook is, however, less satisfac-
tory than that, on account of the failure of the northern
end of the mine and the shortening of the shoot at the
south end as the workings get deeper.
* * * *
These Broken Hill cos. handle zinc figures as
though they were loaded—and perhaps they are. Note,
for instance, how the B.H. Prop., after describing the
“more satisfactory results” from the very latest plant
reconstruction, tells in the monthly report that 5626 tons
of zinc concentrates were produced, hut doesn’t-mention
the quantity of zinc-bearing material treated. In that
connection, a “ colonial expert,” writing in London
Financial Times, says that only 1,000,000 of the 2,000,000
tons of material at surface are rich enough to warrant
treatment. The way in which these dumps have oeen
referred to, just as though they were of even quality and
character, is, of course, ridiculous. The proportion of
the dumps which can be treated depends on the future
of zinc and the progress of zinc-metallurgy. The’ stuff so
far treated has been almost without exception what has
been reckoned the most suitable ; and it is pretty certain
no large quantity even of that has yielded more than-
7s. 6d. per ton profit.
■* * < *
With the Broken Hill Block 14 lode at 600 ft.
manager Hocking is in the position of a thimble-and-pea
man. But it isn’t his fault. A lode that cuts out into a
clean face of mullock one day and shows 10ft. of ore the
next is a born juggler on its own account. There is too
much mullock in the south end of the lode for anyone to
build hopes on, but there may be something going north.
It is one of the confidence-trick lodes common to the
stretch of country between Block 13, of the B.H. Prop.,
and the Junction, and in this instance it seems to be
giving one of its most bewildering performances. It isn’t
to be relied on—that’s all that can be said about it for
certain.
* * *
The South Blocks, Broken Hill, is going to be
a nice little mine. For immediate requirements there is
an attractive lode of free milling sulphides, 700 ft. in
length, 18ft. in average width, and offering about 200 ft.
of hacks. Much of this ore will be dealt with by the jigs,
for it is nearly pure galena. Afterwards, there are the
rich shoots of ore coming in from the South mine at the
lower levels to be searched for. The South Blocks mine
is at the end of the Broken Hill outcrop ; and with it the
well-defined, settled ore bodies seem to end also—unless
they appear again at depth. The A.B.H. Consols, the
next claim along, has no lode anyway like its neighbor’s;
and further south, on the White Leads flat, traces of a
lode at average depth become more rare. Diamond
drilling at the South Blocks Extended, three-quarters of
a mile from the South Blocks hill, has also failed to dis-
close anything of importance.
* * * *
Queen Bee (N.S.W.) has given ex-chairman
John Fox an option over the property. Reporting it,
the bungling dailies referred to it as a cash offer for the
mine, which it isn’t. Fox’s party is to put up £lOOO at'
once ; that buys an option for a month. A further £3125
buys an option for three months more. Another offer
not for an option, but for straight-out purchase, was men-
tioned at the meeting; but as the meeting was called to
deal with Fox’s offer, the real strength of the other
alleged offer couldn’t be tested.
* * * *
There is something pathetic about Ferd.
Kayser’s opening sentence in the last Mt. Bischoff (Tas.)
report:—“ This is the first time during nearly 32 years’
service that I cannot lay before you a report pleasing to
all concerned and satisfactory to myself.” What a long
vista of success ! Misfortune becomes such a stranger
that when he knocks at the door, his tattered coat
often excites resentment. But he is persistent,
and Ferd. Kayser knows it, and talks sensibly: —
“Since the brown face has been worked out, and the
sudden and unexpected collapse of No. 3 bench, unfore-
seen difficulties have presented themselves, which, in the
near future, compel us to face a still further reduced
output. ... It is to be hoped that some of the opera-
tions will turn out satisfactory, and enable us to recover
solne lost ground ; but since the main deposit of the
mine has failed, and nothing promising has been met in
the main ground, it is doubtful if the old prosperous
times will return.” This means that the grandest tin
mine in the world of recent years is tottering. Over
65,832 tons of black tin is its record, and over £2,000,000
paid in dividends. What a God-send it was that the
barren outcrop was cut out of the Van Diemen’s Land
Co.’s ground by the old-time surveyors, who did not
realise the wealth hidden beneath the brown capping !
This excision meant that wealth went to the many in
Tasmania, and'not to the few fatmen at ’Ome. Of course
there is life in the mine yet, but the difficulty is to tell
how much, owing to the shortening of reserves. At the
different faces there is a good deal of low-grade dirt, but
the country seems to be closing in at different points.
The co. ought to have the ore formations measured up,
so as to give some idea of the ore reserves. Directors
talk about using their cash reserve of £50,000 to buy a
new mine, but they are as slow off the mark as the
Curtin-Davis board. Following figures give results of
the past three half-years’ work
Tin. Dividend. Costs per Ton.
Dec., 1905 ..£42,279, £30,000 £0 6 7£
June, 1906 .. 50,023 36,000 0 6 10£
Dec., 1906 .. 48,190 27,000 0 6 10£
The like again is most improbable.
* » * *
Chillagoe (N.Q.) is having a change of luck.
It got off 50,000 of its Mungana shares at 11s. 6d.,
and the buyers have the right to come for another
50,000. It is a good and proper sale, which will help the
co. lift a little of its debt. As for these Mungana shares,
which no doubt will soon get on the market, it will be
just as well to remember that the co.’s past results are
none too good a guide to the future, for the Chillagoe Co.
has done the Mungana’s smelting on what looks like ridi-
culously easy terms. In short, Chillagoe, owning four-fifths
of Mungana, appears to have been feeding Mungana’s
finances, possibly not at its own expense, but certainly
by taking ore on terms that are not likely to be continued
for long. Mungana has two good mines, Girofla and Lady
Jane. Girofla has lately developed an unexpected patch
of very good ore, but it is only a patch. In addition to
Obillagoe’s success in the matter just mentioned, it has
had its new share issue over-applied for;, and the
Etheridge railway construction (in connection with
which the issue was made) seems all plain sailing. .
E. A. Weinberg reckons the mine traffic on the
Chillagoe Co.’s new Etheridge railway will be 45,000 tons
a year. If so, it means a big spurt for Etheridge, for
last year the field produced only 10,457 tons of ore. The
idea is, of course, that Chillagoe will get all the smelting
to do ; but it is quite probable that the best of the mines
will do their own smelting, like O.K. and Mt. Molloy.
The railway will, in any case, facilitate haulage of coke
and return loading of metals, and, if prices keep up,
there will be a large traffic. Weinberg points out that
the gold reefs get mineralised at depth, and require con-
centrating and smelting. But whatever the Etheridge
can produce, one fact sticks out—the German and other
foreign smelters will run Chillagoe all they know for cus-
toms trade. The foreign smelting firms have had repre-
sentatives all over N. Queensland, and shipments of
copper, tip, and lead ore from Cairns are already con-
siderable.

It took 102 separate parcels to make the total
of 16,426 tons treated at Charters Towers during January.
The total value was £60,356, an average of £3 Iss. sd. per
ton. January of last year saw 14,943 tons put through
for £58,594, equal to £3 18s. sd. per ton. Only four
mines, Mills’, Extended, Block and Wyndham, and St.
George, turned out over 1000 tons for the month ; and of
these four the last two are in a rather doubtful way.
Principal producers were : Mills’, 3258 .tons, 1068 oz. ;
Block and Wyndham, 1033 tons, 2940 z. ; Brilliant Cen-
tral, 720 tons, 5450 z. ; Pfeiffer’s D.D., 657 tons, 588 oz. ;
Day Dawn P.C., 198 tons, lOOoz. ; St. George, 1628 tons,
780 oz. ; Stockholm, 113 tons, lOOoz. ; New Queen, 246
tons, 114 oz. ; Extended, 4969 tons, 14600 z. ; Marshall’s,
397 tons, 47Qoz. ; Victory, 362 tons, 6090 z. ; Queen Cross,
456 tons, 2770 z. ; Carrington’s, 568 tons, 3850 z. These
figures are mill gold only. The other 89 parcels were
under 100 tons each.
* • * *
More legitimate prospecting is being done in
Charters Towers’ mines at present than ever was done at
one time before. Certainly the field needs it all, for the
condition of many of the mines was bad. .Even now, out
of the whole bundle of stragglers, hardly one, except
Pfeiffer’s, knows for certain what it is, looking for, and
where it may reasonably expect to get it.
•* * ' * ' *
Carrington’s United, outside Charters Towers,
paid £2875 for January. This show, after much “ engi-
neering,” stands out as the most attractive property on
or around Charters Towers. The 558 tons treated-for
January averaged £6 16s. 4d. per ton. Information con-
cerning the mine (26 miles out) is, however, only handed
to shareholders on a long-handled shovel. There were
300 ba!gs of copper ore despatched to the smelters; but no
space was available in the report to mention the value
of the stuff inside the bags. Still it’s gratifying to know
that the bags were strong and securely sewn. .
* * * *
If it’s the name of boss “caller ” that the Queen
Deep, Charters Towers; is after, it will have to hustle to
beat the Vesuvius. The co. keeps calling and plodding
along trying to make one decent mine but of two or three
old ones. The show is situated outside' the fashionable
area, and receives very little'attention. There was
supposed to be something dazzling about it when it was
floated ; now that the old workings have been drained of
water, it is seen that it was only the prospectus that
dazzled. ,
* * * *
Queen Cross, Charters Towers, has arrived at
bed rock. The top workings, where the voluptuous
dividends came from; are to be entrusted to the gentle
tributor. The co: itself is going to carry on operations at
the 1700 ft. formation, where there is 6in. of reef showing
in the levels.
» * * *
The Mexican’s star has dipped beyond the
horizon at Charters Towers. The crosscut has been dis-
continued at 500 ft. They missed the Mills’United reef,
and went on until they met the old Mexican formation.
Levels have been started, and are out 50ft. east and west
in a formation that was never guilty of gold, though it
may have been accused of it now and then.
* * * *
Victorian brown coal has been dangled before
Victorian investors for the past quarter of a century.
But David Syme’s preaching hasn’t quite convinced any-
one, any more than his banging of the big dram over the
Outtrim coal-mine did. Still, David put up his own
money at the Outtrim ; and there is no proof that he has
done this with the brown coal. That is, unless Croker
has lured him into the Altona project that Bewick,
Moreing and Co. are reported to have taken in hand.
That project needs very careful watphing to see
that the business does not pass into the hands
of London promoters, old land-boomers, and the like.
The idea seems to be to have a cut at raising coal to
supply power-gas for'the electrification of the railways.
If that is good enough for private enterprise, it is good
enough for the State which owns -the..railways, and the
State must not allow any private finger in its pie where
its big railway service is concerned. The warning ought to
be given so that there shall be no crying about a breach
of faith. This warning can be given with the greater
reason now that another coal project is in the air—the
Werribee Park scheme, some 12 miles distant from Mel-
bourne. Brown coal 140 ft. thick is said to exist out
from Altona. If it is any good, the Government ought to
pounce on a big block of unworked ground; if it doesn’t,
it will be squeezed sooner or later.
« * * *
Jorgensen’s, at Stirling (Vic.), is an instance of
the way things ought not to go. The co. consisted
mainly of a smart Ballarat crowd, who found that, when
they got off their own dunghill, they were only' a very
feeble cockerel. The original Jorgensen had a mine on
the Haunted Stream, where he delved hard and with
moderate success. Then came from Ballarat certain
wise men, among them Holst, Buchanan, and Sewell.
They probably envied the Northerner the mUrmur of the
stream ; anyhow, they bought.' But the luck left with
Jorgensen. Even when Jorgensen went back, it made no
difference ; so now a heavy overdraft hangs round the
neck of Jorgensen’s co.—a weary weight to a sad-eyed
Ballarat crowd.
* ■» * *
Cassilis (Vic.) emerges from its retreat to once
more show that it is still a mine, and can build up profits
without any flam or show. Co.’s great trouble is to keep
up ore reserves. This can be seen from the fact that,
while £4054 was netted last half-year, £3940, or nearly £
for £, was mopped up in mine development, to say
nothing of what went as a preliminary in the electric-
power scheme. Heavy development work must still be
looked for, owing to the in-and-out nature of the
ore shoots—short formations, shaped like a string of
sausages. Last half-year 6250 tons went to the mill, and
averaged a shade under loz. Costs worked out about as
follows : Breaking, &c., 245. 9Jd. ; transport, 7Jd.; mill-
ing, maintenance, &c., 20s. 3d. ; total 455. 7fd. per ton.
To this lias to be added development, 12s. 7Jd. per ton.
Mine has in sight 1] ,000 tons, or under a year’s work ; but
it has its shaft 150 ft. below its boti om level, otherwise the
position would be very blue. It has to carry out a big
electric scheme, and wants every penny it can raise.
Without dividends, it is a fair gamble; with dividends
and without more reserves, it ought, to the ordinary
shareholder, to be a case of cut and run.
* * * *
Bonny Caledonia, Pride of Victoria’s Warran-
dyt.e,! .But where are its clansmen? Fallen a prey, alas,
to bears or to bulls: Caledonia has its own tartan
muddied and its claymore dulled. To drop that sort of
thing, the ore it produced last half-year came almost
entirely from the north and south slopes above No. 2
level. * No. 3 level shows fair stone ; “ payable” is the
manager’s expression. Total length of shoot, about
250 ft. This is the mine reserve, with the stone left at
the No. 2 level. Financially, the co. ought.to have built
up a better cash reserve ; but shareholders were hungry
as a foraging Highlander. The co. has a fixed deposit of
£750; also, £155 cash, and net value of stone being
crushed, £lOOO ; but it owes the bank £1649. Yet there
are a few who want to make out that that mine is a
second Long Tunnel.

At Kingston (Vic.), the other day, a road-
repairer named Ricard found three specimens which
yielded 7oz. The same man, working on the same sort
of job a few months ago, uncovered a nugget worth
£4 17s. One of his mates also got a piece worth £5, and
now the Shire Council is being urged to let out the right
to repair roads at a fancy figure.

| (Tarnagulla (Vic.), where the big nuggets come
from, is a favorite resort of civil servants and bank clerks
on their fortnight’s holidays ; but new-chums’ luck has
not yet been conspicuous. One Melbourne clerk got a
30oz. slug when but a day on the field, but never saw a
color for the rest of his vacation.

From the IN orthern Territory :—The Territory
gets another knock: The N.T. Mines of Australia,
Limited, the Bull Co. that had spent tens of
thousands at Yam Creek and elsewhere in sinking
the right holes in the wrong places, &c., has
given up work, and is selling off all its useless
machinery lying about the country. There are some
prizes to be picked up for a song in the way of batteries,
locomotives, traction engines, &c. The diamond drill
did not prove the lode at the Ironblow to be any richer
at 400 ft. ; hence the shutting-down. Yet practical
miners of life-experience pronounce it a fine propo-
sition. The lode measures 40ft. at 200 ft. ;it assays sdwt.
gold, 12oz. silver, 5 per cent, lead, 5 per cent, zinc, i per
cent, copper. Such a body of low-grade ore ought to be
made to pay; but manager Dyson didn’t like it
from the first. Flux was one of his troubles.
Local people bubble over when his reports to London
are mentioned. Certainly it was never likely to pay if
worked as an English prospecting co. always does work
in these parts—with expensive staff and frills and a horde
of Chinese who are pastmasters at dodging graft. Mount
Elleson, the copper show now worked on tribute by
Chinamen, has produced over 60 tons of grey ore, going
50 per cent, copper, in a few months, and the tributors
say they have a good thing'; the heathen nearly always
gets the good things in the Territory.
* * * *
A second battery of 10 stamps is going up at
Mt. Todd, the new Northern Territory' tinfield that pro-
mises so well, and may be expected to get to work in
March. This will help to prove whether the field is what
it appears to be, even though the price for crushing is to
be £i per ton. Three large claims are being vigor-
ously worked. One, with four white men, basakaolinlsed
dyke ; the party is down 50ft. on stuff averaging
10 per cent, black tin. The Evening Star is a good show,
down about 60ft. on a nice-looking lode which will aver-
age 6 per cent, black tin ; the owner has 400 tons pad-
docked. The Clean Sweep, where the battery is being
erected, is very rich on the surface ; £BOO worth of the
richest ore has been sent away in six months, bagged
stuff mostly, with one parcel of concentrates. The
deepest hole is barely 30ft. Owners have 200 tons of
stuff at grass, averaging 8 per cent. A few Europeans
will make something out of this field, but much of the
bream of this, as of the neighboring Horseshoe, is going
to China. Port Darwin takes no interest in the place ;
no Darwin business has an agency there—Chows have all
the trade.
* * * *
“Mallarapoonie”: “ Blister ” (B. 17/1/’O7) gives
some Australasian records of gold production. Probably'
the most lucrative winning of gold in Australia was made
by Chinese with butchers’ knives on the Wandi River
bar, Northern Territory, in the early ’Bo’s, The cascade
formed by the river bar acted as a sluice to the alluvial
deposits higher up, and the quantity of washed gold won
from the crevices on the bar was enormous. The irregu-
larities of the bar acted as natural riffles in the great
sluice, and out of those cracks and crevices the Chinese
hooked the slugs with knives and pieces of bent wire.
While operations lasted the Mongolians swarmed on that
river bar like flies on a treacle tin, and nary white man
got a look in edgeways. It is estimated that fully ’OOOl
of that gold was spent in Australia.

“ Quidam ” : Southerners who meditate the
taking up of land in Queensland would do well to be wary'
of “ settlers’ group ” projects which are tangled up with
butter factory cos. and such like concerns. Some of these
projects may be sound, but it is nqteworthy that Queens-
landers themselves have refused point-blank to allow the
taking up of shares in co-operative concerns to be tacked
on to the conditions attached to such “ groups.” The
impossibility of obtaining any definite information from,
for instance; the people who are booming the “settlers’
group” at Proserpine, Q. (unless moving exhortations to
wire 30s. to becomeamember of the next group with a share
in the butter factory—size of share and liabilities not stated
—can be regarded as information), suggests that the pro-
moters of such schemes are not moved altogether by'
philanthropic motives. Before a southerner rushes to
the north to grow sisal hemp, he wants to make sure that
that vegetable is more reliable than , the sida retusa,
which was introduced with much enthusiasm many years
ago as a valuable fibre plant; and has been cursed with
much more enthusiasm ever since. The strange eager-
ness of some people to allow other people to make for-
tunes off land they themselves carefully leave alone
always suggests caution.

“ C. Ray ” : Re a Bulletin remark that it was
peculiar that when tributors got hold of a certain mine
the value of the stone sprang up, though no new develop-
ment work was done. It’s this way, perhaps : Say a
manager has been hauled over the coals in regard to
expenses. A hundred tons of stone may' cost 20s. to
raise=£loo. With 25 to 50 tonß of country rock added,
it will cost only £lOO (country rock has to be hauled,
anyhow). Result : Manager congratulated over raising
150 tons of ore at same figure as 100 tons originally cost.
Of course returns are lower ; but that isn’t the manager’s
fault, is it ?

From latest accounts it appears that
“Elijah ” Dowie lias lost not only his power and
his Purple Wings, but liis mental balance as well,
and is a physical and intellectual wreck. He now
roams round with his hands and feet bandaged
to the size of four small whales (he believes he has
foughtagreat battle, in which, although victorious,
he was badly wounded) and he curses all and
sundry, not in the great picturesque curse of old
but in plain crude damns. Also he calls himself
.Terry, which, perhaps, is short for Jeremiah.
What a fall ! From Elijah to Jeremiah; from
chariots to lamentations.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
‘G. Mac”.: “Janor’s” timber inspector
(B. 31/l/’O7), who couldn’t detect a stuffed log,
must have been a sewerage inspector. Let me
give him a wrinkle. Hang a watch at one end of
the apparently solid log, directly over the heart,
and listen at the other end. If the ticking of the
watch can be heard the pile is solid. If there is
no tick it’s. 100 to 1 that there is a pipe in
centre. This test has never been known to fail.

“The Proeshan”: John Moran, of
Ravenswood (Q.), doubts (B. 7/2/’O7) the veracity
of my statement to the effect that a child was
slimed over by a large carpet snake at Carr’s
Greek, near Grafton (N.S.W.). Nevertheless it
is perfectly true, and “J.M.” can have confirma-
tion by writing to Mr. Duncan McFarland, sugar
planter, now residing at the place. The child’s
name was Stewart, or Stuart. “J.M,” claims
that no Australian snakes slime their prey. This
has not been my experience. I have yet to find
the snake that does not slime. However, my
object is scientific research, and I am only too
thankful to Mr. Moran if he can prove his state-
meiits to the contrary. Here are the names and
of some gentlemen who can offer
‘J.M.” the proof of tlieir own reliable eyesight
that our snakes do slime, though perhaps not
always, and probably not in the case of small
bodies like frogs, mice, &c. : Sergeant Duprez
Adelong (N.S.W.), will tell how he saw
a 9ft. carpet snake .at Tooma (N.S.W.)
slime and devour a peweet, presented to the
reptile on the point of a piece of pine. Mr. A
Quarmby, storekeeper, of Batlow (N.S.W.), wili
explain how a black snake slimed a young rabbit
near Batlow. Mr. H. Nettle, mine manager,
Batlow, will mention that he saw a 12ft. carpet
snake about to despatch an opossum, fully slimed
at Yangarie, near Maryborough, in “J.M.’s”
own State. Mr. George Westphal, miner, of
Upper Adelong (N.S.W.), will prove that a tiger
snake slimed a jew lizard near his residence. As
Mr. Moran has doubted my veracity, I state no
instances for proof, preferring to let him receive
the evidence of others. I have written fear The
Bulletin for nearly 20 years, and its editor knows
that lam no dogmatist. I write of the bush, its
lore, and its law, as I see it, and I often take
much trouble to convince myself as to the correct-
ness Of what I state. I have no doubt that many
other bush Bulletin men will confirm my recent
assertion. With regard to politics and the fear
of God, I fail to see where they apply in a matter
of natural history.
* * ...
The western part of Central Queensland
has been suffering of late from a plague of frogs,
and in some houses they have been bagged and
carted away as the only means of lessening their
numbers. The frogs have brought an unusual
number of snakes about. One household, which
has had more than its shareof both classes of rep-
tile, captured an old-man goanna with a clothes-
line lasso, and pressed him into the service as a
frog-and-snake-destroyer. He was tied up to a
gidya, and given a plate of frogs and a bucket of
bore water as an inducement to stay. After a
couple of days of this treatment he was allowed
the run of the bathroom with his “harness” on,
and did well on a diet of snakes and frogs. Three
days later, he was let off the chain, and roamed
round the house, ignoring frogs, but still sampling
snakes. At the end of a week, he was tired of
snakes, but had eaten five young turkeys and all
the eggs in the vicinity. Then he was sacked
without a character, and that family now battles
with its own frogs.

of the black soil plains in wet weather, reminds
me of the black soil plains of Gordon Downs,
Central Queensland. Back in the eighties a
parcel of unregenerate kiddies used to get heaps
of fun, and not a little adventure, chasing kan-
garoos across the heavy soil. We used to run
bare-legged, having greased our feet and legs well
with lard, so that the mud would not gather
easily. It was a pathetic sight to see an old-man
kangaroo floun-
dering along with
immense clods
of the universe
anchored to both
his hind legs. A
fairly fleet and
well-conditioned
kid of 12 was
just equal to
keeping up to a
’roo in very
heavy country,
as the ’roo’s
bounding handi-
capped him ter-
ribly. Generally
we managed to
surround one,
but beyond
throwing clods
at him we could
not do him any
harm. We had
a wholesome fear
of in - fighting
with a ’roo, and
didn’t helpmuch
to keep down the
marsupial birth-
rate. At last a
genius took to
fastening the
blades of old butchering knives to light sticks,
which he proposed to throw javelin fashion at the
’roo. But one of the hunters stopped a spear
which wasn’t meant for him, and the hunting left
off about that time.

“ Blank ” tells this tale :
No wonder The Bulletin blushes perpetually. The
amount of bad language it is responsible for is truly
awful. In this “back o’beyond ” ancient copies of the
paper are passed from one bushman to the other, and I
have seen a three-months-old issue brazenly waiting in
the church porch till mass had been said. Perhaps that’s
why an aged par. was being discussed so heatedly in the
killing yard this afternoon.

bullock bein’ yoked to a bull is all blanky fairy tales,”
remarked a boundary rider.
A small crumpled man removed his pipe and expec-
torated deliberately. “It ain’t, Tommy—it ain’t no
blanky fairy tales. I tell yer a blanky bullock can’t
blanky well work with a blanky bull if he ain’t blanky
well changed every blanky week or so. I knoo
Large bearded man broke in: “I knowed blanky bul-
locks before you was blanky well born, an’ I tell yer it’s a
blanky fac’.”
“ That’s right. It’s the blank blanky breaf of ’em,”

Boundary rider interrupted excitedly: “ Don’t care a

Little crumpled man rose from his corner and ad-

“ J.G.” : Be bulls and bullocks working
together. Was once with a threshing plant, when
the engine got stuck in the middle of a paddock,
through a big flood coming down. Although we
double-banked our two teams of bullocks, we
couldn’t pull the mass of iron out —only pulled it
in deeper. The cocky for whom we were working
had a look at things, and said his horses would
pull it out. He brought down six of his best, and
hitched them to the locomotive, but, after they
had given a few tugs, the machine still remained
in the same spot. The cocky’s horses, however,
were scattered all about the place, and their har-
ness was broken to bits. The boss then sent one
of the men for Bruno. Bruno was a bull, whose
favorite occupation was to make scrap-heaps of
all the gates he came across. If he wished to go
for a moonlight walk he simply tramped through
his owner’s gate and scooted across the neighbor-
ing farms. The fence might be down in
places, but Bruno always mooched along till he
came td a gate, and then rushed through it, leaving
behind him a heap of firewood. The boss thought
that Bruno might pay some of his expenses, for
he had cost a lot in gate repairs, by getting the
engine out of the hole. He was yoked up in the
pole, and the reinforced team was urged to give
another pull. Bruno gave a tremendous heave
with his shoulders, and touched up the pair of
bullocks in front of him with his horns, and the
whole team got a move on. The bull heaved as
much as all the others put together—in fact, he
heaved like the devil. The engine came out of
the bog at a run—we had some trouble to stop
the team from bolting with it. We intended to
keep Bruno with us for a few days in case we got
stuck again, but that night he laid out a couple of
other bulls, ripped out the end of the feed-house
adjoining the stable, got in and ate about a ton
of oats, and arrived home with the ruins of a
dozen gates on his horns.

“ T.0.M.” : Here is the law on galls and
Chinamen. I visited my Chow yesterday to get
the true bill. “Clo gaw welly goo, welly littee,
welly pittar, welly narshity tilinkem. Sheposhem
welly ti, too muchee heart workemshumteem, too
ti longa arm, too shtiff, you she ? Clo gaw welly
goo, makem welly quick work, you she? Wombat
gaw welly goo, nishee, shveet, no pittar tilinkem.
Shumteem shick insi, heartburn welly shor, you
sharbee ? My ward, wombat gaw makem welly
feel pettar. Goanna gaw welly goo. Oily more
pettar. Makem belt longa skin, you she?
Yarelem longa podee. Kulem looshatism,
loomartic, anyting likee nart, you sharbee?
Sheposhem lubbem oily longa podee, longa arm,
welly goo, my ward !” “ How do you make the
gall medicine, Charlie?” “Oh, ketchem littee
cup, puttem gaw insi, mixem plandy—little lum
more pettar. Stheem longa shorsheparn harp
how, you she? Stheem mettem gaw, mixem
longa plandy, tilinkem nobbier, go bed. Clo gaw
(grimace) too narshity, pittar, me no likee
tilinkem, you sharbee ? No valk apow howsie,
tilinkem, go bed, lie down velly kwi. Sheposhem
you littee poy ketchem clo, ketchem wombat, me
buy shickshypen too —me too ole, earn shootem.
Clo too fly away quick, no ketchem, you she ? ” I
think this clears the matter up.
Experiments in prickly pear destruction
recently made in the Kingaroy (Q.) district have
proved successful. In October the spiny anathema
was sprayed with a mixture, the nature of which
is a secret, but which is being patented. Three
months later the stalks and leaves of the beastly
vegetable were lying on the ground quite dead,
and on being fired, they burned away completely.
Only a little grass was used to kindle the fire.
The roots were dug up and found to be rotten.
Other pears treated early in January were dying
fast three weeks later. Rain does not affect the
efficacy of the mixture, 10s. worth of which is said
to be sufficient to destroy an acre of the botanical
porcupine.

“ Jane, where’s the bacon-rind that teas in the

It would seem that a rapid variety of
bottled lightning is purveyed at Richmond (Q.).
One Henry Leo recently struck that town with a
credit balance of £l5O, the result of a couple of
years’ toil on Oondoroo station. A fortnight
later he finished up as an almost penniless corpse
in the local hospital, a victim to alcoholic poisoning.
Towards the end he didn’t see snakes or other
products of bush liquor; he had got past that
stage, and couldn’t distinguish day from night.
A whole procession of fantods carrying umbrellas
might have passed him unnoticed.
THE BUSH BEYOND THE RANGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From Crow’s Nest here by Sydney town,
Where crows had nests of old,
I see the' Range where day goes down—
The dim blue in the gold.
And sometimes wonder, half in doubt,
Has there been so much change
As pictured in the prints about
The Bush beyond the Range.
There’s motor-cars and all the “ frills,”
But none of my old mates—
The Bush seems run by Buff’lo Bills
And Hayseeds from the States.
I miss the homesteads and the scrub,
The stock and fences too,
The horse and swagman and the pub
That Minns and Mahony drew.
I miss the drovers, diggers— sheep,
And—lots of things. Ah well I
I wonder if the Kellys keep
The Carriers’ Camp Hotel,
If that still stands by hill and plain
As old Man Kelly’s pride,
Or if he did pull round again
When Mary Kelly died—
And Andy Kelly took to drink,
And Barney took a horse
(And two years’ hard without a blink),
And each one took his course.
And what become of Andy Mack,
Tom Browne, and Pat “ O’Brine ”?
It must be twenty seasons back
Since last I had a line.
I wonder if—but I forget
And wonder like a fool,
Is Bertha Lambert teaching yet
A wretched half-time school.
I hope—Ah ! how the memories come
To bother and defer—
I only hope my boyhood’s chum,
Fred Spencer, married her.
I wonder, and the more it seems
So far away and strange,
For I have lost, except in dreams,
The Bush beyond the Range.
I wonder, too, in fear and shame,
Do they, like me, forget—
I wonder if they mind the name
Of Henry Lawson yet.
N.B.W.

reading on February 6 was 102 deg., and the mini-
mum during the night 56 deg., a fall of merely 46
deg. !
* * * *
“ C. Ray,” a member of two exploring
parties that got thirsty in those parts, gives this
pleasant account of the country between Laver-
ton and Alice Springs—the country that cyclist
Birtles had an idea of biking through :
There are from 300 to 500 miles, with scarcely a break,
of sand and spinifex, and through most of this it would
be absolutely impossible to push a bike. Where it is not
loose drift sand, it is spinifex, from 2ft. to 3ft. high, and
so dense that no bike could run between. Birtles’ idea
of catching the early summer rains stamps him as a man
with no knowledge of the inland desert, as there are no
summer rains to be depended on that far south. The
only way out for a cyclist would be to come back the way
he went before he got too far, or to break south and get
nearer the coast line.
As it turned out, Birtles went back almost before
he had put his nose in.
“ Yaller Box ” : Tim was the
champion practical joker of the dis-
trict. Near his house was an old
hut, and into it he used to invite
sundowners to spend the night.
Now, Tim had an iron tube laid
under the boards of the hut and
coming out through a mound of
earth at the back. Through this
tube he used to speak to his guests
in hollow tones in the dead of
night, and many and weird were
the yells that arose, and many
were the half-dressed forms
that fled through the darkness
from that hut. But retribu-
tion overtook Tim in the
shape of one of his neigh-
bors dressed as a tramp. Tim
failed to recognise him, and
kindly invited him to sleep
in the hut for the night.
He failed also to see a re-
volver which the tramp car-
ried, though it was only
loaded with blank cartridges.
About 10 o’clock Tim crawled
out to his tube, and speaking
through it in a dreadful voice
asked the tramp if he was
prepared for death, and told
him that hell was yawning
to receive him, and that all
was discovered. In reply,
the tramp fired the revolver
three times into the tube,
and Tim reckoned he had
swallowed an earthquake
and a volcano and an es
caped tiger. He lit out
for home, forgetting the
barbed-wire fence he
had carefully crawled J
through a minute be-
fore. Three more shots
were fired before Tim
broke away from the
fence, leaving all his
clothes and a great
part of himself on
the spikes. After that
he was in bed for a fortnight, and for another
fortnight he only took gentle exercise.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: “X.P.D.” writes about his
versatility (B. 10/l/’O7). I claim to be also in
the running. Started off with selling newspapers.
Was 12 months cabin boy—discharged for being
tired ; farm laborer six weeks—booted for spoon-
ing cocky’s daughter ; drove milk cart 10 months'—
sacked for exchanging milk for rum ; offsider for
teamster 13 months—flogged for knocking leader’s
eye out; mining three days—boss told me I ought
to be “mining sheep”; navvy four hours—got
the infernal for telling ganger to run away and
play ; officer of the law 10 weeks—dismissed for
arresting one of General Booth’s female warriois
on suspicion of easing a drunk of his watch.
Have been a son of rest for two years. Would
like a job as manager of a hotel. Age 27.
* * * *
“ Duroe ” ; “ X.P.D.” (B. 10/l/’O7) makes
a good exhibit. I would like to see more of such,
as I hold that there are hundreds in Australia
who Cyin do almost anything, and do it well.
1 lore’s jny little lot. Breed, French-Scotch cross.
Apprenticed to engine-fitting. Cleared off to sea.
Since 'then been seaman, cavalryman (U.S.A.),
exchanged to artillery, sergeant-instructor to
cadets at West Point, singer, actor, musician,
monologue entertainer, advance agent, manager,
auctioneer, upholsterer, polisher, sawyer (both
plain and circular), engine-driver (certificated),
laborer, bricklayer, carpenter, axeman, con-
tractor, horse-breaker, shopkeeper (brief and
disastrous), cocky, dairy farmer, teamster, vine
and fruit grower. Only 46, so may have a turn
at many more occupations yet. In the intervals
of this strenuous life I have managed to rear four
strong, healthy boys (two over 6ft.-)f

“San”: Phylloxera-proof vines are an
absolute necessity nowadays, and so the N.S.W.
vignerons feel that they need them. But they
don’t want to purchase them for themselves.
They are altogether too Anti-Sosh for that. They
want to be supplied with vines by the State.
That fond parent is willing to give the vignerons
phylloxera-proof stocks, but says that the growers
must do the rest themselves, i.e, graft the proof
scions on the stocks. When G.H.R. has got tired
of his sectarian toy the wine-growers are going
to enlist his services to stump the country on the
question of the graft, the whole graft, and nothing
but the graft.
THE JOB WASN’T FINISHED.
THE SHEPHERD : “My man, I’m sorry to say
you’re drunk.”
DEADBEAT : “ ’Scuse me, sir, but I’m sorry to
say I’m not.”
“Parapluie ” : In answer to “ Janor ” (B.
17/l/’O7) re lyre-birds. I have seen that worthy at
work when he has been imitating, or trying to
imitate, everything he could think of —and he
thought of a few. At the bottom of the Falcon-
bridge Falls, near Springwood (N.S.W.), some
years ago, I came upon him. Sneaked to the top
of the falls, and, looking over, saw the bird
scratching and fooling about generally among the
leaves, and whistling most of the time. He
mimicked most of the larger birds that grow in
that neighborhood, notably what we used to call
the mountain magpie, also the crow and thrush.
Then he’d swing off and mix them a bit, and throw
in a few of his own notes to vary it. Again, I
was down among the gullies between Bermagui
and Bega, where there seem to be lyre-birds
(pheasants they call ’em there) in every gully, and
I caught him again. He had a bigger variety
there, but the butcher-bird was his best perform-
ance. He also tried wonga-pigeon, water-hen,
gang-gang (is that the way you spell it ?), parrot,
&c. I have noticed that the lyre-bird generally
pitches his tale a tone or two higher than the bird
he mimics. I have never heard him tackle a fac-
tory at work, or the Legislative Assembly, but he
will sometimes try an axe or saw, though you
have to guess hard and imagine a lot even then.
Next time I visit the country of the lyre, I will
let “Janor” know, and, if he will guarantee not
to go through scrub like a stampeded bullock, I
will take him along and show him. (P.S. —If you
get puzzled about water-fowl being in mountain
gullies that suit the lyre-bird, I may mention that
most of the gullies between Bermagui and Bega
open into lagoons, where are found, at times, all
the water-fowl ever thought of.)
* W * *
“F.H.8.”: Agree with “Janor” (B.
17/l/’O7) re the lyre bird’s mimicking. Lived for
over a year under Mount Bau Ban, North Gipps-
land, which is the poultry’s head station. I have
heard them whistle and jabber in their own
language, in a varied w r ay certainly, butcould never
detect the real true note of the butcher bird,
magpie or leather-head in their rambling state-
ments. The leather-head gets nearer human
speech in his wild untaught state than any other
bird in the bush, and, I think, would make a very
intelligent pet; but, thanks to his drab, unattrac-
tive feathering, he is seldom (if ever) made cap-
tive. The leather-head makes a sound in every
way similar to the “ knocker ” (loose pieces of iron
knocking together), used on many prospecting
shafts; hut this is a mere coincidence, for he
makes the same sound in districts that were never
guilty of harboring even one wild-cat.
11 * * #
Somebody thought he had discovered a
baby bunyip near Seymour (Vic.) the other day.
The animal had the head of a rabbit, a large
irregular scaly body, and a long tapering tail.
When discovered it was squirming about on the
ground. It escaped into a burrow, and was dug
out. Then it proved to be a sft. snake that had.
swallowed nearly the whole of a rabbit tail first,
and was patiently waiting till there was room in-
side for the head. So again the bunyip disap-
pears into the dim recesses of Myth.
“DONE."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was at the Squatters’ Arms that tipsy Councillor
McKenna,
While referring to his bank deposits, startled folks around
By precipitately betting Spink (our local beak) a tenner
That in twenty minutes he would let him see “a hundred
pound.”
When they’d wagered, old McKenna on his monetary
mission
Started off, and Spink dropped down into the cellar
through the floor,
Where he waited till the boaster, with tumultuous
expedition,
Back with twenty fivers in his fist came rushing through
the door.
To the customers McKenna then appealed for informa-
tion
As to “where the blazes” Spink had gone; but little
could he get.
They all seemed quite oblivious of his accurate location,
And observed that he had better “hunt him up ” and
win the bet.
“ You must hurry up, McKenna,” said the harman, who
was trusted
With the stakes. “ I’ve set my stop-watch just in honor
of the bet;
Spink must see that hundred pounds in twenty minutes,
or you’ve busted
Up yer boodle, Mac. Let’s see ! You’ve got eleven
minutes yet.”
“ P’r’aps he’s gone to meet a lady, Mac,” remarked Fitz-
gerald drily;
“Or got underneath the doormat,” ventured Simpson,
with a grin ;
“ Fancy givin’sterlin’tenners round like that,” observed
O’Reilly;
“ It’s the kind of wanton waste,” said Smith, “ I calls a
mortal sin.”
Then McKenna hunted round for Spink, whilst flourishing
those fivers,
With original profanity that soothed the wicked ears
Of the petted cockatoo ; and half-a-dozen bullock-drivers
Said they’d never heard such language, and went out and
left their beers.
There ismelody in swearing. Some just mention “H ”
and “blazes,”
Or p’r’aps touch on other idioms intermittently when
filled
With the throes of discontent; but McKenna cursed in
phrases,
Comprehensive, rich and flowery, and his words were
words that thrilled.
Then he sprinted through the dwelling rooms, dived
underneath the tables,
With despatch that filled the rats W'ith apprehension, and
he fired
Down the backyard and investigated kennels, coops and
stables
Energetic’ly, until the twenty minutes had expired.
Back into the bar he staggered then, defeated and
dejected,
As he flicked the perspiration from his forehead, nose,
and chin,
And beheld the crafty, calculating Spink, as he expected,
Calmly tucking those two tenners in his wallet with a
grin.
Old McKenna’s attitude was picturesque as he demanded
Back his tenner with expostulations lurid, red, and loud,
Whereon Spink, who liked his little joke, was satisfied,
and handed
Back the money w'hen he’d made him order whiskies for
the crowd.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and,
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany. '
