PERSONAL
ITEMS
The late J. Zimri Sellar, the Labor mem-
ber for Adelaide, who died late last year, only left
£l2O behind him.
There may or may not be political signi-
ficance in the fact that G. H. Reid, who, it is
well known, has always had a liking for physical
exercise, was seen lately examining a pair of
running shoes.
S.A. Chief Justice Way is the champion
fusser of his State. He has an idea that nothing
can go on without his help, and is, therefore,
president of everything, from the poultry society
right up to the Supreme Court Bench.
For years it was complained that John
PascoeFawkner, one of the pioneers of Melbourne,
had nothing but a suburban park named after
him. Now, by way of repairing the neglect, Mel-
bourne has named a cemetery after him.
An Australian State Premier who had an
addition to his family lately thought of christen-
ing the youngster “ State Rights.” That was a
style and title affected by many parish politicians
in America during the early years of the U.S.
Old Joe Thompson has been telling his
brother “bookies” in England that this is his
50th year at the game. The sort of book that
Joe Thompson made in Australia in 1857 didn’t
come within the definition of “the game,” if
history speaks truly.
It is 50 years this month since the first
members of the S.A. House of Assembly were
elected. Only one of them —Luther Scammel, a
stalwart Yorkshireman—still survives. He is a
wholesale chemist, and a splendid advertisement
of the advantages of taking his own drugs, or of
avoiding them—whichever it is.
Old Captain Roe, father of the Westralian
J. B. Roe, who died last week, was chairman of
the first meeting ever held to form a mining co.
in Westralia. There are (or were the other day)
only three surviving shareholders—Henry Strick-
land, W. Rewell, and J. Bateman. Though it
lasted 60 years the co. never paid a dividend till
it came to be liquidated.
It is stated of a South Australian Labor
member who died recently that, having married a
wife younger than himself, he made over all his
property to her. She predeceased him without
having made a will, and her relatives insisted on
taking two-thirds of her money. So the ancient
and reckless member had to sell his goods,
chattels, and real property, and pay away the
bulk of the proceeds to strangers.
William Gilbert, chosen as the Ass candi-
date for the Adelaide district in the S.A. Legis-
lative Council in succession to the late Labor
member Jelley, completed his 78th year on Febru-
ary 23. He sat for 26 years in the House of
Assembly, but did not stand for his old district
last year, as he feared that his name had become
Slush. He is a dealer in chaff, and has made a
good deal of money out of that cow and horse
provender.
W. P. Crick is fond of telling a story of a
resident in England who derives all his vast
income from investments in N.S.W. That resi
dent is tapped now and again for subscriptions to
Australian charities, but is mostly flinty. Even
a big coal-mine calamity didn’t draw a copper
from him. But, according to Crick, the mam-
moth absentee once paid £lO,OOO for a doll at a
bazaar, because a royal personage presided at the
stall.
LOYALTY.
CENSUS TAKER : “ And where is your husband ,
madam ?”
LADY : “He died four years ago.”
CENCUS TAKER : “ Pardon me, but judging by
your child you have married again.”
LADY : “ Indeed I 'avc not. I’ve more respect
for his memory .”
u. : Judging mainly from their appear-
ance while delivering their public yelps, I believe
Judkins is a sufferer from toothache, Tregear
from chronic cold feet, and other wailful “re-
formers ” from. dyspepsia, swelled head and
spasms. Some seem to have all these ailments.
It is sad that our noisy brethren should be thus
afflicted, but I really can’t see why they should
choose a church as a suitable place for the utter-
ance of howls caused by their anguish.
Last week the Sydney courts were in-
terested in an application to have W. P. Crick
struck off the roll, an action by Crick against the
Speaker of the Assembly, an action by Crick
against the Sergeant-at-Arms of the Assembly,
and an action by the State for the recovery of
income-tax alleged to have accumulated against
Nicholas Willis during his absence in Africa.
It seems that the least Crick and Willis can do is
to present the N.S.W. Law Department with a
new door-mat. The old one must be pretty well
worn out by the feet of Criok and Willis.
Alexander Berry, whose
estate is coming back to
the N.S.W. people under
various conditions, ob-
tained most of his
Coolangatta land
as a free grant.
He travelled
from England in
the same boat as
Governor Bris-
bane, and had
no difficulty in
arranging mat-
ters. In these
days matters
were easily ar-
ranged. On an
earlier trip he
introduced to
Sydney a cargo
of spirits which
set him well on
hisfinanciallegs,
though, accord-
ing to Governor
Bligh, it pro-
strated most of
the community.
Alexander the
Grabber lived to
be 92, and then,
dying childless,
bequeathed
everything to
brother David.
David, in his
turn, lived to be
97, and, after
bequeathing
£lOO,OOO to a
Scotch univer-
sity, left the
balance of the
estate to his nephew,
Dr. John Hay.
Died in harness,
some five or six weeks
ago in Glasgow, at the age
of 06, John Miller, the oldest
church beadle in Scotland, or
anywhere else. A hard record to
break. John kept a filmy eye on the plate to
the last.
David Paton, who died in Adelaide last
week, at 66, was chief Presbyterian preacher of
that city, and prominent on 'the University and
Public Library boards. He had been ill for
months, and half of Adelaide, when it read the
obituary notices of missionary Paton, concluded
that the Adelaide preacher had gone to a brighter
and a better kirk. David Paton came from Scot-
land in 1877, his predecessor in the pulpit, John
Davidson, having taken the post of Professor of
English Literature at the infant University of
Adelaide.
It is related that the great Malcolm
McEacharn, of Melbourne, used to try to instil
a sense of reverence into his employees. A raw
youth who obtained a job in the garden was
informed—“lt is a custom amongst my men—of
course it doesn’t really matter—but it’s just a
custom to touch their hats when I pass.” The
new man promised to fall into line. When
Malcolm happened along on the morrow he was
met by a wild colonial boy waving his hat and
leaping in the air and shouting “ Hooray ! ” The
wild colonial boy got the sack.
The late John See had real enthusiasm
about opera. He had heard nearly every opera
produced in Australia, and could talk in an
interesting way about them and sing some of the
airs in a fairly-good tenor voice. Writer heard
See sing selections from quite a number of operas
on the road over the Big Hill, between Grafton
and Glen Innes, when the kangaroos sat erect and
wondered. See moistened his throat once or
twice with sips from a square bottle. That was
19 years ago, when he was young and gay, and
before he was fool enough to take office.
Police Inspector John Ahearn, who used
to boast of scenting cattle thieves 40 miles off in
West Queensland, died before luck found him. A
sum of £60,000, left by Dr. A’Heron, of Jersey
(U.S.A.), goes to next of kin ; and the Queensland
Ahearn section alone survives. Of these only
Mrs. Foster, of Warwick, is believed to remain ;
she was a sister of Inspector John and the medico.
The difference in the spelling of the name is
attributed to John. They were a Cork family,
and John for some reason preferred one spelling,
while the medical brother stuck to the other. The
inspector married a sister of lawyer and politician
Thynne, one of the identities of Brisbane.
John Norton, the new R.C. Bishop of
Port Augusta (S.A.), is a builder as well as an
ecclesiastic. He was a Ballarat boy, and when he
left school was put into the office of G. R. John-
son, the theatrical architect of Melbourne, where
he remained some years. So Bishop Norton pro-
bably knows more about building theatres than
churches. The late Archbishop Goold, of Mel-
bourne, rejected Jdhn Norton as having “no
vocation”. The Jesuits who educated him said
he had the required article, and John packed his
portmanteau and went to Adelaide, where he was
accepted by Bishop Reynolds, who sent him to
Europe to pursue his studies.
“ Stony ” : Tender nerves are owned by a
fatted person who is the leading political light of
a rather remote Melbourne suburb. He lives in a
mansion some little distance from the railway
station, and always sends a ’phone message for his
son to meet him when he is coming home after
dark. He does this for fear his person may be
relieved of valuables before he can arrive in the
safe shelter of his little £5OOO crib. When the
son and heir is not available, he takes a cab home,
and the cabby has to dismount and see his fare
right up to the front door. There he must ring
the bell and wait until a flunkey comes on the
scene, lest kidnappers should lurk in the recesses of
the rockery or hide with malign purpose behind
the statue of the Three Graces.
Floral item :
Alderman Patrick Stanley, of Eedfern (Sydney), has
retired from the Council, at the age of 82. The ex-
alderman, in a humble way, is an old Sydney identity.
In the long ago, John Baptist, from Portugal, opened a
butcher’s shop in Goulburn-street. In the late thirties
he took up a site on the sandhills, at the southern
end of Bourke-street, Surry Hills, then a wilder-
ness of sand, and founded a market garden, which sup-
plied Sydney with vegetables. Horticulture was added,
and before florists’ shops existed the elite of Sydney
travelled out to Baptist’s for bridal and tombstone
wreaths. In the old George-street Markets (dating from
1839) Baptist had a stall which was engineered by Mr.
Stanley. On John Baptist’s death, in the early seventies,
his immense property in all parts of the city was divided
between a son and daughter (still living). The business
in the Markets fell to Mr. Stanley. When the old Mar-
kets were demolished, Mr. Stanley removed his business
to George-street, on the w r est side, near Bathurst-street,
and there it is still, the oldest of its kind in Sydney.
QUESTION OF GEOGRAPHY.
“ Shay, boy, am I on the St. Kilda Road ?”
“ No, yer on the jag.”
When Charles Tucker, who has just been
sent to gaol in connection with the Adelaide
Customs frauds, was arrested he was an M.P.
The recent dissolution cured that, but when he
was sentenced he was a city alderman, a J.P., a
member of the Licensing Bench, a’member of the
Hospital Board, and a trustee in the estate of a
man who recently left, subject to a life interest,
£lOO,OOO to the Adelaide Hospital and £50,000 to
the Children’s Hospital. Tucker was one of the
handsomest and most popular men in South Aus-
tralia, and many persons still refuse to believe in
his guilt.
Garnet Portus, N.S.W.’s fourth Rhodes
scholar, is not a big gun at Sydney ’Varsity, but
the conditions, which demand proficiency both at
the examinations and in athletics, narrow the
field of choice. Originally he was a clerk in the
public service, but he gave it up to study for the
Anglican Church, and, after his three years’ resi-
dence at Oxford, he intends to return to Sydney
and wear the long ecclesiastical raiment there-
abouts. If he can say “Haw” correctly on his
return, the Clergy Importation Society may over-
look his Australian birth and give him a decent
living.
Charles Tucker is not the first S.A. parlia-
mentarian to go to gaol. The previous case was that
of a prominent merchant who had traded so very
recklessly that a scandalised Insolvency Judge gave
him two years without any criminal trial. Public
sympathy was rather with the culprit that time.
He had evidently been unable to realise that his
ship could actually sink under his feet, and had
borrowed right and left, after real hope was gone,
in a desperate endeavor to keep on. Some of the
latest lenders—old friends—when they found that
their good thousands had been borrowed just to
throw' into a bottomless hole, turned savage and
pursued him with “ charges.” And once, long ago,
there was a S.A. Attorney-General who only re-
tained his liberty by sudden flight—about the
biggest downfall of Gent One on record.
‘ 4 Spring ” writes about nothing very
coherent: —
During- next session of N.S.W. Assembly I’m going to
watch David Storey. He might get blown out next elec-
tion, and I want to enjoy him while he lasts. David re-
presents Hats, and is the bright particular star in a House
of Dreariness. Last session he watched the Ministerial
benches with the vindictiveness of a man who thinks
that he could shine there as brilliantly as the gloss on
one of his own belltoppers. Last time I saw him he was
dressed immaculately in a grey frock coat, a beaming
buttonhole and other things, and when he stood awhile
at the entrance scornfully surveying his fellow-
members, the gaze of everyone * was upon him.
Having posed sufficiently he strode haughtily to a seat
whence he could keep his cold, watchful eye on the
doings of the Government. The Address-in-Reply was
being cussed—or discussed. Storey sat restlessly waiting
to blister the Ministry. For some hours other members
deliberately jumped up in front of him so as to get him
properly narked and prevent him catching the Speaker’s
eye. From the gallery could be seen the wild
rebellious thoughts surging across his flame-colored
cranium. Suddenly he sprang up with the indomit-
able alacrity of Mephistopheles bounding from the
Infernal Regions. His cloven coat-tails reminded one
of Satan’s hoof uplifted to stamp out virtue. As he
strode to the table the gallery thought that the great
Hat Trade was at last to be put on a firm basis, and that
Carruthers was doomed. His oration bristled with
parish-pump heroics ; and was greeted with wild applause
from all sides of the House. Other members simply
alluded to “ the speech,” but Storey is the type of man
who always refers to the neglected municipal gutter or
the untidy suburban street as His Majesty’s drain
and His Majesty’s highway. Therefore he called the
aforesaid production “His Excellency the Governor’s
Speech,” what time he bowed lowly towards the Upper
House in which the Governor had recently briefly pers-
pired. Storey’s bow is a masterpiece, and he gets it in
wherever he can. But Carruthers still reigns. Yet
next session something may happen. Storey may even
sit on his beautiful, glossy plug hat by accident.
Yic. Premier Bent continues to fall off.
He was preparing to address a country depu-
tation from his motor car, the other day, when
the chauffeur got a move on, with the result that
the Honorable' the Premier bit his native dust.
Thomas landed on his waistcoat, and when picked
up was unable to continue his address, all the
wind having been knocked out of him. He admits
the country saved £lOO by the fall, as he was
about to promise that sum to the deputation, but
changed his mind with his second wind.
The case of Henry Stratton Izard, the
Wairarapa (M.L.) solicitor, who recently got five
years for spending £18,812 of trust moneys, is a
splendid advertisement for the Public Trustee,
whose honesty is guaranteed by the State. If
ever there was a man whose interest it was to run
straight it was Henry Stratton Izard. He be-
longed to one of the “best families” in Maori-
land, and his relations hold high positions in the
social, political and financial worlds. In fact, he
was the sort of man who could not afford to go
wrong on account of his family. Furthermore, he
was married to a charming lady who had consider-
able expectations and a fair income of her own.
He was well off, and his business was growing,
for as the fertile Wairarapa district went ahead,
so did Henry Stratton Izard, who was the out-
ward and visible sign of the district’s prosperity.
Apparently, Henry Stratton was not dishonest in
the ordinary sense, any more than the gorilla is
when it annexes property ; he was simply devoid
of moral sense. He laid himself out to have a wild
and lurid time. Just before the crash came he
trotted round Australia on a holiday tour, had a
great time, saw the Cup, and went back to Maori-
land to file his schedule and announce his short-
age just as calmly as if it had been anybody else’s
shortage. Now he is booked for five years, and
as he is sure to be a good-conduct prisoner, he
will probably get one-third of that knocked off
for good behavior, even if he doesn’t get out
almost immediately because gaol life proves un-
healthy for him.
From an old English pressman :
The death is reported by cable of William Howard
Russell, the over-rated war correspondent of London
Times. Russell was born in County Dublin, in 1821. He
joined the Times as reporter in 1843, and was its corres-
pondent during portion of the Danish-Prussian-German-
Schleswig-Holstein scrap that ambled around in the years
between 1848 and 1851. After writing for various papers,
Russell practically dropped journalism for bar practice.
In 1854 the Aberdeen Ministry, shivering on the brink of
war, was barracked into taking the plunge by the Times.
Then that journal found that the expert it had
selected as representative for the Crimea could not
go, and Russell got the chance of his lifetime by
being picked. Up to that time war correspondence
had hardly been taken seriously. Russell knew little of
military matters, but he had the gift of the blarney, and
managed to acquire a camp reputation as a humorist,
mainly on the strength of a touch of brogue. His much-
boomed letters, exposing the mismanagement of the cam-
paign, were not unique (Wood, the Standard correspon-
dent, forwarded similar communications to his paper),
but the Times was then at the height of its power, and
other journals were scarcely heeded. Russell’s Crimean
writings had much to do with the temporary clearing
away of various antiquated military absurdities, but
there has been a fresh crop of similar idiocies to take
their place in pretty well every subsequent war. How-
ever, Russell suffered severely ever afterwards from
swelled head, and set himself up as a kind of modern
Mars. The Times turned him loose on every war of any
size up to 1882. In the American Civil War his sneers
concerning the defeat of the Federals at the first battle
of Bull Run caused the Northerners to warn off all
British correspondents. When men of the calibre of
Archibald Forbes were discovered by other papers there
was a slump in Russell stocks. Besides war books,
Russell produced a stodgy novel and several dull volumes
written round royalty. He failed as a Tory candidate
for the Commons, and was tame secretary and chronicler
to King Ned during various tours made in his Prince of
Wales period. In 1860 Russell founded the Army and
Navy Gazette, which he edited until quite recently.

SINGLE CLUBBIE: “ Take ray advice, old man,

Captain Barton, about whose administra-
tion of Papua a Royal Commission has reported
most unfavorably, first reached the Possession as
private secretary to Governor Le Hunte, .at a
salary of £3OO per annum. He held this position
for a couple of years, and was then appointed
Commandant of the Armed Native Constabulary,
at the same salary. Next, he became Resident
Magistrate, Central Division, and retained the
position of Commandant. Shortly after this
appointment Le Hunte left Papua for South
Australia, and Judge Robinson was appointed
Acting-Administrator. Robinson came to a
tragic end, and a telegram summoned “Johnny’
Barton to Melbourne. Then he was appointed to
the position of Administrator by Prime Minister
Deakin, at a salary of £1250. There were
then seven officers in the service holding higher
positions than Barton. One was Mr. A. M.
Campbell, the officer so highly spoken of by the
Papua Royal Commission. Apparently Barton s
Johnnyism counted for much in the appointment.

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 19, 1907.
My Dear Arini.—
So the stuffing is knocked out of the Essipoff-
Ouchakoff romance, and it fizzles down to the
plain, unvarnished elopement of two middle-aged
persons. Elopements of this brand are of no use
to the sentimentalist, to the melodramatist, or to
Mary Merely Ann, seated on the chip-box,
reading near the kitchen sink. Countess Oucha-
koff, who was evidently out for dramatic effect,
and desirous of the bubble notoriety, even at
the cable’s end, must have recognised this lack of
varnish on her tale, and tried to atone for it by
“painted devils,” such as an infuriated hus-
band and a large fortune lost for love. For
months the English press, at two a penny,
lias given columns to the history of the
fleeing pair, rushing through Europe, America,
and the Isle of Dogs, with the outraged
husband only half-a-yard behind and gaining
rapidly, his whiskers and his artillery and his cuss-
language streaming in the wind. The Flying
Dutchman was a moss-grown person com-
pared with the meteoric .Russian trio. General
Ouchakotf, according to Madame and the bawbee
press, intended to kill the Captain with bullets
and swords, or boil him over a slow iceberg, or
something, and then haul his guilty partner back
to Russia by the tail, and put her in an asylum, or
else shut her up for the remnant of her
days in the woodshed. ' Mention of the large
private fortune she had forfeited for love was
frequent. The lovers poured over to Australia,
stopped a few days in Sydney, and finally pulled
up in Melbourne for want of funds.
They got a couple of columns of unexciting
comment from a couple of Australian papers, but
although several liners have come into port since
Olga and Ivan (or whatever their front names are)
arrived, the Furious Third tarries elsewhere, and
the murder is postponed. Last English mail
the ha.’penny press put the drab roof on the ash-
colored romance by saying, on the authority of
the eloperess’s mother, that that lady never had
a fortune, and,. on the husband’s authority, that
he never pursued them worth a rusty blunderbuss.
In fact, when he heard that Essipoff had run
away with his wife, he asked in effect, “Why
run ? ” He did come to England, so the ha’pen-
nies assert, but it was with the intention of
making comfortable provision for his wife. Just
like a God’s good man instead of an Ivan the
Terrible.
Perhaps the whole thing is a put-up joke, and
now the Countess may join Bland Holt’s eo., or
go into a bar, and Captain Essipoff into a Paris
cafe. Possibiy they may prove to be a concealed
advt. for a hair restorer.
The latest-created and youngest Judge, Mr.
Street, whose investiture took place with great
pomp and circumstance, last week, has a wife.
She is one of the Austins—a good-looking family,
whose “ Baa ” was once loud and of considerable
value amongst the pure merinos of this State.
Edith, third daughter of Federal Chief Justice
a id Mrs. Griffith, was married last Tuesday to
Ai r. George Doyle, who has a stud-sheep farm at
Molong. Bridegroom’s sister, a handsome girl who
has done considerable earth-girdling, is engaged
to Mr. Percy Griffith, who acts as associate to his
distinguished paternal progenitor. At the wed-
ding of last Tuesday the best man was Mr. G. E.
Flannery, who, report says, will “ arrive ’’with
a full band and a peacock guard, and sit on the
apex of the Bar—in time. Meantime he lives in
single selfishness, and gives his moral support to
his men friends when they stand under the floral
bell and the choir twitters “ P, Perfect Love.”
An English paper announces the engagement of
Mr. Maxwell Learmonth, third son of the late
Mr. A. J. Livingstone Learmonth, to a Miss Las-
celles, a niece of the Earl of Harewood. As
some of the Livingstone Learmonths have taken
root in this State, the item may be of interest.
From Johannesburg (South Africa) a corres-
pondent sends a yard-long account of the mar-
riage of Mr. E. C. Long Innes, of the S.A.C., son
of the late Justice Innes, of N.S.W., to Miss
Violet Alison Vivian, “the well-known and bril-
liant violiniste.” Bridegroom and his best man
were in uniform, and hymns 280 and 578 were
sung. These are the most interesting features of
the narrative.
Occasionally, from some no mean city in
America, comes tidings of that amazing Queens-
land lad, de Conlay. He was raised at Warwick,
drifted into journalism, and was the only press-
man who succeeded in interviewing the Duke of
York. The train enshrining the mighty atom
stopped at the Warwick platform, and de Conlay
arose from between the rails, or out of the
Royal dog-box, or from under the ducal seat. No
one is quite sure how he did it, but do it
he did, and his interview was telegraphed every-
where. Possibly he began the conversation with
“Good morning, Duke.” Soon afterwards he
came to Sydney, struck up an acquaintance with
the captain of an American wind-jammer lving in
port, went off in the old craft, and was three
months getting to San Francisco. He had a gold
watch and a gold tooth, and used to assert
grinfully that the two were enough to
travel on. When he desired to impress
a man he smiled on the golden side of
his mouth, and glanced at his gold watch.
The readiest American could not teach de Conlay
much. At Los Angeles, whilst waiting for a
train, he saw Mrs. Langtry (who has a ranche
thereabouts) on the platform. Cheerfully pulling
off his Queensland panama he introduced himself,
and started to interview her. While talking she
touched his arm, saying, “ Better stand from the
edge, here’s a train coming.” A few hours later
, ")ad despatched to the New York newspapers a
thrilling account of How the Jersey Lily saved
a Pressman’s Life.” He has swooped upon every
city of note in America, and usually stays with the
mayor He earns a big income, for he syndi-
cates his work, and lately got engaged to a mil-
lionaire s daughter, and still more lately the en-
gagement was broken off.
Mention of Mrs. Langtry reminds that she is
now a titled Lily. Hubby number two, Gerald
de Bathe, has succeeded to his father’s baronetcy.
He is some years younger than the now rather
wilted Lily, but as the pair don’t affect each
other’s society at all, he hasn’t much chance to
notice the disparity. A returned Australienne,
who saw the one-time beauty, says she has still a
lovely figure, and dresses smartly, but she is very
hard-faced, with a long upper lip. Her daughter
is a piquante-faced little thing—not a bit like
Albert Edward.
Curiously enough, and against all warnings
such as Shakspeare’s (I’m miles from a copy, but
it’s something about swaying level in your hus-
band’s heart when you are several years younger
than he), marriages where the husband is the wife’s
junior often turn out most happily. Mr. Burdett-
Coutts (who was Ashmead Bartlett when he
married his Baroness) was 40 years his bride’s
junior, yet he wept publicly and bitterly at her
bier. George Eliot and Mrs. Browning are often
quoted as proof of the wisdom 6f having your
old man younger than yourself. And every one
of us can call to mind instances of some woman
friend who proved the fond wife and careful
mother to the man she married. (N.B. —This is
the sort of thing one writes in Lent, when there’s
nothing going but the yachts on the harbor —and
one’s vitality, and the ash shampoo.)
Dr. Bobardt, the fleet surgeon who used to
brighten first nights at Her Majesty’s with his
massive presence, is returning to Sydne3 r , and
will go into private practice here.
Mr. Frank Coffee and daughter Holly are
passengers by the American liner, which, at the
moment of writing, is causing uneasiness by its
failure to keep to the time-table.
Judge Docker, who left by the Britannia on
Saturday, takes his first holiday since he mounted
the Bench, 26 years ago.
Madame Joseph Playoust, a leading member of
the French section of Sydney society, is departing ,
for a visit to the land of Frog. The lady is leav-
ing a nursery full of fine children as an earnest of
her intention to hurry back.
Prof. Carslaw and his Cruickshank ewe got
married so quietly up at Bowral that few knew the
date of the mathematical and sheep alliance. The
bride’s father and her first husband had consider-
able flocks, so she may well come under the head-
ing of sheep. The giddy young pair have gone
to Hobart by one of the apple boats.
An Australienne who has been liberalising her
education in London and Paris says that Sydney
doesn’t know how to do Her hair. Sydney
should decorate it with wings of birds, with
beetles, insects, vipers, with whole birds or made-
up ornaments of glittering, quivering effect.
Her dressing-table should be a menagerie, Her
jewel-case a zoo. Also, Sydney’s hair is not the
correct tint. It ought to be a shiny oakum hue.
Red is not quite the right word. This “correct
tint” is most trying, the traveller admits. It
ages the tinted Venus dreadfully. So there is
some consolation left for those whose hair remains
the color it was when Providence handed out the
fleece.
A mourner on a bike, who attended a funeral
bound from Sydney to Waverley Cemetery last
week, was eyed askance most of the road, and
finally shoved out of the mournful procession.
He was ahead of his time. The eye will get used
to the novelty by-and-bye, and the motor-hearse
with a specially melancholy toot and the bike
cortege will be matters of course.
Miss Rose Gore and Miss Mary Jones, who are
generally referred to as “society dressmakers” (the
latter has been abroad with Miss Eadith Walker),
are reported as going to London to set up a fashion-
altar there. Going from Here to There for the
purpose of making clothes for the Society Swirl
sounds like taking nuts to Brazil, but if you pre-
sent this point of view to a really travelled
Australienne she will sniff and retort: “There
aren’t many really good costumieres in
London. They can do with several from
Australia over there, for most of the dress-
ing is in shocking taste. The somebodies
are mostly very dowdy.” And the travelled
English woman always remarks the dressing
in Australia as being of a higher average
than in her own country. Two of the Throg-
morton family were the pioneers of this Aus-
tralian millinery-mission to London, and are
“Madame Scarlett.” Long ago a Maoriland
girl went there, and started as a Bond-street
milliner. After a while she got married, and
was presented at Court Hatters and frockers
going from south of the Line won’t need to make
demand on the fund for stranded Australians,
if such a fund is started.
The priest (Father Gibney) who is seen in the
last scene of the Kelly Gang Flickergraph at the
Palace, making a rescue from the burning building,
is now Archbishop of Perth. The lcinematograph
has discovered him, so to speak.
Mr. John Young, the Father of Bowls in
Australia, is seriously ill at his very much flowered
and foliaged home at Annandale, but as he is of
the wiry old stock, toughened by Northern
winters, he will probably be bowling again before
the end of this very disagreeable month.
An eighty-six-year-old Maoriland pioneer lady,
Mrs. Godley, died in London last month. Her
husband went to Greenstoneland as leader of the
Canterbury Association, under whose auspices
the province so named was founded.
A restaurant with a roof garden, 20 private
rooms, and a room exclusively for ladies is to be
built in Moore-street. The roof garden will fetch
us. Then shall we see parties of four trying to
look, in dress and pose, like the brave young
quartette in the Gallery-picture “ Sur les Toits.”
’Frisco Bulletin publishes a long list of
moneyed American women who have been mar-
ried to titled Europeans and have subsequently
separated from them, because the duke was a
failure, or the count’s complexion didn’t match
the lap-dog. The catalogue starts with Consuela
Vanderbilt, now legally separated from the Duke
of Marlborough, and goes on: “Anna Gould,
divorced from Count Boni Castellane; Anna
Robinson, actress, separated from the Earl of
Rosslyn ; Bessie Curtis, of Boston, divorced from
the Marquis of Talleyrand-Perigord (he after-
wards married Mrs. Stevens, of New York, who
also divorced him); Helen Morton, divorced from
Due. de Valencay; Florence Audenreid, of
Washington, separated from Count Divonne;
Lilian May, of Baltimore, separated from Sir
William Bagot; Eva Julia Mackay, divorced
from Prince Colonna; Isabel Bunce, of Ohio,
divorced from Baron Bronsart von Scliellendorf ;
Marie Fusz, of St. Louis, divorced from Count
Penaloza; May Yohe, divorced from Lord
Francis Hope. Altogether American women
have brought their foreign husbands 231,000,000
dollars in dowries.” How much their husbands
have cost them for divorce expenses has not been
calculated.
There’s an oil launch now on the Lachlan, I’m
told, for excursion tricks. Forbes folk crowd into
it in squadrons, and it does a little sprint of a few
miles on the face of the waters, and, back again,
and the passengers agree that there’s nothing like
travel for giving one a correct perspective of his
place in the cosmos. Speaking of travel and such-
like, a tall, thin, brown man from the West told
me once of an old lady at Pooncarie, on the
Darling, who repudiated with scorn the idea that
her life was bounded by a narrow horizon, and
said she used often to go to “ town.” He
was impressed till he learned that she meant
Menindie.
Wardrobe dealers—those worthy persons who
“ having left-off clothing of every description
respectfully invite inspection”—complain dread-
fully of the way charity has spoiled their
rtade. It is the same in England. Charitable
organisations (and their name is legion—also
mud) get the clothes that were formerly
sold directly by their owners, or given to
the gentleman’s gentleman and the lady’s lady,
who disposed of them for their own pockets’
sake. One charity organiser tells me that some
of the gowns sent to her would, if worn by one
of her poor women, ruin the reputation of the
wearer. Frocks of chiffon, lace, ribbon-work
decorations, frills, glace silk underskirts and
transparent corsages, if in decent repair, are sold
for the benefit of the charity. It is impossible to
donate an evening dress to the woman whose
clothes were burned when the four-roomed
wooden cottage went off pop, and expect her to
go out looking for work in the garment. But
most of the things come to the charity
in a state of such utter dilapidation that
they are of no use. Many of them have to be
burned. It is surprising to what ruins some ladies
will reduce their evening gowns. These one-time
costly rags and tatters are worn at theatres with
a lace scarf over the shoulders, or the calamitous
state of the frock is hidden under an evening coat.
The famous singer who lags superfluous on the
stage can, with her blurred voice, inflict heavy
damage on her backers. Witness :
Mr. Robert Grau, the famous impresario, has mysteri-
ously disappeared. His friends fear that he has either
met with foul play or become insane through brooding
over the impoverished condition into which he was
plunged as the result of the last tour of Mme. Patti.
Mrs. Long, mother of songstress Nora Long,
who is joining her successful youngster in London
presently, gets a send-off in the shape of a com-
plimentary concert on the 27th. Mrs. Long will
contribute a song written by herself, and set to
music by Mr. Cecil Compton, the organist of
St. Matthew’s.
Miss Edyth Latimer, the handsome Manly girl
now playing in South Africa as leading lady in
her husband’s company, has suffered an inter-
view, in which she is described, as of “Irish
nationality.” Are Australians in such bad repute
in S.A. that the lady feared to own up to being
one ?
Laurance Godfrey Smith, the keen-faced, curly-
headed young party who has returned from
several years’ study under Leschetizky, will give
a concert on March 6. He carries the hall-mark
of the Master of the Ivories in his style of playing
and in writing. L.’s letter makes affirmation as
to his “ distinguished musical talent.”
Mrs. C. R. Deane (she’s a sister of the warbler
who was Miss Josephine Deakin) is the latest
addition to the Sydney Smart Gowning Mission.
The decorative little widow has been in London
and Paris for eighteen months developing a natural
taste for making Dreams for Fair Women. And
she now intends to teach others how it’s done.
That bright young soprano. Miss Carrie
Lanceley, ought to get a capital send-off concert,
judging by the fine gathering that met at Paling’s
to discuss the matter. Mr. J. D. Fitzgerald pre-
sided, and two committees were formed. Mrs.
Mayman and Miss Alicia Nolan will head the
ladies’ com., and Mr. Southwell will steer the
executive com.
The difference between a penny and thrup-
pence, to be sure ! When one had to pay the
latter sum for a speak through the bureau ’phones
there was nary a wait. Now, since the penny-in-
the-slot became the right thing, people are
’phoning what they’ve got to say instead of
putting it in writing on a sheet of paper, tucking
that into an envelope, affixing a penny stamp and
dropping it into a box. There are four or five
bureau ’phones at the G.P.0., and the new,
reduced rate has already caused an increase of
speakers to the number of 450 a day.
From the Trig. Station, at Winton (Q.), comes
a guinea for the Child Study Association. It was
collected among a few men in the Survey camp.
“ Trig.” has, I believe, something to do with
survey work, although it looks like the name of a
dog. Anyhow, the triggers who sent the money
have a lien on Heaven, and are hereby thanked.
Five women were fined for shop-lifting at one
Sydney suburban police court on Wednesday, last
week. They stole blouses, collars, corsets, skirts
and other frills and furbelows, and their Christian
names were Victoria, May, Annie, May and Lily.
It takes a lot of frill to live up to the names of
Victoria, Lily and May—and sometimes the frill
has to be stolen. Sarah, Mary Jane and Susan
are plain calico or bagging names that require less
living up to, therefore they are less frequently
called out in the halls of Justice, except when
Sarah has struck Susan on suspicion of annexing
her “ boy ” Bill or Ginger.
My Brisbane correspondent writes :
Lady Chelmsford will arrive in town for a fleeting
moment this week to arrange a few further details of the
coming ffite, but the household will remain secure at
Gabbinbar until the heat wave evaporates. At present,
life is one long, free Turkish bath with the towels left out.
Tidings of Queenslanders in London continue to sift
through. Pretty Claudine Hamilton and her mama
found the husband-elect awaiting them at the Liverpool
Pier, with the latest thing in Panhard motors, a £l5O-
- diamond ring, and a sealskin coat, just as a few
slight offerings before deciding on the wedding gifts.
Mrs. Gerald Hanbury is at the “Mayfair,” and, like all
Australians, bucks at being charged for a bath. Mrs, D.
T. Seymour and her little son are to be seen around daily,
and this dashing lady has already earned a name for her-
self among Bridge devotees.
Very real sorrow is expressed at the tidings of the death
of pretty Nina Clerk, only daughter of Geo. Temple CRrk,
which took place, through appendicitis, at Glen Innes,
whither she had gone on holiday. Miss Clerk, who was
engaged to Mr. Noel Drury (third son of the late E. R.
Drury), was one of the brightest and sunniest-natured
girls in Queensland. Mr. Clerk is himself an inmate Of a
private hospital at present.
The proposed meeting to arrange for the introduction
of over-sea domestic servants seems to be hanging fire,
and so far the doings of the sub-committee that was
appointed to make preliminary arrangements are shrouded
in gloom and wrapped in mystery. The Danish Mary
Ann and the anticipated Norse Biddy are as far off as ever.
Yours Affectionately,
AKKENEHI.
“C.N.”: The statuette which French
sculptor Fremiet presented to V ic. National
Gallery will excite considerably more public
interest than his heroic “ Joan of Arc/’ for which
the city paid him £lBOO. The statuette, a bronze,
shows a Jap, or a gorilla, or something to that
effect, holding a nude white woman on his left
shoulder, while he grasps a gibber, weighing half
a cwt., in the dirtiest left extant. His mouth is
open, and he is roaring “Banzai” at some persons
who desire to rescue the lady. The whole compo-
sition is hair-raising, flesh-creeping, and calculated
to send infants into hysterics. It is understood
to be symbolical of the relations of Japan and
Australia.
“It’s the las’ time I’ll enter a lydy’s bed-
room in a gentle, unhobtrusive sort o’ way,” said
Bill when he pulled up with his mates after a quar-
ter of a mile run. “If I had entered as a burglar
villain, she would have fainted for sure, if she wus
awake, an’ I’d ha’ got the joollery. But entering
as I did, she took me for her ’usband, fired a boot
at me, smashed the mirror by mistake, an’ then
yelled to make it seem it wor ’im behavin’ cruelly
again. There wus nothing for it but to run.”

establishment of the Valaze Massage Institute in our
midst brings us up to date with Europe, as far as the
treatment and cure of all facial blemishes and disfigure-
ments are concerned. It is rather wonderful to think
that here, in far-away Australia, we can find the latest
cures for such trying disfigurements as red noses,
birth marks, warts, double chins, abnormal hollows,
wrinkles, &c. The Institutes are established at
158 Pitt-street, Sydney, and 274 Collins-street,
Melbourne, where all the latest apparatus is installed,
while the treatment is in every way hygienic and anti-
septic, and the utmost care in this direction is exercised.
Here not only red noses, distended veins, superfluous
hairs, double chins, undue redness of the face/smallpox
marks, and almost every face disfigurement are eliminated
by one treatment or another, including the wonderful
suction and injection treatments, but the everyday wear
and tear, the roughened skin, wrinkles, blackheads, and
all the small blemishes are made to disappear, and a
fresh, clear complexion adds charm to the personality.

will call at the Beauty Institute, 158 Pitt-street,
Sydney, or 274 Collins-street, Melbourne, at any
time, when she will be happy to give all information
in her power about the various treatments or
any of the Valaze preparations. Valaze is the safest,
pleasantest and most reliable skin food on the world’s
market. The effect of Valaze is certainly magical.
Freckles, tan, sallowness, sunburn and almost every skin
blemish disappear under its spell immediately. After
regular appliances of Valaze the skin becomes fair and
radiant. Three months’ trial of Valaze will give you a
new complexion. It will rejuvenate you, and will leave
the muddiest skin as soft and glowing as a child’s. Valaze
is not an unknown, unproven preparation, but it is a
guaranteed Skin Food, which has stood the test of years,
and is a preparation of the cleverest skin specialists of
Europe. Valaze Blackhead and Open Pore Cure
cures the most obstinate cases ; approved by medical
authorities. 2/6, posted 3/-. Valaze Herbal Soap,
beautifully cleansing and purifying, made from almonds
and rare herbs, 2/- cake, postage 2d. Rusmo Hair Killer,
for the home removal of superfluous hair ; won’t harm
the most delicate skin ; has a great vogue in Europe.
5/-, postage 3d. Valaze is sold by Chemists everywhere
at 3/6 and 6/- jar. If posted direct from me, add 6d. for
postage. MLLE. HELENA RUBINSTEIN, 158 Pitt-

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 18, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
This Wednesday the curtain is to be rung up
on the Federal House of Babel. The blue and
white cards are out for the show, and, despite
the fact that no bun and milk follow the Gover-
nor-Generals blessing, there’s a clamor for the
admission msteboard It’s a barren season, and
the crowd *kes any sort of a show where there’s
a band and a viceroy.
Mrs. R. S. Whiting and her two chicks are off
before the cold season to study sunsets and
cherry-blossoms in the land of the Mikado. At
present the fish-named family is lodged at its
Macedon eyrie, giving week-end parties and in-
viting the fashionable element to come and roost
on its fence.
There are two globe-trotting millionaires at
large in this State. Both are mated. At a
swaggah hostelry these unfortunates, burdened
with their embarrassment of riches, are lodged at
present, and casual visitors, who always like to
see who’s a-nibbling there, suffer from eye-strain
in an endeavor to sort out the bloated plutocrats.
But there’s nothing to identify a millionaire by.
He bites his potato like any member of the
common herd, and drinks his tea in the plain old
way, by lifting it to his mouth, and then dis-
turbing the cup’s centre of gravity. So the
millionaire passes for any old thing on his globe-
trot.
A large family of Der.iliquin Lambs has been
drafted over here, and is now settled for the rest
of the season in the most stylish pens at Toorak’s
toniest mansion for paying guests. I believe
grass is good on the stock routes this season, and
the Lambs got over without any losses or serious
hardships.
Another ex-fashionable goes into the bonnet
business. Half of Toorak has pledged its im-
mortal soul to buy its best toque from the widow
in question.
Mr. Jardine returns next week to his legal
harness from a great old globe-trot. Probably,
too, the large, affable lawyer will resume his place
on the Catmittee of the Women’s Hospital, unless
his golden time of pleasure has altogether spoilt
his talent and taste for settling the squabbles of
the clawful sex.
One of our most ocean-gadding girls, who has a
mischievous spirit joined to good business capa-
city, has hied to Maoriland to start a branch of a
popular beauty factory in windy Wellington,
where good complexions get blown to pieces unless
preserved by (it is contended) certain rules and
treatment. On Friday a shoal of farewellers
gathered to wave a collective hanky at the beauty-
parlor nymph and cheer her out of sight.
They have been holding lace hops at the other
end of the globe. There were duchesse-point
Lancer sets, and every variety of hand-stitch
brand known to fanciers. It was a notion of
Lady Aberdeen to promote the lace cause in
Erin’s Isle. That sort of thing wouldn’t appeal
to the stylish elans hereabouts. A departure
suitable in many respects to the rival Toorakites
would be a Fancy-claw affairs at which such sets as
the Tortoiseshell, the Chinchilla, the Persian and
the common or kitchen tabby might be formed.
It’s on the cards that the Dame of Rupertswood
pays a flying visit to ’Roo-land this year with
daughter Phyllis, the story-writer. Probably it’s
for a final look at the beautiful Sunbury estate ere
it passes under the hammer.
A surgeon of this metropolis has an enormous
practice among the petticoated sex, principally,
one sharp Miss tells me, owing to the amount of
tenderness- he introduces into his treatment of
dislocated wrists and sprained ankles. The Miss
in question was being treated for a misplaced
funny-bone, I fancy, and she at first found the
surgeon’s solicitude a great boon. But when he
proposed to kiss the dimple on the girl’s elbow
she rebelled, and later on paid her account per
post, for fear Sawbones might again be demon-
strative. She learnt that he always did kiss the
patient’s funny-bone when no obstacle was placed
in the way, and that the pampered and petted
sex indulges him a good deal in the matter.
The vigneron Rowans treated their big circle
of friends to a lively hop last week, as an act of
grace after a daughter’s marriage. The gratitude
of parents whose chick has married advantage-
ously and to their liking (which is really the same
thing) is a shy and furtive emotion. Seldom does
it grow articulate or proclaim itself to a full
house. The Andrew Rowans provided the ex-
ception. Their hop at “Arranmore” was a
joyous affair—a litany of praise in polka form.
The Chief Judicial clan was expected, but did
not transpire.
They say a certain moneyed inarm keeps her
penniless but foolish spouse on tenter-hooks.
Every time he flirts with a random Miss, or a
visiting Mrs., his Apple Blossom makes a fresh
will. He knows all the quasi-secret preparations
—the drive into the city to the solicitor’s office,
the threatening brow, the ominous silence of the
dame. He reforms, apologises, and once more
the will gets altered in his favor.
A Werribee girl, who is M. A. by virtue of her
learning, got rid of a burdensome label, the otner
day—that of Hevingham-Root. Up till Wednes-
day of last week she had staggered under the
dead weight of her vast appellation, and in
marrying Dr. Laurie (second hope of the Prof.)
she must have felt that she had at last dropped
something heavy. Marriage is indeed a haven of
rest to some women—to Miss Jabcloots, for in-
stance, and Miss Mary Ann Corpse, and Miss
Jemima Slopp.
The R.C. knot-tying is suspended for the sack-
cloth and black ash (grey is sentimental) period.
In anticipation of the Lenten close season, couples
rushed headlong to the halter on Tuesday. It
was a fierce and virulent matrimonial scramble,
and it broke out in all the suburbs simultaneously
and sent the Fathers of the Church galloping
through the orange-blossom service. They
couldn’t leave any surplus couples on the mat, so
they went at express speed and tied every knot
hard and fast before Shrove Tuesday had glode
into Ash Wednesday.
A pair of elderly sentimentalists, who have both
worn the willow and been taking notice since
“something considerable,” are discussing a matri-
monial project. It’s a serious step—which really
leads on to an assortment of other steps in the
blending of two family broods. But the proposer
(one presumes the male sex is still the proposing
one) is so wealthy that the amassing of step-rela-
tives is nothing-—he could afford to take in all
Brigham Young’s descendants.
Last week the name of Miss Dorothy Frances
M‘Crae was blotted off the passenger list for the
Big Smoke and the bracketed label, Rev. and
Mrs. Perry, substituted. The charming Dorothy
has got herself engaged. The fatal act couldn’t
be averted any longer. There’s a lot of endearing
young charms about the graceful Hawthorn verse-
spinner. A curate, son of a bishop, is the happy
man. He is youthful, up-to-date. High Church,
and almost worthy in other respects of the peach
that has cotne in his way. He couldn’t be quite—
the peach is a prize one.
Sweet Nell comes along—on a recreative tour
this time. Tumor was the trouble and cause of
all the broken American engagements. Now it
has been treated, and the fair right shoulder of
Miss Stewart is only rivalled by one other thing
on earth, and that is her fair left shoulder. She
went aboard at Marseilles last week, and lands
here immediately after Musgrove’s consignment of
voices from Germany.
No one dreams, in regarding the teams of kiddies
in the pantomime, what the work of the wings
and the dressing-rooms means. Discipline has to
be maintained, quarrels prevented, giddiness
treated severely, and a perpetual watch kept over
the affectionate and sentimental girleens of the
young co. A disposition to sit and moon over the
drove of devils who dive into the stage trap-doors
has to be ruthlessly squashed. Miss Jeannie
Brenan is chief priestess of the wings, and she
prevents any serious love-sickness for devils, or
geese, or kangaroos by a rigorous discipline. Up
to this, no little waratah-girl-flame or baby-wattle-
sprig has suicided for love of a little boy-wallaby
One of the most expert she-fiddlers of the State,
Miss Lottie Schutt, has just floated back from
Europe, where she has been studying under the
eye and direction of masters more or less famous.
In the Big Smoke Miss Schutt fiddled with Hans
Wesseley, and later, having waited a whole year
for Professor Sevcik—the highest authority in the
land of the Kaiser —the aspiring Williamstown
duck (there are no chicks at our marine suburb)
travelled to Bohemia and toiled in the Professor’s
own melodious locality—Prague—for four precious
months. Each lesson cost two guineas, but the
Victorienne reckons she obtained full value for
every note. And now the fiddleress is back in
this Yarra capital. Under her chubby chin she
tucks her beloved fiddle, and saws out melody that
makes one’s heart feel glad. Hers is emotional,
broad-toned music—generous and good.
We’re smiling over here at the thought of the
moon-eyed tenor, Walter Kirby, lifting his melody
before the loftiest pair of royalties in the Empire.
Walter had just a little of Uriah Heep’s expression
of unworthiness when he sang. He bent his neck
at the angle of apology, and a polite forelock
drooped ’umbly upon his modest brow. The voice
was so much finer than Walter. It had vigor,
quality and positive charm. But Walter looked
sorry and sad, and seemed to ask you if he might
remain on your planet for the night. One wonders
whether his sensibilities have been blunted by the
vicissitudes and hardships of a few Big Smoke
seasons, so that he stood up to his inches before
royalty and tossed a valiant forelock as he burst
into song, or whether he languished as of yore
like a drooping lily.
“The New Clown” audience squashed into the
quartet of boxes on Saturday night, and over-
flowed the other less expensive parts of the Prin-
cess. Rupert Clarke had his pal, manager
Musgrove, and a golden-headed friend in his
pen. The vigneron Lukes, with a male trio that
might have been Matthew, Mark and John, were
perched in another. Mrs. Luke displayed a lot
of handsome shoulder to view. It was a big,
cheerful audience, kept on the grin by Hugh
Ward and co. till curtain-fall.
A flock of Nice People gathered on the Spencer-
street platform to weep “Au-revoir and be-
back-soon, Beaueaire” on Julius Knight’s bosom
last week. He of the heavenly calves was on his
way to Ballarat, and the regrets of the Tooraketty
quarter stood forth naked and unashamed. One
could scarcely hear the warning belch of the
engine at its start for the sighs that rent the late
noon atmosphere. The love for the commonplace
strutter passes all understanding.
The Palotta boom started at the hour of her jubi-
lant arrival. A pack of wolf-like adorers (mostly
the she-kind) rushed to devour her. I saw several
parties of feminines in a cafe casting green glances
at Grace on Saturday, and totting up enviously
the years that have rolled on since that star first
glimmered in these heavens. “How does she
keep her youth ? ” their jaundiced glances asked.
Grace would tell them in a minute. “By a glad
heart—which is health.” That’s the secret of her
perpetual bloom.
The mixed couple has found use for the silver
reaches of the Yarra from the willow-bend of
Studley Park to the wattle-banks of Alphington.
The boat shed is within a quarter of an hour’s
tram-ride of town, and the mixed couple flies
thither, and gets aboard, and leaves dust and
clamor behind it. On a recent golden Saturday,
an unbilled and wholly impromptu regatta set
the face of the river into a legion of dimples and
smiles. Boatloads of assorted sex oared their
way along—the boys straining at the blades, the
girls doing nothing save be ornamental, with
their gossamer-sails floating behind in the sun-
shine. Here , and there a long mauve hat
streamer seemed to be tracking the party, cloud-
fashion, to its evening lair. Sometimes a scarlet
gamp set its glow on a fair languid Gwendolen at
the steering perch. The boats bore the glaa
couples along in endless procession. Their rain-
bow contributions to the river picture almost
moved the idle loungers on the banks to cheers.
But almost as good as the oared treat is the
languor of the willow banks, where philosophers,
poets, and lonely souls sit and ponder beauti-
fully, and are supplied with a set of entrancing
river pictures on the cheap.
There has been a scandal with a rather curious
termination at one of the nicest of our suburban
paying-guest establishments. The wife is rather
frivolous ; the husband seems rather sleepy. The
attentions of the new boarder to the wife became
so pronounced that the proprietress of the estab-
lishment left him a note to say she required his
room. The boarder, of course, left. But next
week the husband and wife also left, and now the
same boarder passes the same husband the bread
at the dinner-table of another paying-guest estab-
lishment.
From “Rosna” (Adelaide):—
News comes from, the Fog that South Australian actor
Otto Hegg'ie has been engaged- fora 14 weeks’ tour of
Uncle Sam’s land with Ellen Terry and other celebrated
folk ; also that he will play leading parts. This State
chiefly remembers Heggie as the large mysterious
“ Messenger from Mars,” w hich part he played here some
time before his departure on the Bullward journey. The
company in which he will proceed to so astonish the
Yankee that he will have to leave off whittling is managed
by Ellen Terry’s daughter.
A number of enthusiastic folk have arisen and an-
nounced their intention of forming a Players’ Club in
Adelaide, a village that already possesses several drama-
tic clubs of the usual amateur standard. This affair is to
be managed by elocutor Percy Walsh e, who was at one
time assistant stage-manager for the Broughs. The new
club starts with a performance of “ Sowing the Wind,”
at the North Adelaide Institute, some time in March,
when the Frill of the City is just waking out of its
summer sleep.
Several globe-jaunters return to Adelaide by the
Mooltan this week, among them Harold Parsons, the
’cellist, who has been ’celling in Kaiser William’s country
for a year or two, and comes home to take up the post of
’eello'master at the Conservatorium. I confidently ex-
pect an epidemic of she-pupils on this large uncanny
instrument. Mr. Parsons w'as a handsome musician,
without a curl to hjs name, when he left South Aus-
tralia, but no doubt “Germany will have developed the
beautiful trailing locks beloved of the feminine heart.
The same boat brings the chief Conservatorium poten-
tate, Dr. Ennis, w ho rushed to Britain a little w'hile ago
to bring home his wife.
Professor Darnley Naylor, new classical professor at
Adelaide University, returned from poor little Britain by
the Bremen last week, and just ran into Melbourne for a
moment to shake a farewell paw' or two before taking up
his quarters in this city of great silent distances. The
learned one was formerly vice-master of Ormond College,
at Melbourne ’Varsity, and w'ears some of the alphabet
trailing after his name, as Brunhilda trailed behind the
wild horse in 613 a.d.
Feminine Adelaide, with its love of weddings (it will
walk miles to hear the Voice), has lately been consoling
itself for a dearth in this respect by tearing along to the
various Weddings at the Theatre Royal, and has crowded
that edifice so that it is bulged quite out of shape. The
Royal hasn’t seen such girly enthusiasm since the days of
Julius Tremendous. But this time the attraction is not
a star wi h calves at one end and a beautiful and haughty
marble brow at the other. It is the Weddings and the
frockiness of the heroine that draw the she-crowd in
such hordes.
Philanthropist Charles Goode, during his recent en-
circlement of the globe, visited the far-famed hog factories
in the great hog city of Chicago, and waxes almost as
eloquent as Mr. “ Jungle ” Sinclair about them. He says
that, dirty as many of the factories are, the chief dis-
grace is the fearful condition of the streets and tenements
in the meat-packing quarters. The streets are all unpaved
and unmade, although (or because !) the City is teeming
with millionaires. There are almost as many millionaires
as hogs, and it is often difficult (as well as useless) to tell
one from the other.
Editor Bonython leaves Adelaide for England during
the first week in April, and will be absent for eight or
nine months. Most of the time will be spent in his be-
loved Cornwall, where the ancestral estates of the
Bonythons are situated.
Yours Affectionately, Tryphena.
The idea of “squaring” the High Court
has been entertained at least once. Just before
the hearing of a Chinese appeal a sleek Celestial
called upon a lawyer and tendered a cheque for
£B5O. “Sir Samel Glippet, tlee hun’ en fifty;
Sir Toby Barton, two hun’ en fifty ; Sir Lichard
O’Con’, two hun’ en fifty,” he said, in explanation.
But their Honors never got the refusal of the
money.
Buying a Pig in a Poke.
THE proprietors of The Lone Hand are quite
aware that in inviting the Australian public
to send in, now, subscriptions to that publication,
they are asking them to “buy a pig in a poke,”
as the homely proverb puts it.
Of the character and merits of The Lone Hand
the public can know nothing, except what it
takes on trust. The date of publication has not
even been fixed, though it is now very near.
On the other hand, it’s “ just a slip of a pig ”
a twelve-months’ subscription is only 12s.—to buy
on trust, and it is offered by The Bulletin Co.,
which has never yet palmed off a poor beast on
the Australian public.
Still those who have sent in their subscriptions
in advance before publication make The Bul-
letin—which stands sponsor for The Lone
Hand —feel proud. They have come from all
parts of the Commonwealth, and from all classes
of society—from the Governor-General and the
Chief-Justice of the Commonwealth to the
shearer and the mechanic—from doctor, journalist,
and parson—and they represent a practical confi-
dence in the future of the new publication.
It is the intention of the proprietary to keep
these early subscribers in mind as The Lone
Hand progresses. As a beginning, among the
first subscribers will be allotted 50 “Founders’
Certificates,” entitling the holder to receive The
Lone Hand free for life. An effort will be made
to keep these Founders permanently linked to the
fortunes of the magazine until the days when its
early beginnings are matters of history.
The date of publication of The Lone Hand
will be announced shortly. The subscription is
12s. per annum post free.
THE LONE HAND
214 George-street.

INTERSTATE JEALOUSY.
Sydney, Monday.—The State Premier (Mr. Carruthers)
has received a letter from a well-known resident of Syd-
ney, now in London, in which the writer states that Vic-
toria is always to the fore, and N.S. Wales is not even
given an equal chance at the office of the Commonwealth.
He adds :—“ I saw the files of the Melbourne morning
papers, but not a Sydney morning paper was filed, or a
copy to he had. I asked myself, Is Victoria Australia ?
If not, why have N.S. Wales papers not the same pro-
minence as Victorian ? I think it is surely time N.S.
Wales woke up to her position in the Commonwealth.”—
Daily Paper.
The Man prom Sydney : “ Snubbed once morel
Now, I wonder why the dickens people never
respect me ? I’m alivays out of everything.”
When Queensland ex-Premier Philp opened
his arms to Premier Kidston’s late colleague,
Denham, he thought that a number of Inde-
gendent's would follow Digby in his retreat.
>ut no one has done so up to now, so Philp is only
one vote to the good, and is burdened with a
funereal butter-dealer, who will insist on having
a portfolio if ever Philp has portfolios to give
away. And Denham, by resigning because Kid-
ston wouldn’t coalesce with Philp, has clumsily
made it evident that Philp was quite ready to
coalesce with Kidston or with anybody so long as
he got his fork into the fleshpot.
The Victorian ex-Cohentingenters, some
dozen or so of whom meet annually to celebrate
their past misdeeds by a dinner, have struck a
medal bearing the inscription—“ South Africa,
1899—1902. For Empire.” But it’s wrong. The
Jews have no Empire. Perhaps the German
Empire is meant.
The Prussian Chamber of Agriculture is
pressing for the importation of Chinese coolies to
the German agricultural districts. High Protec-
tion has caused such a demand for labor in Ger-
many that there is danger of the crops being left
unharvested. Of course Chinese labor importa-
tion isn’t the remedy, but what is the remedy for
too much prosperity it is hard to say.
An unobtrusive advt. inviting applications
for 25 billets in the clerical department of N.S. W.
public service shows that another Joe Carruthers
reform has broken down. It was understood that,
by a process of transfer and re-arrangement, the
service was to struggle on for an indefinite period
without new appointments, despite the dropping-
out of the aged, drunk, dead, dissatisfied, and
infirm. But the civil servant’s salary rises auto-
matically to £l5O a year, and the policy of making
no new appointments brought about the dreadful
result that the office stamp-licker might advance
to £l5O a year, and still be licking stamps because
no junior stamp-licker had been appointed. So
the blessed reform sagged down in the middle
through its own weight.
Staggering statement made at a recent
meeting of the Vic. branch of the Immigration
League of Australia :
If there is no land in Victoria, the League, which
is Federal in its character, should indicate where land
is available in the other States.
“No land in Victoria.” And yet half-a-day
from Melbourne in a motor would take the
heaviest member of the League out on to the
beginning of the boundless western plains, and
he could follow them right away to the South
Australian border and travel the whole distance
over large estates. He could travel all day and
night, at top speed, and see nothing but land and
sheep. If he had a g«od telescope he might
occasionally see a man, although there are parts
of Ripon, Hampden and Grenville where the best
telescope would only show him more land and
extra sheep. All this land should be in the hands
of farmers ; and the counties named should have
ploughs sticking up all over them, and houses
everywhere, and children enough about to make
motoring dangerous. By the way, the Argus
humorously heads its account of this meeting
“ Land for the People.”
Premier and Captain Jack Evans of Tassy
has come back from his trip to Maoriland with
ideas. The trouble is. however, that he has also
come back with himself. He has brought back the
same old Jack. He wants to adopt a lot of Maori-
land’s progressive methods, and yetremain thesame
old Jack. He is in the position of the allegedly
“liberal” Launceston Examiner, which has a
fierce diatribe against the Tas. Upper House
every week, and points out that the Council is
always in the way of reform, and that it is quite
incurable ; and concludes by saying that it must
not be supposed for a moment that it would
abolish the Upper House, such a thing being con-
trary to precedent. That is Evans’s trouble. He
wants to do a whole lot of new and vigorous
things without shaking up any old precedent.
A few years ago, when the Labor party,
with misplaced generosity, allowed G. H. Reid to
run the N.S.W. Government, Pumpkin Smith
was Minister of Agriculture. In those days
Anti-Sosh was not in the
dictionary, and Pumpkin
Smith, grieved at the mess
Private Enterprise was
making of the dairy busi-
ness, decided on a Socialistic
move. The Government im-
ported a draft of prize cattle,
to be used in improving the
local herds, and Private En-
terprise howled with anger
at the idea. On the arrival
of the stock, Private Enter-
prise shouted with derision.
The beasts were too big, and
too small, and too square, and
too round; they were ill-
bi'ed; also they were the
wrong color, shape and age ;
likewise they were dead.
This lamentation of Jeremiah
the Farmer lasted until the
animals came out of quaran-
tine. Then there was a rush
for their services. Since that
date their progeny have
swept the board at the local
shows, and the recent great success of a grandson
of one of the imported animals at the Berry
show has started a cry for further Socialistic
interference in the Cow question.
Iceberg Irvine continues to make speeches
at smolce concerts on the questions of the day, and
generally to act as though he were the leader of
the party that is struggling to “establish a sound
and stable form of government.” And George
Reid makes never a speech, nor gives a sign of
taking the slightest interest in this country and
its grand, vast natural resources. Either his con-
tempt for Irvine is beyond words, or he has
practically thrown up the sponge.
Westralia gaily floats a kite for another
million in London, and a further £1,500,000 is
authorised. If Price and Kidston, who are both
careful financiers, can hold their positions in
S.A. and Queensland for a few years longer, it
seems likely that Westralia will beat them in the
race for the ownership of the Biggest Debt per
Inhabitant on Earth. Yet Westralia should be
the last place to require more loans. Even after
paying interest on its present debt, it has a
public revenue of over £l2 per head per annum.
There is nothing else to equal it on the surface
of the globe, and if Westralia can’t save the price
of a beer or a few public works out of that sum,
there is a bitter time ahead when the Golden
Mile fades.

Mort’s Dock, Sydney, reckons it can build
the torpedo boats that Australia wants, and use

tile vessels. There is no visible reason why it
shouldn’t build boats quite equal to the imported
