Saul among the prophets ! Last week
Melbourne Argus, after praising Newport State
workshops to the extent of a column and a
turnover, hove the following off its withered
bosom :
The possibilities of Newport are illimitable. Some
day, when occasion and the East arises, it may have to
turn its attention to other things than railway work. “To
make small arms,” says the manager (Mr. T. Smith), “ we
want the machinery for rifling the barrels, and that we
would have to import. But as for turning out big guns,
as they did at Kimberley, why, we could start on one
to-morrow.”
Granny Argus forgets that last December
Foreign trade was the only wear, and such a
thing as an Australian small arms factory merely
a wild chimerical dream of those Socialists whom
it behoved all good men to repudiate with a
shudder.
“Podargus” makes some sweeping asser-
tions :
A full Treasury blinds the public of Victoria to the
weakness of its more than average stupid Government.
Tommy Bent is merely a boorish man with a thick bull
neck, an unrivalled knowledge of political tactics, and a
marked trait for rewarding his friends and never for-
getting a good turn. Bent is a physical leader, with
much moral courage and a very low order of intellect.
Put in power by the Reform party, he has not instituted
a single reform, and, on the contrary, has largely in-
creased the number of officials and the public salary list.
Rent’s J.’sP. are as plentiful in Victoria as snobs at a
Government House ball. Every Government department
in Victoria is in a state of chaos, the worst, perhaps,
being the Mines, presided over by Donald M’Leod, a
doddery septuagenarian who was for 40 years town clerk
of a decayed mining hamlet. Donald has an unparalleled
knowledge of the fiscal value of a village pound, or of the
municipal dog-tax, while about thistles, stinkweed, and
street lighting he knows all there is to be learnt. The
Mining Department is short of surveyors and scientific
officers, no geological surveys are proceeding, no fresh
plans are being issued, leases take
from six to twelve months to issue,
and the Minister’s decisions are
always parochial and never for the
good of mining. Why E. H. Cameron
is permitted to act as Minister of
Public Works surpasses all reason-
able understanding. Cameron is in
the eighties, and has been a quiet,
weak, dull member of the Assembly
for over 30 years. In that time, he
has never made one sensible speech
nor made one business-like sugges-
tion. His department is at sixes and
sevens, and the officials act on
their own responsibility. Conse-
quently mistakes, and not small
ones, are constantly being made.
A big one is the blunder over the
Waranga Basin, which will have to
be re-made. Still Cameron doses
and dodders, and can always be
found in his office on the last day
of the month. Another Minister of
the mesozoic order is old Tommy
Langdon, who has never been known
to do or say anything of value during
his long career as one of Duncan
Gillies’ Parliamentary “old hats.”
Langdon is now Chief Secretary,
and his chief charge, the police force, is in a maze
of a mess from which Tommy is powerless to ex-
tract it. Sachse is Minister of Education, and the
necessary educational reforms in the Victorian school
system are at a standstill awaiting Mr. Sachse’s retirement,
j. M. Davies, Minister of Justice, is an elderly solicitor who
has won a name for much wisdom by holding his tongue
and looking- as wise as Lord Brougham. The Titles Office is
in a deplorable condition, the police magistracy is filled
with cranks, the Law Department suffers from clogged
machinery, the Probate office is run by dotards, and the
cost of the Attorney-General’s and Solicitor-General’s
staffs expands yearly. J. E. Mackay has yet to win his
spurs as Minister of Lands, for he took over a depart-
ment which has become a bye-word for ineptitude,
thanks to lazy Jack Murray. George Swinburne is the
only really able man in the Bent Cabinet. Shrewd, in-
tellectual, and enterprising, he just saves it from being
known as the weakest and stupidest Ministry Victoria
has so far had to endure.
That’s something like a curse.
A whoop from Melbourne :
The annual picnic of Melbourne postal and telegraph
employees was perpetrated last Saturday for the exas-
peration of the public in general. The G.P.O. was mostly
closed at 10 a.m., the money-order office was graciously
permitted to remain open until 10.30 a.m. A number of
minor post-offices within the Melbourne radius were
closed altogether at 10 a.m., and the ordinary mid-day
mails to the suburbs were suspended. And all because
of an annual picnic which most of the holiday-making
postal and telegraph employees don’t attend. If
they all attended it, the “ function ” would be none the
less a scandalous wrong to the community that continues
to endure it without protest. No public purpose can be
served by taking hundreds of public servants off duty on
one particular Saturday, when the rest of the world is
working and expecting letters as us ;al. With the ex-
ception of half-a-dozen departmental officers who loom
large on this lamentable occasion, all the free-picnicked
employees, as well as the others who picnic privately or
don’t picnic at all, would rather be given a whole day off
in small detachments than be collectively relieved of less
than a day’s work. This trip down the Bay seems to have
originated in the Melbourne boom time, when every man
who could pose as a representative person and get him-
self reported in the newspapers, was bent on promoting
festive occasions for his little burst of oratory.
Japan preserves some semblance of the
promised Open Door for trade at its
newly-acquired Manchurian ports, but
there is a big pit inside the door,
and the foreigner falls therein!
By a combination of mys-
terious circumstances that
the stranger can never get
to the bottom of, it costs
about four times as much
to unload cargoes from a
white-owned ship as from
a Jap one. Then there is
the Jap tariff to argue with.
And finally the white man’s
goods pay from 50 to 100 per cent, higher rates
on the inland railway journey than Jap goods
do. When all these things are added up the
white man’s burden is more than he can bear,
and he loathes the eight of a Jap.
The Adelaide Tramway Trust has made a
good start. It has decided that Australasia can
supply quite as good an engineer as can be had
anywhere for the £1250 a year which it proposes
to pay, and it isn’t inviting applications outside
the Commonwealth and Maoriland.
Yellow Pup spent a pleasant evening on
February 12, chawing a bone in the privacy of its
kennel on the occasion of the annual banquet.
After reading apologies from Geo. Reid and Joe
Carruthers, Yellow Pup was unanimous in de-
ploring the introduction of sectarianism into
politics,' and decided that the recent losses
sustained by its champion, Geo. Reid, hap-
pened because Cardinal Moran ordered the
Catholics to vote on the other side. Having
decided that the R.C. element had dominated
the election, Yellow Pup barked with delight
when its president described the same election as
a glorious Protestant victory, because the number
of R.C.’s in the Australian Parliament had not
increased. About this time the Pup got dizzy
with pursuing its own tail, so it disentangled
itself and went home.
The wail of an “ Importer ” in a recent
Herald :
There is a perfect organisation at the Commonwealth
Customs-houses for recording and communicating the
peccadilloes of traders in the matter of their invoices at
each port. If an importer be caught in any of the multi-
tudinous nets spread for him by the Customs Act and
regulations, or if there is any suspicion as to the genuine-
ness of his documents, the information is immediately
entered in the “black book” and sent to the various
Customs-houses as an eternal record against him.
This is one of the best things we’ve read about
the Customs administration for a long while. But
that “perfect organisation ” must be a bit trying
to swindlers.
The sort of speech (to a crowd of country
land-owning cadgers) that makes The Bulletin
forgive Bent most of his sins—even his songs
It is “Give!” “Give!” all the time. I do not care
how much they circularise, I will not spend money to
improve private property.... These country shires get
a grant, lately increased from £50,000 to £76,000; they
get a special grant of £20,000, and they get a licensing
equivalent of £90,000. They have the unused roads fees—
now they want flood damages.... They pay a wretched
Is., or even 9d., municipal rate, while the suburbs stump
up 2s. and 2s. 6d.-—and they want more 1 They can take
my billet themselves if they like. The country owes a lot
of money yet.
THE GARRISON.
Where has there been such development in any
country? We have one-and-a-half persons to every
square "mile. —Prominent Politician.
In Victoria T. Bent steadily sets his large
feet in the back track. He has abolished Irvine’s
separate representation of public servants. He
has nearly abolished the deficit. He promises to
abolish the shameful act of economy which
reduced the maximum old age pension from 10s.
to Bs. per week. In all these things he has done
well. And now (alas !) he threatens to take the
back track to the borrowing days, and float a
million loan. Some people don’t know when they
have had enough back track.
S.M. Herald is joyfully celebrating the
German 'Kaiser’s ‘ ‘ victory, ” which consists in his
Government being directly supported by the third
strongest of 16 parties in a Legislature of 39/
members. But Deakin’s return in Australia with
a direct following of about one-third and a policy
following of two-thirds of the Parliament is a
disastrous defeat. Still, it is reckoned that the
Kaiser, by a fusion of parties, may have a majority
in support of one big item of his policy—that oi
building a huge fleet in red-hot haste and smash-
ing Britain on the sea before the peaceful, torpid
0.-B. Government manages to revolve on its slow
and friendly axis and realise the situation. And
that's what the Herald is cheering about !
A RHYME OP DEAD STATESMEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where have the godlike statesmen gone
Who lived of old
And made the mould
Of Egypt and of Babylon ?
Where is that Moses who so well
Did dare God’s frown
And bring Him down
To make the nation Israel ?
Where are the Judges, mighty men,
Who in the gate
Kept law and state,
And made each man a citizen ?
Where have the godlike statesmen gone?
Where’s Rameses
Of victories ?
And where is royal Solomon ?
Where is that sage who left behind
On slabs of clay
Laws that, to-day,
Are fresh and new for humankind ?
Whe e is Lycurgus, calm of brow,
And Draco, stern ?
Each in his urn,
Or grave, lies very lonely now.
Where is that Lord of Light and Ease
Who wreathed his town
With violet crown ?
Ah, where indeed, is Pericles?
Where is Augustus, proud and pale,
Who made of Rome
Earth’s marble dome
That now is but a Pontiff’s gaol ?
Where is that Dietrich Slash-and-Hew,
The Ostrogoth,
Who with new cloth
Would make the Roman purple new ?
Justinian has gone before
With all his laws,
Code, pandect, clause, —
And where is William Conqueror ?
Ye who would rush red-fanged for Fame,
And then thereby,
Through Fraud and Lie,
Gain pompous nonsense of a Name—
Not one, when all thy work’s complete,
Of all thy kith
Dare sleep thee with
One night beneath the winding sheet.
V. J. DALEY.

Government Notice.
CLOSER SETTLEMENT ACT.
Gobbagombalin*Marrar Estates
Land District of Wagga Wagga.
NEAR THE THRIVING TOWN AND BUSINESS CENTRE OF WAGGA WAGGA.
HE above Estates, recently purchased by the State Government, have been subdivided into
184 FARMS
which become Available for Application
under the provisions of the Closer Settlement Act of 1904, and the Amended Act of 1906, on
18th MARCH, 1907.
38 of these farms may be the subject of a preferent claim to apply by tenant farmers on the Estate,
The farms range in area from 59 acres to 964 g acres, and in value from £2 2s. to £l4 10s. per acre.
Full particulars and directions for applying, together with Lithographs and descriptions of the
various farms, may be obtained free of cost on application by post or otherwise to any Crown Land
Agent in New South Wales, or the Information Bureau, Department of Lands, Sydney.
The Estates are situated midway between Sydney and Melbourne, and are in close proximity to
the important town of Wagga Wagga, and Railway "Stations of Harefield, Coolamon, Marrar, and
Bomen. No part of the land is beyond 15 miles from, and the greater part is within 10 miles of, a
Railway Station.
The climate is good and very healthy. The average rainfall is about 22 inches per annum.
A number of the farms have frontage to the Murrumbidgee River ; a large number are pro-
vided with supplies of water from tanks and wells, while others have excellent facilities for con-
servation of water. The land is all good, sound, grazing country, suitable for sheep or cattle, while
the bulk of it is eminently suitpd for wheat ; growing. The frontage lands offer special facilities
for dairy farming. The land is also suitable for orchards apd vineyards.
Terms: —5 per cent, deposit, balance (including principal and interest) in annual payments
of 5 per cent., spread over a period of 38 years.
Persons who lodge applications for any of the farms referred to herein will be allowed a re-
fund of half the rail-fare paid by them on their visit of inspection, and on any journeys they
may require to make to appear before the Land Board in connection with their application.
Successful applicants, their families, and such belongings, including live stock, as were in
their possession immediately prior to the making of the application for land, will be carried at
half rates, provided that, in the case of live stock, it be not more than sufficient for the land
acquired.
Persons desirous of obtaining concessions in respect of railway fares, may obtain a form from
any Crown Land Agent in the State, which must be given to the Railway Station-master when
obtaining a ticket, or if unable to obtain a form, must ask the Railway Station-master to take
their name and address, amount paid, and number of ticket.
5a116 ROBERT McDONALD, Acting Under Secretary for Lands.

At all the Leading- Centres of Australia and New Zealand.
Lance Skuthorp, the greatest horseman the
world has ever seen, the champion Australian buck-jump
rider, is now touring the western parts of Queensland,
making for New South Wales, expecting to open in
Sydney at Easter. His show consists of 150 animals, all
buckjumpers of the highest class, amongst them being
the two greatest buck-jumpers that were ever created,
Frisco and Snips. Both these horses can he backed to
throw any man in the world out of the best saddles they
can produce.*

SPORTING
NOTIONS
As a rule, there is a suspicion in benefit matches
that both sides play with a view to scoring at the gate
in preference to the board, but in that tendered to
Gregory 7, both sides seem to have been doing their level
best. The spectators had the unusual exhibition of a
N.S.W. XI. being thoroughly crumpled up. In the
first innings, N.S.W. was dismissed for 256, the Rest
ringing frequent changes on the bowling. At one time
it looked as though N.S.W. would not reach the second
hundred, eight having fallen for 137. But Carter and
Garnsey got together, and, the former playing a great
innings, they lifted the score to 256 before Garnsey
was caught off Bowden. Of the 7 bowlers, the most suc-
cessful was Wright, who took four N.S.W. wickets for 81.
Bowden and Armstrong also took a wicket each at a cost
of 13 and 17 runs respectively,
However, the remark was, “Wait till N.S.W.
gets ’em at the sticks, and then see what a mess there
will be.” With two wickets down for eight, it certainly
did look that way. But Vernon Ransford came along,
and for 3} hours defied the bowling, striking 16 fours,
and making-136. Then one of Cotter’s cannon-balls came
along and smote him on the lips, causing him to knock
down his wicket. It was just a fluke that Ransford’s
face wasn’t smashed beyond repair. Saturday’s play
ended with The Rest having scored 356 for the loss of six
wickets. On Monday 7 the N.S. W. bowling was thoroughly
collared, and The Rest made 544 before the N.S.W.
bowlers could get them out.
•Back again on the timber patch, N.S.W. seemed
in a fairly hopeless position, and at the close of play on
Monday had lost five wickets for 92. It was not a case of
winning, but of avoiding defeat by an innings. On Tuesday
the sixth wicket fell for 102. Then E. L. Waddy came along,
and things brightened somewhat, and before the seventh
wicket fell the score had climbed to 204. After that
trouble came again. Macartney 7 had not recovered suffi-
ciently from his injuries to play, so N.S.W. was a bat
short, and couldn’t afford it. Another eight runs
were notched, and down went the eighth wicket.
Waldy’s innings of 129 was distinctly good. He
bested the bowling for 115 minutes, and recorded
14 fours. He was N.S. W.’s last hope, and before Bowden
bo vied him he and Carter lifted the score to 312. With
Waddy’s dismissal the innings ended. So the Rest of
Australia only required 25 to win, and had a whole
innings in which to get them, and the thing was over
bar miracles.
* * * *
Trumper having dropped out of the team for
Westralia, A. J. Hopkins takes his place, which is good
news for the Westralians. Whatever Trumper may have
been in the past, and may 7 be again, he is at the present
time not worth going across the street to see. He made
11 in the inter-State match, and again last week, in
N.S.W. versus the Rest of Australia, he made nine in
the first innings and three in the second. And this is
the gentleman who objected to accompany 7 the team to
Westralia because he considered it weak and unrepresen-
tative 1 Certainly it is a lot stronger since he kindly
eliminated himself, and made way for that s erling bats-
man Hopkins. In the light of his receno performances
on the field Trumper’s other performances are amusing,
and if he had only some glimmering of humor he would
go away into a corner and smile at himself.
* * * •*
What promised to be a noble cricketing record
came to a sudden end, the other Saturday. On a patch
of ground in South Melbourne, a junior batsman named
Castlehow in one over smote the six balls across the
fence for six each. Then he planted the first ball of the
next over in a passing St. Kilda train, and it didn’t come
back. That stopped the play.
* * * *
Fighting Billy M‘Elhone having returned
from his honeymoon, N.S.W. Cricket Association admin-
istered a well-deserved snub to the alleged Citizens’
Committee, which is trying to bluff the Associa-
tion into unconditionally lifting the disqualifications
from the disloyal cricketers. The committee’s letter was
received and ignored. The Association will do no more ;
any of the disqualified cricketers who wish to have the
embargo lifted will have to make personal application.
* * * *
Adelaide is having two keen controversies on
umpiring at cricket. In one case, mid-off appealed for
leg-before, and the umpire declared himself “insulted.”
The other was more important. A wide was called, but
the batsman jumped out at it, and the long-armed wicket-
keeper gathered it in and stumped him. The not-out
decision seems to be correct, as the batsman was clearly
not trying to run, and so could not be run out.
Clem Hill, in the Rest of Australia XI. to give
the side a rep. flavor, was the funny man on Saturday.
Clem is as round as a barrel, and out of form, and
naturally failed to time fast bowler Cotter in the early
part of his innings ; but he usually brought the bat down
with a resounding smack about a minute too late. The
turn was enjoyed—it was hilarious, and in thorough
keeping with the seriousness of the match. Clem Hill, it
is stated, intends to hang up the flannels for good at the
end of this cricket season.
The enervating effects of the summer have not
troubled the Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union, which
has secured an all-round reduction in tiie prices of two of
the grounds. The Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees have
hitherto demanded 20 per cent, of the takings. The
Union considered that the fact that it got nothing from
the army of members entitled it to a lower figure, and
resolutely refused to play on the ground at the price.
Now the Trustees have come to the conclusion that it is
better to meet the Union’s wishes, and have reduced the
price to 12 per cent. So during the coming season, which
starts on the last Saturday of April instead of the first in
May, as in previous years, the best match of the week will
be played at the Cricket Ground. This arrangement will
not interfere with the agreement arrived at with the
Sports Ground, which will also have a grade match every
Saturday, for which the Union guarantees a minimum
sum for five years on condition the Trust erects an up-
to-date and commodious grand stand. The Union has
also secured a reduction in the price of the University
Oval, in Camperdown district. Altogether, the financial
prospects of the Union are much more rosy than they
were last year.
• * * *
The N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union (the amateur body)
unearthed a couple of new champions at Sydney Sports’
Ground on Saturday afternoon. The Half-Mile Champion-
ship produced a brilliant sprint, in which Schultz and
Berry, of Queensland, were passed and beaten by W. S.
Knox and H. K. Maxwell, of N.S.W. Knox won by a
wheel in lmin. 34 4-ssec. The Ten Miles Championship
produced an enormous field, no less than 17 going
out after the trophy. Only two men tried to get a break
on the field, about half-way through the race, and when
they were collared the field settled down to do a crawl
till the last mile was entered upon. Then a struggle
commenced. At the bell, Watson and Schultz went
down with a clang. The field got past without falling
over them, and McLean went to the front, only to be cut
down by Shepherd. Berry came past both of them.
Then Gannon shot up beside him, and cut him down to
the edge of the track, and the Queensland crack, unable
to get through, hadn’t enough steam left to go round.
Next, Maxwell sprang from the bunch, and, taking a
desperate chance, swung betw'een Berry and Gannon as
they momentarily opened out. Gannon made a frantic
effort to head Maxwell, but the latter lifted his machine
over the line by about tw 7 o inches, the order being—
Maxwell, Gannon (both of N.S.W.), and Berry 7 and
Atherton (both of Queensland). It was fine racing 7 , and
if the amateurs can be relied upon to put up finishes like
those, the sooner they start track-racing 7 the better.
People will roll up to see that sort ot entertainment.
* * * *
The first two heats of the Horder-Mutton
cycle match duly transpired at Sydney Sports Ground on
Monday night last. In the first heat over the quarter-mile
Horder showed a large contempt for his rival, and the
result of his overweening confidence was that Mutton did
a sudden jump half a lap from home, and streaked away
at such a pace that Horder w as left half a length behind
at the finish. In the second heat, over half a mile,
Horder was very wide awake, and, although Mutton led
by 7 two lengths at half a lap to go, when he made his
jump he found Horder had anticipated him and jumped
first. After a strenuous sprint Horder w 7 on by half a
length. This made a deciding sprint over half a mile
necessary, and it will be run off next Monday night.
* * * *
The Motor-Cycle Race (ljmiles) for members of
the Pioneer Motor Club of N.S.W., w 7 as the exciting item
at the Cyclists’ Association meeting, at Sydney Sports
Ground, last Monday night. In the first heat four
started, and Powell, on a 4h.p. motor, won by 30yds.
from the 390yds. mark. The second heat was delirious.
Doherty w 7 as on the 80yds. mark, and drove his machine
recklessly 7. Witbam, the scratch man, soared up along-
side, and Doherty drove him up the hank. If
Witham got ahead it was all up. The other three
were not in it. Round and round the two leaders
flew, Witham trying desperately to get round
ahead of Doherty, and the other driving him up the
bank on every occasion. It was great generalship,
but whether it was fair writer doesn’t know, not having
seen the rules they raced under. After a desperate con-
test over three laps Doherty 7 showed he had still an ounce
or so of reserve power left, for as Witham was just pre-
paring to cut down in front of him, Doherty’s machine,
with a bound, cleared the line half a length to the good.
It was a display of reckless speed and cool, perfect
handling of the machines. The mile and a half w 7 as
covered in 2min. 4 l-ssec. In the final F. Daniels, with
440vds. handicap, was never overtaken, and Witham had
his revenge, beating Doherty by 7 quite a distance for
second place.
* * * *
The Prospect Cycling Carnival, to be held over
the good, bad, and indifferent roads around Sale, in
Gippsland (Vic.), from the 15th to the 28th of this month,
is a big affair, and looms up alongside the Warrnnmbool
to Melbourne road race in importance. There are seven
road races and a number of track events, and the whole
has been organised with a viexv to bringing the district
before the public eye and advertising it as a tourist resort.
The promoter, J. McLachlan, who is in business in Sale,
will distribute about £5OO in cash prizes, and these will
be competed for by about 200 cyclists from all the Aus-
tralian States. Tw 7 o of the road races—from Saie to
Bairnsdale, and from Traralgon to Sale, 44 and 52 miles
respectively—carry first prizes of £lOO each. Tents are
to be erected to accommodate the expected rush of visi-
tors, and the meeting is to wear—as far as possible—the
aspect of a huge picnic. The ided of promoting big
sporting events to popularise a tow 7 n is very much in
vogue in U.S.A. ;an example w 7 as the £6OOO purse which
the town of Rhyolite offered to put up for a
fight between Jeffries and the next best man, Squires
preferred. The real estate owners and the mining
magnates were the people who offered to elevate the
money, and they hoped to get it all back, just as does any
other man who spends a lot of money on advertisement.
Of course, anything is possible in the States, but this sort
of thing is a new departure in Australia.
In motoring circles they are talking about a car
Reliability Test from Brisbane to Adelaide, or vice versa.
It is argued that to do a thousand miles careering round
practically the same road is not much of a test, whereas a
straight run round practically 7 the whole of inhabiled
Eastern Australia would throw some light on what motor
cars could really do in war time. The distance is,
approximately 7, 1850 miles, and includes some of the best
and some of the very worst of Australian roads. The
absolutely 7 w 7 orst patch is a stretch of pathless sand and
tussock betw'een Kingston and Meningie in South Aus-
tralia, but as it has already 7 been traversed, and by
a woman, it is not impossible. Mrs. Thompson, of Ade-
laide, crossed it some years ago in a single cylindered
Wolseley 7 car. Between Sydney 7 and Brisbane the moun-
tain climbing capabilities of the cars would be tested to
the uttermost. This sort of thing is expensive, and it
has been suggested that good larg'e money 7 prizes should
be given. But the trouble is that it is not the people
whose cars got through who would most w 7 ant the
money, but those who had to abandon hundreds of
pounds’ worth of valuable automobiles down precipices
and in muddy gullies. And they vvould get nothing.
* * * «
The trouble about previous motor reliability
tests has been that no points were allowed for the ear
that arrived in the best working order. The wounded
mobile that just managed to crawl in on its last legs, so
to speak, scored equally with the one that roared in at
top speed without a wheeze in its breathing department.
In the next reliability race points ought to be deducted
for having the engine in a damaged state, or gasping out
its last strokes.
* * * *
The Sydney Royal Yacht Squadron’s ocean
race for the Gascoigne Cup attracted more than usual
interest on Saturday. Rawhiti, which had won La
Carabine Cup in Melbourne, w 7 as back and pitted against
that new boat, Awanui, which made such a startling run
on its maiden trip. Brockhoff's boat was called upon to
give the newcomer Bmin. 9sec. handicap, but Awanui
romped down the harbor at -such a rate that it had
gained nearly three minutes on the handicap at the ocean
mark. On the return journey Rawhiti walked through
the water at a splendid pace, overhauling boat after boat,
and leaving them standing still, but Awanui and Heather
flew on before, and although Rawhiti gained steadily 7 at the
winning line, Awanui, which had hoisted no extras, was
still 6min. 48sec. to the good. It is doubtful if Raw'hiti
w 7 ould have secured second place had not the club
steamer, Bobbie Burns, who was probablyrin his usual state,
interfered with Norman Murray’s chance for second by
steaming along in Heather’s wake and depriving the boat
of her wind. It w’as not till off Bradley’s that Rawhiti
overtook Murray 7 ’s yacht, and at the finish secured No. 2
position by the narrow margin of 16sec.
* ■* * *
There’s not a yacht in Sydney harbor half so
buoyant as the Gascoigne Cup. It has been floating
round ever since 1887, and, by the look of things, will go
on floating round when the present generation of yachts
and yachtsmen are gone and dead. It has to be won
three times in succession to be acquired outright, and so
far only two men have managed to get in even the second
leg. Messrs. Dempster and Bremner each annexed it
two y 7 ears running, only to drop as far out as ever at the
third time of trying. It is like the Maoriland lease in
perpetuity 7, guaranteed to go on for 999 years without a
break.
* w * *
The 100yds. ladies’ swimming championship of
Victoria result d in a great struggle against wind and
water at the Brighton baths on Saturday afternoon.
After a cold, unnecess ry wait the event got a move on.
From an even start Dolly 7 Cooper was the first to show
out ahead of Julie Smith, Hilda Bradley and co., and the
leader stayed in front till the 50yds. turn. There
the field got on equal terms to battle home
against a heavy cross-sea. It U'as anybody’s race up till
syds. from the tape, where Hilda shot out and won from
Doily, with Julie and Connie a dead heat for third place.
A sheet of paper could have covered the four if the paper
were large enough. Miss Bradley did the 100yds. in
lmin. 34sec.
*■■#■!**
Melbourne’s diver and swimmer, Alec. ,Lamb,
last w 7 eek swam seven miles from Hawthorn Bridge to
Princes Bridge in 3hr. smin., starting with a high dive
(68ft.) from Hawthorn Bridge, and plunging down from
every bridge en route, bar one where no firm foothold
could be got—eight bridges in all, of an average height
of 51ft. He felt no ill effects, except in the last mile,
when exhaustion and the extra-cool semi-salt water
began to tell. He ripped his costume when he struck the
water at the first, and highest, plunge ; but that was the
worst that happened.
The Melbourne speedway, a strip of land sliced
from Albert Park, returns to its old condition as part of
the people’s park, the club, after spending about £lOOO,
having discovered that, beyond a few Canadian enthu-
siasts, no one took any interest in the speeding of fast
trotters.
* * * *
As a sample of the stuff that confiding punters
are fed on, take this from Melb. Sporting and Dramatic
News :—“ ’Tis scarcely 7 likely that Istria was specially
wound up for this engagement, and, as she had not
carried silk for nearly a year, it stands to reason that
she was a little short of condition.” Which is merely a
variation of the bad old doctrine : “ A horse needs one or
two gallops in public to complete its preparation for a
big race.” As though the ptesetice of a few thousand
people on the course made an.v difference in the
physical effect of a racing gallop! Revenue had
not “carried silk” for two years befoie he reap-
peared in public and won the Melbourne Cup, Play-
away 7 didn’t “ carry silk ” for six months before her vic-
tory in the Newmarket. Yet a turf scribe has the gall to
argue that Istria must have been short of condition w'hen
Decollete beat her the other Saturday 7. Both mares are
wanted for the forthcoming Newmarket ; therefore the
winner probably 7 was not more “w'ound up” than the
defeated favorite. Moreover, the winner had not raced
in public for some months. The performance was the
best of evidence that Decollete in the meantime has
made more improvement than Istria,Who beat her on
two or three occasions last autumn.
* * * *
Last Saturday’s racing at Caulfield threw little
light on the New'market Handicap, the field for the Oak-
leigh Plate being rather weak. Beresina managed to
win, but itw 7 as not a brilliant performance, and on that
“form” the other light-weight mare (Red Clover)should
beat the penalised Beresina at Flemington over straight
six furlongs. The second Saturday of the Caulfield meet-
ing should have more instructive results than anything
seen last week.
* * * *
If Ellis were a stayer (which he probably is not)
the colt’s defeat of May 7 King and Antonious for the St.
George’s Stakes would give him a first-rate chance in the
Australian Cup. A better test of stamina was applied to
Mira and Realm when they fought out the finish of the
Bond Cup (11 furlongs). Allowing that one oi two of the
badly beaten ones were not showing any spur marks
after the race, it was, nevertheless, clear that Mira did
remarkably well in doing better than Realm. The little
mare is by Malvolio, and, like some other members of the
family, she is only occasionally fit. Just now must be
one of the occasions.
* * *
Since the Pastime Club began to grow so
luxuriantly in the Sydney suburbs, it has become
advisable to cultivate a peaceful expression and a charm-
ing manner. Brusqueness or a tendency to talk stoush
is liable to bring' trouble from the most unexpected
quarters. The burly man who relies on his weight
and war like appearance is having a bad time. At
Bondi, last week, 15-stone of aggressive smoker, who
refused to shut down his volcano when requested by an
undersized conductor, was violently thrown out of the
car, and laid out with two on the point and a stomach
rip. In Oxford-street, recently 7, a policeman who was
being badly mauled by a couple of toughs, was rescued
h.Y (ye gods!) a tailor. He was small, hut he hewed a
way through those two toughs like a needle through
shoddy, and cutting himself loose from his base, skirled
round in a manner that suggested a kinematograph
show. In William-street a barber attacked a powerful-
looking individual, whom he accused of ruining his sister,
and floored him as fast as he got up. He also floored a
tram conductor and driver who attempted to mitigate
, the butchery 7, and finally retired in good order in the
face of overwhelming numbers, leaving the alleged
seducer with a broken nose, a much-wrecked face, and a
ruined suit. But to give him his due, the Pastime Club
product does not appear to be a brawler, and in every
case w 7 here he has come before the public he seems to
have had right and decency on his side.
* * * *
The champion bantam, Ted Green, met the
featherweight, Thomas Hanley, at Sydney Gaiety Hall
on Saturday night last, and between them they put up
eight beautiful rounds. In the language of the sport, it
was very willing. Then Hanley began to weary ; but in
the tenth round he brightened up, and made a whirling
effort to finish the conflict in short order. His frantic
efforts w 7 ere met with steady, hard hitting, and w 7 hen the
long r lly that had constituted the round w 7 as over the
featherweight was a very 7 exhausted midget. Green, too,
showed signs of wear and tear about the boiler section,
but had still enough steam left to smite Hanley consider-
ably. The feather saved himself as much as possible for
a final effort, which he made in the 14th round. But it
was more disastrous than his previous rally, and after
being laid out three times in rapid succession,"his seconds
raised the towel of defeat above the horizon.
The meeting: between Cripps, of Sydney and
Ed. Williams, of Victoria, for the middle-weight cham
pionship and a percentage of the gate, at Melbourne
Cyclorama Hall on Monday night, was marked by ex
treme caution on both sides in the beginning. As the
scrap wore on the men increased the vigor of their
attack, and from the 16th to the finish the
was very heavy. Cripps confined his attack to the body
dealing sledgehammer blows on ribs and diaphragm
while Williams smote sullenly away at the top end. in
the 17th round a riot was nearly provoked by Williams
back-heeling the Waler, but as Cripps did not appeal
referee Basto took no notice. As they grew excited
Williams began to hit wild, while Cripps kept his head
very well, and hit hard and often where it, would take
the most etfect. Although his left cheek was laid open
and his body showed signs of having received some bat-
tering, Williams kept going, and looked as though able
to last another 20 rounds, but when the limit was
reached there was no question as to the justice of Paddy
Basto’s decision. P.B. gave Cripps the fight on points. ‘
* * * ■*
Bob Whitelaw, of New South Wales, versus
Jock M‘lvor, of the Bent State, at Melbourne Cyclorama
on Monday (11th), was an ill-advised arrangement, and
lasted 2J rounds, when M‘lvor received the culminatiii"
punch, and was on the floor for lOsec., shaking his head
and apparently wondering if a brick wall had fallen on
him. Lately Victorian lightweights have been pitted
against several bulky Sydne.v-siders like Whitelaw and
Monty Andrews, and it is time the Olympic Club ds-
covered the vanity of such fixtures. Whitelaw is aboxer
to put up against welters and middles of the second or
third degree, not against lightweights of the medium
order like MTvor, who could not get within the range of
the big Sydney-sider’s machine-like left. The hit that
finished Mac. was a historical smite. , The large crowd
departed, as on the previous Monday, having sein
nothing worth mentioning for its money. This is the result
of injudicious matching, and the multitude, by takingiis
disappointments peacefully and coming again, demon-
strates a fervid interest in the game, which deserves more
consideration.
* * V *
Bill Squires had an experience with a would-be
pug named Gamble, while showing recently at Ballarat.
Gamble stands 6ft. 3in. high, and with outstretched arms
spans the phenomenal distance of 6ft. 9in. But he has
a soft spot in his head, and this caused him to believe
that he could knock out the champion. Of course
Squires was not desirous of risking a fluke that might
make a standing joke against him on both sides of the
Pacific, and he kept, the big yokel at a safe distance.
Gamble, who had been jumping around like a fighting
’roo, at last rushed on to Bill’s brawny fist, and fell
heavily, on the stage. It was half-an-hour before Squires
was sure of escaping a charge of fool-slaughter.
■* * * *
Willie Squires was given a complimentary
slugging social at the Cyclorama on Wednesday night el
last week, to enable Melbourne to express approxal of
the departing champion, and present him with a large
leather hag embossed with brass in which to carry borne
his American winnings. Orators hoped the bag would
not be big enough ; so did William. In his farewell
speech he was" a deeply-affected spectacle. Bill,
having removed his cap from his impressive head, said:
“Gen’l’men, I never say much. It’s not my way; but
I’m always out for keeps.” Thus, proud of his way of
not saying much, Bill lapsed into silence, and 4000 cit-
izens concluded 1 heir volley 7 of applause by roaring, “For
he’s a jolly good fellow.” Here was a Bill to be adored-
a typical boy of the bill-dog breed !
■* * * *
The navigation of the air begins to take form.
Roosevelt has purchased from the Wright Brothers their
aeroplane contrivance, with which they 7 are said to have
flown safely for 30 miles before something went wrong
and brought the contraption to the earth with an awful
smash, the navigators escaping by means of parachutes.
The bucolics Who attempted to examine the thing were
kept at a respectable distance with revolvers till the
brothers removed the parts that embodied the secret,
and drove them aw-ay in a cart. So far, all that is known
of the Wright air-vehicle is that it is cigar-shaped,with
huge aeroplanes protruding from the sides, and looks
like a magnified cockroach blown up by an explosion on
thekitchen hearth. The French military 7 authorities have
got a dirigible balloon, which they have called the
“ Patrie,” and which, with a crew 7 of three military men
(in gorgeous uniforms and gold braid) and two mechanics,
manoeuvred over Paris several days for the space of 50min,
per day 7. All sorts of capers and evolutions were described
to show that the thing w 7 as absolutely under control, and
finally it stopped w'hile an officer in a blue coat and red
pants lowered a rope with an official report to the com-
mander of the forces in Paris, and hauled up permission
to take the Pegasus back to the stable. The question is
how much lyddite can the thing carry.

“ The Croaker ” : There’s trouble ahead of tie
promoters who reckon on big gate-receipts and water-
bank returns at the Durnan-Towns match. At different
places the Government road leads to the river, and any
effort to close this right-of-way will he vigorously com-
batted. Despite the booming in the Sydney daily pres 7 ,
the fixture isn’t likely 7 to be such a money-bag after all.
The sale of the right for “ publicans’ booths” was a dril
affair ; and, when Bung falls flat, it isn’t an encouraging
omen.

There is reason to suppose that, despite
the vigilance of the Customs officials, the yellow
population of Australia is frequently added to by
smuggling on the part of regular traders. The
first time that an E. and A. boat was searched at
Cairns, a few weeks ago, two Chows were dis-
covered in a small locker in the stern of the ship.
A cabin boy took the blame, after investigation,
and explained that to land them meant 300dols.
for him. The only punishment he will get, it was
stated, was the sack when the boat returns to Hong-
kong ! There seems to be a screw loose if a cabin
boy can have two stowaways on hand without a
responsible officer knowing something about the
matter. Or, perhaps, there was really no screw
loose at all. Three hundred dollars for the dis-
posal of two men at an Australian port is a
tempting offer.
Although two regular lines of steamers
belonging to Australian owners run to Thursday
Island, the military people recently despatched
14 soldiers for the local toy garrison by a vessel
flying, of all flags in the world, the Japanese
ensign, and named Something-Maru. The irony
of it!
Bishop Clarke, of Melbourne, says the in-
feriority of the Australian aborigines was due to
the fact that they never “got religion.” Dr.
Clarke is in error. They did get religion. In
fact, they died of it. An utterly foreign religion
forced upon a savage race is almost invariably
fatal. Our religion and our trousers killed the
Australian aborigines. The trousers were part of
the religion—its outward and visible expression,
so to speak. The blackfellow put his wild, free
legs into the flapping entanglements of a split
creed and faded away.

GRIEF WRECKED SYSTEM.
HARROWING TALE OF DESPAIR- LOSES MEMORY AT TIMES-
Clements Tonic
WORKS A WONDERFUL CHANGE.
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
So many go on suffering year by year through
lack of serviceable information, it is deemed ad-
visable to place before the public the story of Mrs.
Mary Janeßudd, of 38 Young-street, Red fern, so
that a salutary lesson may be learned. V\ hat
Mrs. Budd said to our special was this
“It is now eight years since my husband died,
a calamity that took place when we had been
living in this house nine years, so you see I have
been residing here a good while.”
“Yes,” assented the reporter. “ I believe you
are an old resident of these parts ? ”
44 j was born in Bourke-street, and have lived in
this locality all my life. But, to go back to my
story. I«t was not long after my great bereavement
that I became a total wreck. The blow nearly
killed me, and for a good while I was really
stupefied with grief. For some considerable time
my memory was completely lost, and it was im-
possible for me to concentrate my mind for a few
minutes upon any subject. When my dazed con-
dition passed away it was then that 1 realised how
terribly my whole system had suffered, a fact
which dawned upon me as I stood at the table one
day feeling so ill that I did not think I would live
till the morrow. For my own part, I did not care
what happened, but I thought of my three little
children, and said to myself : ‘What ever can I
take to do me good for their sakes ?
“Was that the first time you thought of taking
anything?” . , , ,
“ It was the first time I thought of acting on
my own judgment, the reason being that I was
under a doctor for some length of time, and his
treatment hadn’t benefited me in the least.”
“What did your medical attendant say was
wrong with you ? ”
“He told me I had taken my afflictions in-
wardly, and that my nervous system was in a very
weak state. I knew that well enough, unfortu-
nately, for I could not even bear to hear the
children talking, their voices got on mv nerves so,
and if a tap came to the door I nearly fainted with
fright. I hadn’t the least desire for food, and I
think one of my greatest afflictions was my in-
ability to get to sleep. For four years I did not
know what it was to sleep properly for one night.
It was usual for me to remain awake till about 3
or 4 o’clock in the morning, and then dose off till
half-past 5, when I always awoke with a start, and
would get no more sleep till next morning. And
the dreams I had each time I slept—they were
awful.” • i j ■
“You must have lost weight during your
illness ? * *
“ oh'! I was ghastly. My bones were almost
protruding through my skin, and anybody seeing
me could not have thought otherwise than that I
would never get better again. I really thought I
was doomed to die, and feel sure I would have
been dead long ago ONLY FOR CLEMENTS
TONIC, WHICH WAS THE LAST MEDI-
CINE OF ALL THAT I TOOK. A friend often
used to say, ‘ Why don’t you try some Clements
Tonic ?’ but I always postponed doing so ; but at
last I thought that as I had heard so much about
it, and nothing else could dome any good, I would
give Clements Tonic a trial. This was the decision
that I came to the day that I stood at the table
feeling that I could not last another twenty-four
hours, so what better reason than that could I have
for saying that Clements Tonic saved my life?
You must understand that I was quite incapable
of doing any work, and that it was a common thing
for me to be in bed a fortnight at a time, utterly
unable to attend to anything that required seeing
to, so when Clements Tonic began to put a little
life and strength into me you can imagine how
thankful I was for it. And weren’t the neighbors
surprised to hear that I had taken a turn for the
better ? My word, it was a matter that astonished
nearly the whole of Redfern. But what a relief
it was to have my headaches ease off after suffer-
ing so long with them ! And wasn’t I glad to feel
that Clements Tonic was also doing good to my
nervous system? Why, I had only been taking
that remedy a few weeks when I couid stand noises
of any kind without wincing, and already I was
getting more sleep than I had had for years.”
“Did you feel more refreshed after it than
formerly ?”
“Oh ! yes; much. The reason why, I suppose,
was that I did not dream at all, so both my brain
and body were resting all the time, and when I
awoke 1 felt so fresh and lighthearted—no nasty
taste in the mouth as before, a clean tongue, and,
my word, what an appetite fer breakfast I had !
Clements Tonic did make me eat and no mistake,
and my nerves were getting stronger and stronger
as the days went by. Eventually my old fears and
imaginations died right away, nothing more
troubling me, and all this wonderful change was
entirely due to Clements Tonic. Oh ! I have
thanked God thousands of times that I was guided
to take that remedy, and I am so anxious that
other sufferers may regain hope by hearing of my
cure. But Clements Tonic served me well in other
ways. It put an end to the giddy feelings I used
to have, removed the swollen feeling from my
eyes and the suffocating sensations from my chest,
then, finally, built up my strength so perfectly
that everyone could see my health was thoroughly
regained.”
“ May I publish your remarks ? ”
“I gladly give you permission to publish them
in any way.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I MARY JANE BUDD, of No. 38 Young-s reet, Redfern, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, consisting of five
folios and consecutively numbered from one to five, and that it contains and is a true and faithful account ©cjmy
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish in any way my statements—
which I Rive voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900. (Sgd.) MARY JANE BUDD.
Subscribed and declared at Redfern this twenty-seventh day of August, one thousand nine hundred and four,
before me, THOS. CADDY, J.F.

CATARRH is inflammation of the lining-
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose jo the head,.ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the brhath offensive, breaks down
tlie affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stotpach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

Eruptions Appeared on Chest, and
Face and Neck Were All Broken
Out—Scales and Crusts Formed
lowa Lady Has Great Faith
in Cuticura Remedies for Skin
Diseases.
ANOTHER WONDERFUL
CURE BY CUTICURA
“I had an eruption appear on my
chest and body and extend upwards
aiid downwards, so that my neck and
face were all broken out; also my arms
and the lower limbs as far as the knees.
I at first thought it was prickly heat.
But soon scales or crusts formed where
the breaking out was. , Instead of going
to a physician, I purchased a complete
treatment of the Cuticura Remedies, in
which I had great faith, and all was
satisfactory. A year or two later the
eruption appeared again, only a little
lower; but before' it nad time to spread
I procured another supply of the Cuti-
cura Remedies, and continued their use
until the cure was complete. It is now
five years since the last attack, and
have not seen any signs of a return. I
have taken about three bottles of the
Cuticura Resolvent, and do not know
how much of the Soap or Ointment, as
I always keep them with me.
“I decided to give the Cuticura Rem-
edies a trial after I had seen the results
of their treatment of eczema on an
infant belonging to one of our neigh-
bors. The parent took the child to the
nearest physician, but his treatment did
no good. So they procured the Cuticura
Remedies and cured her with them.
When they began using the Cuticura
Remedies her face was terribly disfigured
with sores, but she was entirely cured,
for I saw the same child at the age of
five years, and her mother told me the
eczema had never broken out since. I
have great faith in Cuticura Remedies
for skin diseases. Emma E. Wilson,
Liscomb, lowa, Oct. 1, 1905.”

BUCKLEY &NUNN, Ltd.,
MELBOURNE.
The Inky Way.
J. L. Kelly, who for eight years has discharged the
thankless task of editing the Times (Wellington, M.L.),
has made way for another, who is to try the impossible—
that is, to please everybody, and to hell ice over a
cold fire. The directorate of the Times is a fear-
some collection of irreconcilable elements —Liberalism
and Toryism, beer, ieetotalism, vegetarianism, and sun-
dries. If there was a cannibal on the directorate things
would be complete. All the directors nave a voice in
running the paper, and every man on it is haunted by
certain directors who want his biood, or, at least, his
dismissal. But they burn their fingers occasionally
through their mania for changing the staff.
a sub-editor, was awarded six months’ salary by-the
Supreme Court when he was suddenly dismissed'; and
with the general manager they got from the S.M.
Herald— -he is long since back at the Herald —they had
to make sundry arrangements, which meant paying out
money. The new morning paper to be soon established
in the windy capital will give more trouble to the
astonishing Times directorate.
* * m m
“E.”: A copy of No. 3 of Dramatic and Sporting
Chat, published at Hobart, is the first I have seen of that
unique newspaper. The' matter, mostly taken from
British sources, is very readable, although some weeks
behind the date. And how humorous is the publication
in design ! To get a first copy of Dramatic and Sporting
Chat one must place an “annual subscription” fee of 6s.
with one of the advertised agents. In the edition for.
’ eacH'State' the names'and addresses of local agents only
are included. Having paid your 6s. subscription, and
given your name and address, you are gravely informed,
by tbe .agent that subsequent issues of the rag can
be obtained gratis by all “subscribers” who call at the'
Hobart publishing office. Then you take your copy of
the latest issue (if you want it), and your receipt for the
six bob,.and in due course kind Mr. Tattersall- sends you
a ss. ticket for his next sweep, followed by a result sheet
of the drawing. Thus does Mr. Tattersall encourage the
demand for Chat. In return for his kindness you may,
if you like, again become a subscriber. And in that case
another ticket is forwarded to you., Dramatic and Sport-
ing Chat is a wonderful journal. There never was
another paper that got so enormous a, proportion of its
circulation paid for in advance, or so many repeated sub-
subscriptions from individual subscribers.

“ Q.”: In tjie same way that men deem all mortal .but
themselves.'so do they seeiri to think that'the past gene-
ration was always old., In the Vic. Manufacturers’ Exhi-
bition, artist Elamite Young shows a piotUre representing
the printing of Johnny Fawkner’s first Melbourne news-
paper, the Port Phillip Gazette. ! When 'this 'event
occurred, Fawkner was a blithesome youth, and the whole
population of the Port Phillip; settlement was young—it
probably averaged about 20 or 25 years of age. But Blamire
has constructed ; his picture , from authentic: documents
and sketches of the same individuals drawn many years
latey, with the result that he shows the P.P.G. being,
turned out by a body of sere and yellow journalists, who
would ha,ve taken two hours to catch the slowest item of
news that ever ran down a drain'.

One of the quaintest swan songs ever sung was that of
the Koqkvnif. Herald (W.A.), which, one day,last months
printed this ‘ announcement under “Deaths”: “At
Kookynie, on the 12th inst., in its second year, the
Herald, of tcedium vitae . Friends will please accept

“Hamer ” : There is something more than a bare possi-
bility of a new Sydney' daily, run in the Labor interest,
seeing the light during the present year. When such a
•paper is successfully launched, i<■ will mean a pretty Con-
siderable bump to the circulation of the Herald and
’Graph, for thousands of people who are compelled to
read those autocratic journals for their daily news would
jump at the rpportunity to change to a journal of cleaner
politics. Probably, too, it .would mean a considerable
change in the politics of the present papers, in the
struggle to placate the disgusted readers who want a
' change of literature. It is thedaily news,-however, that
is the difficulty, and the Herald and ’Graph, which
talk much about unions and the iniquity of interference
with free competition, wilL.fight as they.have fought
before to.exclude any new journal from their news syndi-
cate! But the finances of Labor are fairly good now, and
an effort of the sort could not be crushed as easily as it
was on a previous oocasion. A new paper might even
force some decency of argument upon its,older rivals. A
paper that would rise up the da£ after'the} publication of
a'report about sundry hatters, or wrecked .Chinese, or
shattered marriage ties, arid not only call the rag that
published it a picturesque liar, but prove it one, would
make the vendors of those lies sit up-considerably; also
it would furnish some excitement for. the-community.
Unfortunately, there is likely to be over-much vagueness
about- the fiseal pol-icy-of-a -Labor - daily';— Yefr the~Labor
party has of recent years been gaining sense, and,
at any rate, its journal would - not ory-out -for-Belgian
goods and the destruction of Australian industry quite
all the time.
ODE TO PETER MARK ROGET, WHO WROTE THE
“THESAURUS.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O, Peter Mark, I must, shall, or will say that your book, '
work, volume, tome, or opuscule is one of the most !
pleasing or entertaining that I keep in my house,,
shanty, or hovel; !
I’d rather or sooner read or peruse it any day, hour,
week, or month than the newest, latest, or most
recent narrative, story, or novel;
It gives me (myself, the undersigned) words, epithets, or
phrases to throw or hurl about when I’m as wild,
enraged, oiSangry as a clucking dove,
Or, on the other or opposite hand, helps or enables me to
properly or successfully guide or steer my pen when
I wish to be as mild as any sucking, dove ;
Prom its depths I can dredge or scoop charming or lovely |
specimens of trope, metaphor, catachresis, simile, or
whatever may tickle or please ye,
Which, for the making or manufacture of good or first-
class literature, needs only adequate setting (and that,
of course, is very simple or dead easy).
And if I wish for a group of prosy or platitudinous ex-
pressions to hang some highly respectable and conse-
quently half-dead metre or verse on,
So that I can follow or emulate the lowest or deepest
bathos of our old or ancient acquaintance or friend
the right or proper-thinking person,
I have no need to strain or rack my brain or thinking
apparatus until I acquire the not-altogether-desirable
look or expression of a thoughtful or pre-'oc'cupied
plesiosaurus—
No, not a bit or scrap of it! For I can simply march or
toddle to the part or section of my bookshelves
devoted' to R,, and lift or reach down “ Roget’s
Thesaurus,”
Which is why, among other or alternative causes or
reasons, I am moved to devoutly hope and trust that
in elysium or glory you sit, man,
Along with those other admirable and altogether delight-
some threaders or stringers of words, Rabelais, Burton
of the “ Anatomy,” and Walt Whitman,
With the ball of jest and mirth and good-fellowship
always or perpetually kept going (its course was
probably, or not impossibly, started or begun by Rab),
And the demijohn or decanter of nectar sometimes, or
not infrequently, set flowing (to it, I doubt not, or feel
fairly certain, justice would be done by Rab) ;
And some day I will certainly travel or journey to your
mansion or abode in those realms of joy or bliss to
express my admiration or esteem’s abundance,
And rushing passionately forward, I will fervently ejacu-
late, exclaim, or say—
“ Oh, Peter Mark,
Illumer of verbal dark,
Did your earthly or mundane conversation, talk, or chit-
chat never suffer from something like that faintly
indicated or outlined above, which may be described
as plethora, engorgement, or redundance?”
YIC.

SYDNEY
You meet it now in quite unexpected
places. For instance, in a recent address made
by president J. M. Joshua, of Melbourne Chamber
of Manufactures :
We must provide land for those who will come here
and settle. ... You will say there is no fun in paying £5
for land that we sold for £l. No, neither there is.
Neither will there be any fun in paying £2O for the same
thing ten years hence.... A Domesday Book, in which
present values can be recorded as a basis of future tran-
sactions, is necessary. The unearned increment farce
must cease.
Still, with a sufficient land-tax, there wouldn’t be
much need of a Domesday Book; though a
Domesday Book might be a convenient thing as a
basis for determining whether the land-tax was
high enough or not. If future land values got
ahead of Domesday Book values,, then it would be
good evidence that the tax needed hoisting.
“0.K.”: The aesthetic sense of fair “ Try-
phena ” is naturally offended at the recent
crop of Penny Ruskins in charity covers.
But in some previous popular editions of
the works of the great word painter there
is a cheapness which is not nasty. The
Shilling Ruskins, first issued by George Allen
ere his copyright had expired, are the nicest
of little books. The simple dignity of the “get
up ” seems to proclaim the character of the work,
even as the pedantry of literary cliquers is shown
in the fanciful bindings, expansive margins, and
odd decorative effects of their books of essays
which appeal only to readers of their own mental
type.
Premier Bent, the Promiser : “We have
been Goths and Vandals in the past, but now the
Minister of Lands is going to make a condition
that where a tree is cut down another must be
planted in its place.” Unless something like this
is done, Victoria ,will soon be within measurable
distance of haying no trees to cut down. But
Bent makes this sort of promise at the rate of
about two per day, and promptly forgets all
about them.

“ G-.” writes :
Speaking- at the annual luncheon of the A.N.A., Vic-
Minister of Agriculture Swinburne was daring enough to
chide public speakers for the vice of over-talking. Con-
sidering that the A.N.A. itself has been responsible for
the most horrible examples of long-distance oratory that
afflict Australia, the surprising thing is.that the members
did not arise as one man and pelt Swinburne to death
with cake and turkey. But their consciences evidently
smote them, for they said “Hear hear” in a chastened
way, as if they had never been guilty of such a thing.
What is badly wanted in Parliament is the Swiss
system of dividing the time of the session by the
number of members, and allotting to each his fair share.
The professional bore who h d only half an hour left,
when the session had three weeks to run, would exhibit
some masterly examples of condensed thought. The
adoptiou of the rostrum would be another improvement.
The BHtish practice of allowing a man to speak from his
seat in the House is probably responsible for more of the
loose-jointed, inconsequential flapdoodle that disfigures
Hansard than anything else. When a man has to mount
a platform and put forth his views, he takes the trouble
to prepare them.
“ Snell ” discourseth on drink, girls, &c. :
It’s an ill wind that blows nowhere, arid the much-
abused new Victorian licensing law has served a good
turn to cabmen and people with vehicles to hire. There
is nothing so provocative of tfiirst as the knowledge that
you can’t get a drink, and when Melbourne is doing a dry
perish in the waste places of the Seventh Day, it rushes
the cabs and traps, and drives beyond the 10 miles radius,
regardless of expense. Consequently, the spectacle of
large feet hanging out of cab windows in the Sabbath
moonlight is now quite common in and about Melbourne,
and, as the drunk is proverbially lavish to his cabman,
the Jehus are thinking-of making T. Bent their patron
saint.
Another person who has occasion to bless the law-
makers because of the new order is the barmaid. Bent’s
Act has put quite a new complexion on St. Kilda Esplan-
ade on Sunday nights. For a.time habitual frequenters
of the grand promenade, wondered at the great excess of
vivid damsels with peachy'cheek’s "and garments finer
than those of the numerous Mrs. Solomon when she bad
been shopping in Jerusalem! The new-comers are the
bright band, of barmaids liberated from Sunday graft in
the dim bar, and free to join their sisters Tn the human
race, which is developing into the mere brazen pursuit of
man by woman.
By the,same token, young men are much more freely
distributed about the public resorts than formerly, a fact
which maiketh glad the heart of woman.. Bent’s, enact-
ment hasi scared; the bachelors who fo’rtnbfly' lurked in
the rum religious light of the forbidden bar; they are
driven back upon the girls for consolation, and the girl is
much more in demand as-a.popular pastime than she was
wont to be. The law that treats alike'the publican and
the sinner has been most effective in breaking up Sunday
drinking bees. The appearance of an ingenious man who
well succeed in driving a brewer’s lorry through the Act
is eagerly anticipated, but meanwhile the girls are glad,
and a marriage boom is predicted.

George Fairbairn writes from Melbourne :
As I know your newspaper likes to be accurate, I would
like you to give me space to rectify an obvious error in
your last week’s issue. You make me say that the
British municipalities’ Socialistic works “ returned a
profit, after paying interest and providing sinking fund,
of only 6s. 3d. per cent.” What I did say was that the
profit returned on the £121,000,000 of investments made
by these municipalities was oidy 6s. 3d. per cent., without
paying any interest and without providing a sufficient
sinking fund. My figures were collected from Mr.
Schooling’s article in the Conthmporary Review, and
which have never been controverted. The question is, of
course, how the people’s business can be best done, and
if 6s. 3d. per cent, is to be the outcome of municipal
trading the sooner we get done with it the better.

/"\NE of the sights of London, and one you should not miss, is the magnificent six-story building just erected by STEWART DAWSON & CO.
for the better reception of the many visitors to London from Australia. It is miles ahead of any building anywhere devoted to the Jewellery
business. Fireproof throughout, it has superb Shipping Offices for the prompt despatch to our Australian and New Zealand Stores of all the new

will be cheerfully supplied, and Mr. Dawson will always be pleased to see any one personally if they so desire.

Mention The Bulletin.
“ Yawn ” holds forth with unexpected
firmness:—
When a certain type of Australian is let loose on a
holiday, he grabs what is affectionately termed his “ bit
of skirt,” and gyrates in a monotonous evolution. This
is “ darncirT.” Even barbarians, when they wish to ex-
press joy, or any form of elevated emotion, make use of
rhythm, which, in the healthy human animal, takes the
form of graceful movement—graceful because of a sense
of measured motion. Bowen, one-time Governor of
Victoria, when viewing a band of Fijian dancers, re-
marked : “ It’s nautchy, but it’s nice.” And so it
is with any form of dance that expresses an
emotion—“ it’s nice,” however “nautchy” it may be.
Take the peasantry of some European countries—
Italians, Spaniards, Hungarians, Poles, Russians—all of
whom have much less reason for expressing joy than the
Australian. They have distinctive dances which express
joyous feeling, and, especially in the Mediterranean
countries, the close relationship to the drama is a con-
spicuous feature. Some of their dances are strengthened
by expressive mime, and no physical effort is ever so
weak as to show a lack of healthy vigor. Dances closely
associated with religious rites belong to other countries.
There again the appreciation of graceful movement pre-
dominates.
These somewhat philosophical comments were pro-
voked by viewing a “ bloke ” with his “ bit of skirt ” on
New Year’s Day. To use the vernacular of the type, they
were “on their own.” Selecting a level patch of grass,
they embraced, and with mincing steps moved like re-
volving lay figures to the music of a mouth-organ. The
instrument was played over the girl’s shoulder by the
youth, and the absence of healthy movement and grace
was only equalled by the lack of a sense of humor, a
failing shared by nonchalant onlookers, who saw nothing
ludicrous in this lugubrious entertainment. It is such as
these who compete at suburban waltz contests, where
the judge, the better to see the feeble movement of the
high-heeled feet beneath the lady’s skirt, must lie down
on his (excuse me) stomach.
In affecting the genteel, to use a vulgar word appro-
priate to the subject, an inert and meaningless dance has
been developed. This type of Australian is peculiarly
sensitive to ridicule, which, combined with his lack of
humor, tends to check any effort in the direction of
graceful movement. Grace would at once be stigmatised
by his “ cobbers ” as “ actin’ the toff,” or what a higher,
but no less vulgar, stratum of society describes as
putting on dog.”
We have Highland fling's and Irish jigs, but these are
not of our soil. Even the Irish-Australian, when he
attempts the steps of his fathers, affects a set counte-
nance, out of keeping with the spirit of the dance. I
once attended a “grand ball in aid of the Jumberwallock
Football Club.” My unexpressed opinion was endorsed by
an old lady to whom I ventured the remark :
“ Fine to see the young people dancing, Mrs.
O Grady ! ” ' '
* l ® anc ' n ’j yez call ut? Sure they do be turkeying,
nothin’ but turkeying. Why, I moind the toime whin
on the ould sod, and even neow if ut wasn’t for me
rhematiz,” etc.

A CONVERSATION.
Q. I have been feeling very poorly lately, and have just been told by my doctor that I an 1
suffering from uric poisoning. I shall be glad if you will tell me just what uric poisoning means. lan
a serious matter ?
A. Uric poisoning is caused by the retention in the blood of various substances which should
leave the body in solution. The retention of these substances is due to a diseased or inactive condition
of the kidneys. When the kidneys are working perfectly, they filter and extract from the blood of the
average individual about three pints of urine every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved
various waste material produced by the wear and tear of the tissues of the body. This is dead matter,
and its presence in the blood is poisonous. The three pints of normal urine should contain about ten
grains in weight of uric acid, an ounce of urea, together with other animal and mineral matter varying
from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
Q. Then lam to understand that the substances you mention when not eliminated from the
bodyi'n the natural manner constitute what is known as uric poisoning. What are the usual symptoms
by which the presence of these poisons is manifest ?
A. Now you are asking rather a large question. Many complaints which are commonly called
diseases are not actually diseases in themselves. lor instance, Hlieumatism, Gout, Neuralgi* ,
Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel, Stone, and Bladder Troubles are all caused by uric poisons.
Indigestion, Ansemia. Persistent Headache, and General Debility are often solely due to
the same cause. In fact, if the kidneys are doing their work freely and thoroughly, none of the com-
plaints mentioned could trouble us as the causative poisons would be absent.
Q. I had no idea that so much depended upon the efficient action of the kidneys. I suppose that
when anyone is suffering from lidieumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, Anaemia, Debility, Persistent Headache, or Indigestion the
scientific method of effecting a cure would be to directly treat the patient for the kidneys ?
A. Exactly. In fact, that is the only way in which a radical and permanent cure can be effected.
The kidneys must be restored to health and activity, so that they may be enabled to remove the daily
production of poisons in the body, or the patient must continue to suffer.
O. I have always been under the impression that the liver had a great deal to do with the main-
tenance of our general health, but it seems that the kidneys are the chief cause of most of the disorders
from which we suffer.
A. The work done by the liver is of the utmost importance and it is closely associated with the
work done by the kidneys. Indeed, when anything is the matter with the liver the kidneys are almost
always directly affected, and the contrary is likewise true. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made every day. The liver takes sugar
from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able again to supply it to the
blood °radualiy, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
lived their life and are useful no longer.
O 4.s the functions of the kidneys and liver are so intimately related, I gather that if there is
reason to suspect that either organ is not doing its work efficiently a curative agent should be
employed which would act equally upon the kidneys and liver?
A Yes, that is the case, and it was the realisation of this important fact which led to the dis-
covery of that invaluable medicine Warner’s Safe Cure. About thirty years ago, certain medical
men knowing that if they could find a medicine which would beneficially affect alike the kidneys and
liver they could control most of the common disorders, devoted themselves to the search for such a
remedy. After many disappointments, their efforts were rewarded, and a medicine now known as
Warner’s Safe Cure was proved to possess the required properties in the fullest degree. Warner’s
Safe Cure has a marvellously stimulating and healing effect upon both the kidneys and liver, and by
restoring those vital organs to health and activity, it necessarily cures all disorders due to the retention
in the blood of urinary and biliary poisons, such as
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders
Ancemia
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Trouble
General Debility
Sick Headache
Even Bright's Disease, probably the most fatal of all diseases, yields to treatment by
Warner’s Safe Cure.
A great feature too, is that cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent, simply because
they are natural. (5J
The End of Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
bright Day gathered his lazy hosts
I When he saw the hordes of Night
Mustering fast on the eastern coasts,
To threaten each beacon light.
Black and fierce was the Night’s array,
While the Day’s gay army seemed
Naught but laughter and bright display
In harness of glass that gleamed.
The white Day spoke to his careless men,
And they gathered on either side ;
And there was a testing of sword blades then,
Till the keen steel sang and cried.
And dark and scowling the sullen Night
Came forward to meet his foe,
Never looking to left or right—
Sinister, evil and slow.
The brave Day laughed in the face of Night,
And smote with his sword swung high,
And every stroke was a beam of light
That dazzled the Night’s reply.
And slowly, driving the swarthy ranks
Ever to right and left,
The Day host marched in a firm phalanx
Down a path by their good swords cleft.
The strong Day sang as he led them on,
Though his blood the red path dyed.
Quoth the : “ March on till we reach the Sun,”
And they fought and bled at his side ;
For the swords of the Night found many vents
In their armor of brittle glass,
Yet the blades of the Day made recompense,
And" they had to let him pass.
Faint grew the Day and his weary hosts,
Fighting to reach the Dawn ;
And Night held sway on the silent coasts —
On river, and hill, and lawn.
One by one Day’s staunch braves died,
Till he marched West alone,
Dying, swaying from side to side,
’Mid foes who had hearts of stone.
Then burned a Star in the murky sky—
White as the Day it shone—
And the spent king lifted his crown on high,
Ancbhis sword with its brightness gone.
He spoke to the Star: “ The hosts of Night
Arb stronger than I; take these,
You will meet the Sun at the fringe of light
In the mists of the morning seas.
“ Say to him that another Day
Has sent him a sword and crown,
For the hosts of the darkness held the way,
And my wounds have borne me down.”
The brave Day died ; and fast and far
The Star through the darkness whirled ;
For it was the brilliant Morning Star,
That carries the light of the world.
A new Day called to his eager hosts
In glittering ranks up-drawn,
And marched away to the eastern coasts,
In the steps of his queen, the Dawn.
He carried the sword of the Day that died,
The soft clouds saw him pass ;
Going to death they watched him ride
In armor of brittle glass.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
