BENNO AT A BOXING BOUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
big building was crowded. Below,
in the ss. and 10s. reserves, was a flood of
human faces. In the balcony opposite, the
2s. push piled itself against the railing. The
atmosphere was one of blue smoke, and through
this the stout, bald, purple M.C. in the ring
shouted the names, weights, and colors of the
competitors in the preliminary tournament, his
voice like that of a very old crow.
“’Struth, his jibs wouldn’t earn peanuts ez
gripman on er mussel barrer,” said the youth
next Benno, sociably.
The clerk looked wise. “Tha’s right, Ned,”
he said ; “ but Dinny’s er good ernough spruicher.
Grivin’ chat t’ aujinces this size jiggers up ther
-’uman organ, I tell yeh.”
Feathers, on a seat behind, touched the lad on
the shoulder, and edged him away from Benno
with a sidelong motion of the head. “ Knows er
bit erbout elercution, he does,” he said confi-
dentially behind his hand, ‘ ‘ somethin’ iv er horater
’imself. Fam’iy’s in Parlymint.”
“ Gar-rn ? ” said the lad.
“ ’S truth,” Feathers assured him. “ Mother’s
got the contrac’ fer scrubbin’ ’n’ dustin’.”
“Get yer ear out, Ned, ’r he’ll bite it,”
said Benno sourly to the stranger, suspecting
mischief.
Benno had been to a couple of fights before this,
and felt entitled to be regarded as an authority.
Down in his heart he cherished an idea on the
strength of the experience thus acquired that,
should occasion arise, he could give an excellent
account of himself. He had never hit a man, but
it seemed easy, and he had magnificent dreams
of outing fourteen-stone foes with a right cross
which he had frequently tried on a bed bolster
with success. This visionary feat always took
place under the eyes of astonished and admiring
multitudes.
Two featherweights were scrambling in the
ring, exchanging wild swings, and the referee
was dodging about, looking for points in a
pointless exhibition. Benno watched closely,
making a little critical “tut-tut-tut ” now and
again. During the rests he instructed the lad
next him with a wise, old air. The lad chewed
gravely, and said little. Occasionally he jerked a
quaint grimace at an acquaintance on the off
side, but nothing in his attitude was intended to
disturb the clerk’s pleasing faith in himself. The
lad had a thick ear.
“O’Brien ’ll out ’im this round,” said Benno,
with calm confidence, when the gong sounded.
“He’ll get that crool right iv his ercross, ’n
Scorcher’ll go t’ bunk, you take it frim ther per-
fessor.”
“ Bin in ther gameyerself, Ned,” said the lad,
with a touch of mock reverence.
Benno lowered his voice. He had an uneasy
consciousness of the proximity of Feathers and
Nicholas Don, the driver. “ Oh, nothin’ t’ speak
of,” he said in the way of a man deliberately
hiding his light. “Matter ever scrap ’r two.”
“ Perfeshional ?” asked the lad.,
“No-o,” said Benno, “not yet.”
O’Brien aimed a blind swing at Scorcher ;
Scorcher took it on his glove, and prodded his
man a straight left flush. “Ha-ha,” cried
Benno, starting up excitedly. “ What’d I tell
you? O’Brien’ll out ’im: O’Brien’s got ’im
goin’ ! He’s got ’im goin’ ! Five t’ one O’Brien !
I bet five t’ one O’Brien !”
“ In cherry-bobs ! ” said a derisive voice. _
Before the clerk could frame an apt rejoinder,
the Scorcher planted a left on O’Brien’s mark,
and a right on his chin, and O’Brien went down.
Eleven seconds later he was carried to his corner,
as limp as a dead eel.
“Er quitter!” cried Benno, disgustedly.
“ Ther Scorcher never touched’im. Tha’s offen
ther way with clever sparrers, they’ve got science,
but no ’eart.” Then Benno went on to explain
to the lad with the thick ear how O’Brien must
have beaten the Scorcher to dough if he had kept
at him. The lad seemed quite grateful for this
polite attention. “If ’e’d on’y kep’ that left iv
’is goin,” said Benno, regretfully.
A youth in front rounded on the clerk with
vociferous disgust. “ Garn, get work !” he
said. “ O’Brien never had a possible. ’E ain’t got
a left ’and, and ’e ain’t got a right. ’E’s a gander.
’E don’t know enough to win a tombstone novice
tournament up in the ’Ome for Incurables.”
“ Oh,” said Benno, composedly, “p’raps I know
somethin’erbout ther game. It’s just possible.
Then again, o’ course, I may be little Georgie, the
gazob, ’n’ maybe I’m goat ernough t’ gnaw ther
posters off ther ’oardings if I ain’t watched.” He
looked about to note the effect of this preposterous
supposition, and then warmed up. “But, all
ther same, Ned, I’m bettin’ twenty t’ five in quids
O’Brien does Scorcher up inside seven rounds
with two-ounce gloves. Come, now ! ” ,
“G’ out,” snorted the other; “you ain’t
talkin’; you’re on’y makin’ a noise.”
Benno grew rash. Money was nothing to him.
“ I’ll make it er ’underd t’ twenty,” he said, “ ’n’
arrange ther match meself. Er ’underd t’ twenty
on Jimmy O’Gobs. Say what?” He even
plunged his hand in his trousers pocket, where
lurked 3s. 4d. and a bone stud. “Is it er bet?”
he asked, defiantly.
“ My name ain’t Chirnside,” growled the youth,
quite overwhelmed. “ I ain’t got twenty spits.”
“Then get ’em ’n’ pull ther string,” retorted
the clerk. He looked about him magnificently.
Several people were regarding him with marked
respect. Benno exulted. He felt that he was
being mistaken for a bookmaker. “ Listen t’ ther
oof bird twitter,” he said. “ How does it talk
’im down.” He slapped his pocket.
“ What do you think of Brophy’s chance in the
big fight, young gentleman?” asked the grey-
beard on Benno’s left, with proper diffidence.
Benno looked him over slowly, somewhat
superciliously, took out a cigar-case, and drew out a
cigar. It was a somewhat worn and ragged cigar,
and Benno, who was no smoker, had once taken
it for a threepenny shout. It came in handy now.
He lit it like a millionaire. Then he condescended
to speak. “If yer wantin’ t’ pick up good money
dirt cheap, Whiskers,” he said, “ back Brophy.”
“Fer a win er a place?” asked the derisive
voice.
Young Mr. Dickson felt he could afford to
ignore the interjection. “Pi.t yer plate on
Brophy,” he repeated, “ his chances ’re all right,
He’s got ther quids ez good ez in ’is ’and. Hark
to the prophet. I admit ther big feller’s strong
’n’ game, but what’s ’e done? ” He looked hard
at the old gentleman, toying daintily with his
ragged cigar the while, and repeated, “ What’s ’e
done ?”
“Yes, yes; that’s the point,” said the grey-
beard.
“Whereas, look at Brophy,” continued Benno
tor the benefit of the company ; “ he’s bin through
’em all. He’s er bit small fer this, but he’s got
the science. He’s used t’ handlin’ big blokes,
too, ’n’ you jist watch out ’n’ see him quilt Mr.
Rocker “Dodd from gong t’ beddy-bye. Dodd
won’t ’it ’im, he’s too shifty. He’ll just pelt ’em
inter Rocker’s biscuit barrel, ’n' slide out every
time ther’eavyweight comes t’ pass’im one.”
“Ever seen either of ’em fight?” asked the
unbeliever in front.
For a moment Benno was taken back. As a
matter of fact, he had not. He rallied quickly.
“ Have I seen ’em ! Have I what ?” he retorted.
“ Brophy’s ez quick ez dam it, ’n’ gets erbout er
bit, I’m tellin’ yeh.”
“I ain’t seen Brophy,” growled the other;
“but this Dodd can shift. He ain’t as slow as a
house.”
“You’re cornin’ on all right, Ned,” said Benno
soothingly. “When you’re growed up you’ll
know er bit, I promise yeh, but jist now I
wouldn’t open me head too wide erbout boxin’ iv
I ifras you.”
Benno was very excitable and very demonstra-
tive during the other preliminary bouts. He
brandished a note-book and talked in hundreds
with the glibness of a King of the Ring. He even
shouted directions to the combatants, using their
Christian names familiarly. Feathers and the
Don, sitting behind, were enjoying themselves
immensely. They refrained from interfering for
fear of spoiling “our Mr. Dickson’s” flow of
skite, and the clerk, in his great exultation, had
forgotten their existence.
But'it was when the fight of the night came on
that Benno excelled himself. While the men
were in their corners his enthusiasm in the cause
of Brophy manifested itself in a reckless offering
of the odds to all and sundry. He stood on his
chair gesticulating over the heads of the standing
crowd, till those behind howled him down. His
cheap, dry cigar burst into flame at this point,
but that' did not disconcert him. Threats of
personal violence he treated with a cold, scornful
smile. He hoped he was looking as if he could
fight a bit. In his calmer moments he invited the
lad with the thick ear to call on him at his hotel—
Benno lived in a humble five-roomed cottage with
his ma and his sister Amelia—and he would teach
him a few hits that might be useful to him in his
dealings with a cruel world.
In the first round Mike Brophy showed himself
a flash fighter, and very fanciful in his movements.
He had a fine figure and feline grace, and was
anxious to display himself in the limelight, and,
while he was pouting his chest and striking atti-
tudes for the benefit of the artistic minority,
Rocker Dodd, who was a hard-headed, lumbering
pug with no eye for the beautiful, swung in a
punch that lifted the smaller man up off his two
feet, and dropped him like a bag of scraps.
“On’y er slip!” cried Benno. “He’s up agin
all right.” And so he was, but looking like a
man full of warm whisky suddenly ejected into
the cold night. Rocker sprang at him again, and
Mike ducked by instinct into safety under his
enemy’s wing. ‘‘ What’d I tell yeh ? ” squealed
the clerk. “ See that? What’d I tell yeh ? Ain’t
’e er beaut’ ? Rocker can’t ’it ’im. He can’t ’it
’im, that’s what.” Rocker shook his man off,
and punched with right and left, putting a lop-
sided head on Brophy, and then the gong
sounded, and a thankful fighter sank into Mike’s
corner.
“Ain’t ’e er beaut’?” vociferated Benno.
“Good man, Brophy! It’s twenty t’ one on
yeh ! Twenty t’ one Brophy ! ” cried the clerk
raising his voice. “ Twenty t’ one Brophy ! ”
A red-faced, pimply man bobbed up out of the
crowd, and pinned Benno with a fat forefinger.
“ Done ! ” he said, “ I’ll take that, Ned.”
“Twenty t’one in quids erbout Brophy!” re-
peated Benno, flourishing his pocket-book.
“Same fer me, mister,” said a sport with a
broken nose, tugging at Benno’s coat.
Several others were anxious to do business with
Mr. Dickson. “I’ll have er dollar’s worth in
that,” said the lad with the thick ear. “ Referee’s
decision.”
Benno was too elated to heed them. He was
shouting expert advice to Brophy’s corner. “Give
’im ther tow’l! ” he cried, disgustedly. “He
don’t want no sermons frim you. Wag ther
washin’, blarst yeh ! ” He turned to the lad with
the thick ear. “Wish I was in his corner,”
he said.
“ Bitter bad mozzle fer Brophy yeh ain’t,” said
the other.
In the second round Brophy was more anxious
to keep out of trouble than to show his elegant
shape, and he sprinted about the ring with Rocker
lumbering after him, punching anyhow and any-
where. Twice Mike was down, thinking, but
that did not damp Benno’s ardor. The clerk
was now offering 30 to 1 Brophy with splendid
prodigality, and there were many takers whom he
didn't even notice. “He’ll let ther big bloke
wear ’imself out, ’n’ then he’ll dish ’im up on ther
arf shell with er taste iv lemon,” said “our Mr.
Dickson.” Brophy was bleeding from a cut over
the eye, and had a numbed nose and a split lip,
and Rocker Dodd was wishingthe fight would be-
gin in earnest.
During the next interval Benno was more con-
fident than ever. He said it was picking up
money backing Brophy. He casually offered to
lay two hundred to ten about it. He addressed
Brophy like an old and valued friend, advising
him to keep cool and fight his own hand.
Mike tried to make the third round a foot-race,
but Mr. Dodd was there, and wouldn’t hear of it.
He blocked Brophy in the corners, and punched
him, and his punches liad all the nervous energy
that is in the heels of an exuberant young pack
mule. When they reached Mike, Mike’s feet
sprang up, and he struck the boards with the back
of his head. For the fifth time in two minutes
Mike was down, and now his better judgment
prevailed, and he pretended to be asleep in order
to evade the responsibility of getting up again.
The seconds called him early, the ten-seconds-
check reminded him it was time to be up and
doing, but Mike slept on. It was all over. The
referee, in a few terse, epigrammatic words, de-
clared Rocker Dodd the winner.
“Yah-a-h, er schlinter!” cried Benno. “Er
schlinter ! Er schlinter ! ”
The red-faced man was working his way towards
Benno through the press, pinning the clerk with
that imperious forefinger. He took Benno by a
button. “ That’s a matter o’ twenty jim you’re
owin’ me, Ned,” he said.
“ Ah, scratch!” retorted the clerk. I hat
wasn’t no bet.” ~, , ,
The sport with the perverted nose and the lad
with the thick ear were clamoring for an imme-
diate settlement of their claims. Several others,
thinking the blutf might bo worth it, put in large
demands. The red-faced man was furious and
threatening. He recited terrible things that
would happen to “our Mr. Dickson if he did
not instantly hand out twenty pounds, lhe pam
ful nature of the situation flashed upon Benno.
He had been taken too seriously. He went chalk
white, his legs wobbled foolishly. ,
“Bli’me, ’twas on’y er joke, he protested.
“ I was list talkin’. I ain’t got erbean.
“A blessed welsher!” yelled the red-faced
man. “An infernal gun ! ” cried the sport.
Twenty hands fell upon Benno. He went
down into dark night that was full of arms and
feet and broken chairs, and his only sensation was
of being a mere scrap of himself whirling in the
blackness, in which the arms, the chairs, and the
feet continually multiplied themselves. lhe
packer and Nicholas Don, ably assisted by six
policemen, effected a rescue, and what they
rescued was a weary fragment. Very reluctant
were the police to give Feathers charge of it,
for it was ragged, dusty, blood-stained, hat-
less and battered, and would have been a striking
object lesson in the dock next morning. How-
ever, the packer’s eloquence prevailed, and he
and Nick took the weary fragment away with
them, wrapped in Nick’s overcoat. Without that
coat it would not have been possible to introduce
the clerk into mixed company on a tram, what
remained of his clothes being hardly sufficient to
establish identification.
“Well, you’re ther king cop : out,” said the
packer, having delivered Benno into his sister s
hands at his own door. “If yeh was t’ go t
church ther boiler ’ud bust.”
Benno answered never a word. His splendid
spirit was broken. „

II-Gotten Gold.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rush had set in three months be-
§ fore, and the old silence of the bush was
broken by creaking windlasses, and torn
by the babble of the tongues of many nations.
The held was counted rich, even at a time when
rich gold finds were common in Australia, A
third of the diggers were making fortunes. 01
the rest, very few were not on payable gold. The
long lines of white tents that stood out in bold
relief against the dark foliage on the hill-side
were constantly being added to, while the calico
stores and grog shops of a month ago were
already being replaced by more pretentious struc-
tures.
Old Moss Isaacs had the most substantial build-
ing on the field. Its sides of heavy ironbark
slabs, with a strongly locked and barred door of
the same timber, seemed to defy unwarrantable
entrance. Unlike the publicans and the other
storekeepers, Isaacs would not buy gold from the
diggers, but for a consideration of 2s. an ounce
he offered his store as a safe depository in
which they might put the metal until the
escort took it away. The diggers soon found
that this was more profitable than selling
locally, and Isaacs found that he got twice
as much gold deposited as he could have hoped to
buy. Besides it brought custom to the store.
Business was flourishing one afternoon, and the
excited nose of Isaacs gleamed like burnished
copper, when Morgan drifted in.
‘‘How’s your mate to-night ?” asked Isaacs.
Very low,” was the reply. “Had the new
doctor up to see him, and he said he can’t live
more than a couple of days—if he lives that
long.”
Morgan bought something for his sick mate,
and drifted out again. He was a young man, but
he always seemed to drift.
They haven’t had much luck since they came
here, said Isaacs, with a flow of sympathy.
“They’ve got a bit of gold deposited here, but
not a quarter as much as they might have had, but
for Steele getting sick. He’s not done nothing the
last three weeks. He was always as pale as a
ghost before he took to his bunk. Now that a
doctor has come he might pull,through, but he
doesn’t look like it.”
He didn’t pull through. Next evening, before
sunset, the word passed from claim to claim that
Morgan’s mate was dead. The doctor was with
him to the last, but could do nothing, he said.
A coffin was made out of slabs, and Morgan and
the doctor laid the body in it. Various offers of
assistance were declined. The general impres-
sion was that the new medico was doing his work
like a man and a brother.
Next morning at sunrise the coffin was carried
to the burial place. A couple of hundred men
followed it, and stood reverently round the grave
with uncovered heads. The doctor read the
burial service, the grave was tilled in, and Morgan
carved the name, “ Con Steele, ” on a tree at its
head.
It was just then that the crowd recognised a
vast commotion at the camp, and the centre of
the rush and bustle was Moss Isaacs’ store. And
in five minutes the air was full of the news that
Isaacs had been found gagged and bound, that
the store had been looted, and that the whole of
the deposited gold was gone. Isaacs described
how two men knocked at his door as he was going
to bed. On opening it, they seized him, took his
keys, and he tore his beard and left the rest
of the story to the imagination. The diggers
raged, and search parties scoured the country,
and in the slow progress of time the police hap-
pened on the scene, but all without finding the
least trace of the marauders or the gold.
Old Isaacs was a square man. He offered to
sell the contents of the store and divide the pro-
ceeds among those who were losers through the
robbery. Some of the diggers refused to hear of
this, but a few insisted on the sale. The stock
didn’t realise much—it was a much smaller stock
than it looked—and a week afterwards the old
storekeeper pathetically carried his swag away
from the field, a broken Hebrew.
Three months passed after Isaacs faded over the
local horizon, and the robbery was almost forgotten
except by those who were victims. Then the
doctor signified his intention of returning to
England, as his health was giving way.
After receiving a presentation from the miners,
he also left the field.
It was the very morning after his departure
that the diggers were throw'n into a new excite-
ment. Con Steele’s grave had been disturbed the
night before. In a few minutes the news spread
from end to end of the lead, and in half-an-hour
hundreds had gathered round the grave, which
looked as if it had been opened and refilled the
previous night. Some were already shovellingout
the fresh earth by way of investigation, while
those around were talking excitedly. One man
remarked it was strange that the grave should be
disturbed the night the doctor left. The hint
was eagerly grasped by the crowd, and vengeance
was vowed against the departed medico for his
supposed crime of desecrating the digger’s grave
in order to obtain a skeleton. Local ideas about
skeletons were vague but bitter. The shovels
rattled on the coffin, and it was hauled to the
surface.
“The body isn’t gone, after all,” said one of
the men who lifted the burden. “It’s too heavv
for that.” J
The lid was unscrewed, and, as it was lifted
the miners crowded round. Then they fell back
aghast. Not a man could speak. In the coffin
before them, with glassy eyes staring at the sky
was the body of the doctor.
At the same hour two horsemen were riding
through the mountains, 40 miles from the mining
camp. There they found a man waiting for them
with three horses. If such a thing as a Hebrew
bushman were possible this third man might have
passed for that individual.
“You’ve got herein good time,” said the bush
Jew. “ But where’s Dick ? ”
Neither replied. They were changing their
saddles on to the fresh horses.
“ Where’s Dick ? Has anything gone wrong
with the affair ?” he repeated.
“Better ask Con about him,” said Morgan.
“No; everything was all right. The gold is in
the pack on Dick’s horse.”
“I’ll tell you where lie is,” said Con, with an
uncanny laugh. “ He’s in my grave. When we
opened the coffin last night and took the gold out,
he said, ‘ O’ course you understand that I get half
of this, and the other half is to be divided among
the three o’ you.’ We said we understood nothin’
o’ th’ kind. Then he said he made that arrange-
ment with you. Besides, he reckoned that he was
th’ main man in the business, because he planned
everything, so he ought t’ get more’n us. Of
course, he played his part well enough ; but what
about me actin’ the corpse? Jist think o’being
screwed down in that coffin for a couple o’ hours !
It was horrible ; it seemed years ”■ '
“Well, don’t whine about it,” said the man
who wanted information. “Think of me, lying
half tiie High t with a gag in my mouth, and the
rope you fellows tied me with cutting into my
wrists and ankles ! I thought I’d die before any-
one came to the rescue. But, go on, Con.”
“ When we told Dick he could on’y get a fourth
share, the same as us, he got in a rage, an’ said
he’d take his half there an’ then, an’ go his own
road. He started sortin’ out the bags, an’, some-
how, I hit him with th’ spade : an’ we left him
there.”
“Now, then, Isaacs—let’s make a start,” said
Morgan.
And Cornelius the corpse, and Morgan his
partner, and the bush Jew headed on through the
ranges.
PAUL CUPID.
World-Chiming.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FAINTHEART heard the sighing
chimes !
Hear our chimes !
Change the times or suit the times !
Think'st thou to uproot the times t
Hear our chiming,
Suit the times !
Faintheart hearkened to the chimes,
Suit the times !
So he labored, and fulfilled
All the chiming world-song willed.
So he lived until he died,
Faint heart, small heart, satisfied.
Where he resteth no man knoweth,
Listen ! how the waste wind bloweth,
Mocking, mocking ! Toll, toll !
This echo of his puny soul.
Hear our chimes !
Suit the times or change the times I
Suit the times and they will suit thee,
Fight the times, they will refute thee,
Suit the times 1
Follow fast where range the times,
Climb thou where their temper climbs !
Hear our chiming
At thy timing
Thine own chiming to the times !
Hear our chimes,
Suit the times 1
Swiftheart heard the ringing chimes,
Hear our chimes!
Suit the times or change the times,
Soar thou, and outrange the times !
Hear our chiming,
Change the times !
Swiftheart hearkened to the chimes,
Change the times 1
So he rose, without a sword,
Hurling forth the naked word,
Calling, calling, till he died,
Rash heart, lone heart, crucified.
Where he resteth no man knoweth.
Listen ! how the wild wind bloweth,
Mocking, mocking ! Toll, toll !
This one echo of his soul.
Hear our chimes !
Suit the limes, or change the times !
Grasp occasion ere its blooming,
Hurl thy rashness on its dooming.
Change the times!
Heed not fashion, leave occasion.
Climb thou where thy fancy climbs !
Hear our pealing
At thy sealing
The unripeness of thy soul!
Hear our toll,
Change the times !
Greatheart heard the rolling chimes,
Hear our chimes !
Suit the times, and change the times !
Mark the times, and nurse thy power
While it ripens, hour on hour,
Fine in temper, keen in blade,
Purging forth the dross it had ;
Till thine arm is brawn and bone —
Now thou art a man full-grown !
Flash ! and smite with thine own sword,
Forcing home the hammered word!
Hear the chiming
Of the times !
Greatheart hearkened to the chimes ;
Bide thy times !
Then he rose, and swift outpoured
Shining glory from his sword,
So that each man liearkeneth
To the word of life and death.
Where he resteth each man knoweth,
Heed thou what the wise wind bloweth,
Pealing, pealing ! Toll, toll !
Full ripe echoes of his soul.
Hear our chimes !
Suit the times, to change the times !
Suit the times until they suit thee ;
Soar too swiftly, they'll refute thee ;
Bide thy times !
Follow fast, but Work unending
At thy making, at thy mending ;
Steel thy soul, and wall thy path
With the stones that custom hath ;
Turn the force that else will end thee
To a weapon to defend thee,
Till thy wings out-range the times.
Hear them chiming
At thy priming !
Not by swiftness, but long labor
Hast thou fashioned forth thy sabre;
Hear men hail thee, change the times /
The whole world chimes —
Changed the times!
N.S.W.
